en|leaders and at least a dozen items to the subject; and a poster, in the
en|largest type and reddest ink which the “Register” office could supply, confronted one at every
en|fork and crossing of roads leading to and from Barton, and informed every passer-by that
en|Major Ben Bailey, the well-known champion of the temperance cause, would address the meeting, that
en|the “Crystal Spring Glee Club” would sing a number of stirring songs, and that the
en|Barton Brass Band had also been secured for the evening. The only inducement which might
en|have been lacking was found at the foot of the poster, in the two words,
en|“Admittance Free.” No wonder the villagers crowded to the Methodist Church, the most commodious gathering-place
en|in the town. Long before the bells had ceased clanging the church was so full
en|that children occupying full seats were accommodatingly taken on the laps of their parents, larger
en|children were lifted to the window-sills, deaf people were removed from the pews to the
en|altar steps, and chairs were brought from the various residences and placed in the aisles.
en|Outside the church, crowds stood about near the windows, while more prudent persons made seats
en|of logs from the woodpile which the country members of the congregation had already commenced
en|to form against the approaching winter. A sudden hush of the whispering multitude ushered in
en|the clergy of Barton, and, for once, the four reverend gentlemen really seemed desirous of
en|uniting against a common enemy instead of indulging in their customary quadrangular duel. Then, amid
en|a general clapping of hands, the members of the Crystal Spring Glee Club filed in
en|and took reserved seats at the right of the altar; while the Barton Brass Band,
en|announced by a general shriek of “Oh!” from all the children present, seated themselves on
en|a raised platform on the left. Squire Tomple, the richest and fattest citizen of the
en|town, was elected chairman, and accepted with a benignant smile. Then the Reverend Timotheus Brown,
en|the oldest pastor in the village, prayed earnestly that intemperance might cease to reign. Squire
en|Tomple then called on the band for some instrumental music, which was promptly given and
en|loudly applauded, after which the Crystal Spring Glee Club sang a song with a rousing
en|chorus. Then there was a touching dialogue between a pretended drunkard and his mother, in
en|which the graceless youth was brought to a knowledge of the error of his ways,
en|and moved to make a very full and grammatical confession. Then the band played another
en|air, and the Glee Club sang “Don’t you go, Tommy,” and there was a tableau
en|entitled “The First Glass,” and another of “The Drunkard’s Home,” after which the band played
en|still another air. Then a member of the Executive Committee stepped on tiptoe up to
en|the chairman and whispered to him, and the chairman assumed an air of dignified surprise,
en|edged expectantly to one side of his chair, and finally arose suddenly as another member
en|of the Executive Committee entered the rear door arm-in-arm with the great Major Ben Bailey
en|himself. The committee-man introduced the Major to the chairman, who in turn made the Major
en|acquainted with the reverend clergy; the audience indulged in a number of critical and approving
en|glances and whispers, and then the chair announced that the speaker of the evening would
en|now instruct and entertain those there present. The speaker of the evening cleared his throat,
en|took a swallow of water, threw his head back, thrust one hand beneath his coat-tails,
en|and opened his discourse. He was certainly a very able speaker. He explained in a
en|few words the nature of alcohol, and what were its unvarying effects upon the human
en|system; proved to the satisfaction and horror of the audience, from reports of analyses and
en|from liquor-dealers’ handbooks, that most liquors were adulterated, and with impure and dangerous materials; explained
en|how the use of beer and light wines created a taste for stronger liquors; showed
en|the fallacy of the idea that liquor was in any sense nutritious; told a number
en|of amusing stories about men who had been drunk; displayed figures showing how many pounds
en|of bread and meat might be bought with the money spent in the United States
en|for liquor, how many comfortable homes the same money would build, how many suits of
en|clothing it would pay for, how many churches it would erect, and how soon it
en|would pay the National Debt (which in those days was foolishly considered large enough to
en|be talked about). Then, after drawing a touching picture of the drunkard’s home, and dramatically
en|describing the horrors of the drunkard’s death, the gallant Major made an eloquent appeal to
en|all present to forsake forever the poisonous bowl, and dropped into his seat amid a
en|perfect thunder of applause. The lecture had been a powerful one; it was evident that
en|the speaker had formed a deep impression on the minds of his hearers, for when
en|the pledge was circulated, men and women who never drank snatched it eagerly and appended
en|their names, some parents even putting pencils into baby fingers, and with devout pride helping
en|the little ones to trace their names. Nor were the faithful alone in earnestness, for
en|a loud shout of “Bless the Lord!” from Father Baguss, who was circulating one of
en|the pledges, attracted attention to the fact that the document was being signed by George
en|Doughty, Squire Tomple’s own book-keeper, one of the most promising young men in Barton, except
en|that he occasionally drank. Then the list of names taken in the gallery was read,
en|and it was ascertained that Tom Adams, who drove the brick-yard wagon, and whose sprees
en|were mighty in length and magnitude, had also signed. Half a dozen men hurried into
en|the gallery to congratulate Tom Adams, and so excited that gentleman that he took a
en|pledge and a pencil, went into the crowd outside the church, and soon returned with
en|the names of some of the heaviest drinkers in town. The excitement increased. Cool-headed men--men
en|who rarely or never drank, yet disapproved of binding pledges--gave in their names almost before
en|they knew it. Elder Hobbedowker moved a temporary suspension of the circulation of the pledges
en|until the Lord could be devoutly thanked for this manifestation of his grace; then the
en|good elder assumed that his motion had been put and carried, and he immediately made
en|an earnest prayer. During the progress of the prayer the leader of the band--perhaps irreverently,
en|but acting under the general excitement--brought his men to attention, and the elder’s “Amen” was
en|drowned in the opening crash of a triumphal march. Then the Glee Club sang “Down
en|with Rum,” but were brought to a sudden stop by the chairman, who excused himself
en|by making the important announcement that their fellow-citizen, Mr. Crupp, who had been a large
en|vender of intoxicating beverages, had declared his intention to abandon the business forever. The four
en|pastors shook hands enthusiastically with each other; while, in response to deafening cheers, the heroic
en|Crupp himself was thrust upon the platform, where, with a trembling voice and a pale
en|though determined face, he reaffirmed his decision. Old Parson Fish hobbled to the front of
en|the pulpit, straightened his bent back until his mien had at once some of the
en|lamb and the lion about it, and, raising his right hand authoritatively, started the doxology,
en|“Praise God from whom all blessings flow,” in which he was devoutly and uproariously joined
en|by the whole assemblage. This done, the people, by force of habit, waited a moment
en|as if expecting the benediction; then remembering it was not Sunday, they broke into a
en|general and very enthusiastic chat, which ceased only when the sexton, who was a creature
en|of regular habits, announced from the pulpit that the oil in the lamps would last
en|only a few minutes longer, and that _he_ had promised to be at home by
en|ten o’clock. Squire Tomple took the arm of the penitent Crupp and appropriated him in
en|full. There was a great deal to Squire Tomple besides avoirdupois, and when thoroughly aroused,
en|his enthusiasm was of a magnitude consistent with his size. Besides, Squire Tomple was in
en|the habit of having his own way, as became the richest man in Barton, and
en|he appropriated Mr. Crupp as a matter of course. With Mr. Crupp on his arm
en|and the great cause in his heart, he appeared to himself so fully the master
en|of the situation that the foul fiend of drunkenness seemed conquered forever, and the Squire
en|swung his cane with a triumphal violence which seriously threatened the safety of the villagers
en|in front of and behind him. The Squire held his peace while surrounded by the
en|home-going crowd, as rightly became a great man; but when he had turned into the
en|street in which Mr. Crupp lived, he said, with due condescension, “Crupp, you’ve done the
en|right thing; you _might_ have done it sooner, but you can do a great deal
en|of good yet.” The ex-rumseller quietly replied, “Yes, if I’m helped at it.” “Helped? Of
en|course you’ll be helped, if you pray for it. You’ve repented; now address the throne
en|of grace, and----” “Yes, I know,” interrupted Mr. Crupp. “I’m not entirely unacquainted with the
en|Lord, if I _have_ sold rum. You know his sun shines on the just and
en|the unjust, and I’ve had a good share of it. It’s help from men that
en|I want, and am afraid that I can’t get it.” “Why, Crupp,” remonstrated the Squire,
en|“you must have made something out of your business, if it _is_ an infernal one.”
en|“I don’t mean that,” replied Mr. Crupp, a little tartly. “You’ve been on your little
en|drunks when you were young, of course?” The Squire almost twitched Mr. Crupp off the
en|sidewalk, as he exclaimed, with righteous indignation, “I never was drunk in my life.” “Oh!”
en|said the convert. “Well, some have, and pledges won’t quiet an uneasy stomach, no way
en|you can fix ’em. Them that never drank are all right, but the drinking boys
en|that signed to-night’ll be awful thirsty in the morning.” “Well,” said the Squire, “_they_ must
en|pray, and act like men.” “Some of ’em don’t believe in prayin’, and some of
en|’em can’t act like men, because ’tisn’t in ’em. There’s men that seem to need
en|whisky as much as they need bread; leastways, they don’t seem able to do without
en|it.” “If I’d been you, and believed that, Crupp,” replied the Squire, with noticeable coolness
en|and deliberation, “I wouldn’t have signed the pledge; that is, I wouldn’t have stopped selling
en|liquor.” “P’r’aps not,” returned the ex-rumseller; “but with me it’s different. There’s some men that
en|b’lieves that sellin’ a woman a paper of pins, and measurin’ out a quart of
en|tar for a farmer, is small business, an’ beneath ’em, but they stick to it.
en|Now I believe I’m too much of a man to sell whisky, so I’ve stopped.”
en|The Squire took the rebuke in silence; however much his face may have flushed, there
en|were in Barton no tell-tale gas-lamps to make his discomfort visible. The Squire had grown
en|rich as a vender of the thousand little things sold in country stores; he had
en|many a time declared that storekeeping was a dog’s life, and that he, Squire Tomple,
en|was everybody’s nigger--but he made no attempt to change his business. “What I mean,” continued
en|Mr. Crupp, “by needin’ help, is this: I know just about how much every drinkin’
en|man in town takes, an’ when he takes it, an’ about when he gets on
en|his sprees. Now, if there’s anybody to take an interest in these fellows at such
en|times, they’re going to have plenty of chances mighty soon.” CHAPTER II. BUSINESS _vs._ PHILANTHROPY.
en|On the morning after the meeting the happiest man in all Barton was the Reverend
en|Jonas Wedgewell. He had been one of the first to agitate the subject of a
en|grand temperance demonstration; in fact, he had, while preaching the funeral sermon of a young
en|man who had been drowned while drunk, prophesied that the sad event which had on
en|that occasion drawn his hearers together would give a mighty impetus to the temperance movement;
en|then like a sensible, matter-of-fact prophet, he exerted himself to the uttermost that his prophecy
en|might be fulfilled. He subscribed liberally to the fund which paid for advertising the meeting;
en|he labored personally a full hour with the performer on the big drum, and ended
en|by persuading him to forego a coon-hunt on that particular night, that he might take
en|part in a hunt for nobler game. The Reverend Jonas had drafted all the pledges
en|which were circulated during the meeting, and had seen to it that they contained no
en|weak or ungrammatic expressions which might tempt thirsty souls to treat disrespectfully the documents and
en|the principles they embodied. He had reached the church door at the third tap of
en|the bell, had greeted all his reverend brethren with a hearty shake with both his
en|own hands, and had offered the Reverend Timotheus Brown so many pertinent suggestions as to
en|the prayer which that gentleman had been requested to make that the ancient divine remarked,
en|with a touch of saintly sarcasm, that he did not consider that the occasion justified
en|him in making a departure from his habit of offering strictly original prayers. Through the
en|whole course of the meeting good Pastor Wedgewell sat expectantly on the extreme end of
en|the pulpit sofa, his body inclined a little forward, his hands upon his knees, his
en|eyes gleaming brightly through polished glasses, and his whole pose suggesting the most intense earnestness.
en|He discerned a telling point before its verbal expression was fully completed, his hands commenced
en|to applaud the moment the point was announced; his varnished boots and well-stored head beat
en|time alike to “Lily Dale,” the march from “Norma,” “Sweet Spirit, hear my prayer,” and
en|such other airs as the band was not ashamed to play in public; he sprang
en|from his seat and approvingly patted the youthful backs of the pretended drunkard and his
en|mother, he laughed almost hysterically at the wit of the lecturer, and moistened handkerchief after
en|handkerchief as the able speaker depicted the sad results of drunkenness. While the pledges were
en|being circulated, the reverend man occupied a position which raked the house, and he was
en|the first to announce to the faithful in the front seats the capture of any
en|drinking man. He intercepted Tom Lyker, a tin-shop apprentice, who had signed the pledge, in
en|the aisle, immediately after the audience was dismissed, and suggested that they should together hold
en|a season of prayer in the study attached to the church; and the rather curt
en|manner in which the repentant but not altogether regenerate Thomas declined the invitation did not
en|abash the holy man in the least; for, as the audience finally dispersed, he secured
en|a few faithful ones, with whom he adjourned to the study, and enjoyed what he
en|afterward referred to as a precious season. Mrs. Wedgewell, who rendered but feeble reverence unto
en|him who was at once her spouse and her spiritual adviser, had been known to
en|say that when the old gentleman was wound up there was no knowing when he
en|would run down again; and all who saw the good man on the morning after
en|the meeting, admitted that his wife’s simile was an uncommonly apt one. Squire Tomple believed
en|so fully in the advantages of the early bird over all others in search of
en|sustenance, that his store was always opened at sunrise; yet George Doughty had just taken
en|the third shutter from the front window, when a gentle tap on the shoulder caused
en|him to drop the rather heavy board upon his toes. As he wrathfully turned himself,
en|he beheld the approving countenance and extended congratulatory hand of the Reverend Wedgewell. “George, my
en|dear, my noble young friend,” said he, as the irate youth squeezed his agonized toes,
en|“you have performed a most noble and meritorious action--an action which you will never have
en|cause to regret.” For a moment or two the young man’s face said many things
en|not seemly to express in appropriate words to a clergyman; but he finally recovered his
en|sense of politeness, and replied: “I hope I shan’t repent of it, but I don’t
en|know. It may be noble and meritorious to sign the pledge, but a fellow needs
en|to have twenty times as much man in him to keep it.” “Now you don’t
en|mean to say, George, that you’ll allow such a vile appetite to regain its ascendency
en|over you?” pleaded the preacher. “_’Tisn’t_ a vile appetite,” quickly replied the young man. “I
en|need whisky as much as I need bread and butter--yes, and a great deal more,
en|too. I have to open the store at sunrise, and keep it open till nine
en|o’clock and after, have to make myself agreeable to anywhere from two to twenty people
en|at a time, sell all I can, watch people who will steal the minute your
en|eye is off of them, not let anybody feel neglected, and see that I get
en|cash from everybody who isn’t good pay. When there isn’t anybody here, I’ve got to
en|keep the books, see that the stock don’t run down in spots, and stir up
en|people that are slow pay. The only way I can do it all is by
en|taking something to help me. I _hate_ whisky--I’m going to try to leave it alone;
en|but I tell you, Dominie, it’s going to be one of the biggest fights you
en|ever knew a young man to go into.” The reverend listener was as easily depressed
en|as he was exalted, and Doughty’s short speech had the effect of greatly elongating the
en|minister’s countenance. Yet he had a great deal of that pertinacity which is as necessary
en|to soldiers of the cross as it is to those of the bayonet; so he
en|began manfully to search his mind for some weapon or means of defense which the
en|clerk could use. Suddenly his countenance brightened, his benevolent eyes enlarged behind his glasses, and
en|he exclaimed: “Just the thing! My dear young friend, the hand of Providence is in
en|this matter. Your worthy employer was the chairman of our meeting last night; certainly he
en|will be glad to give you such assistance as shall lessen the amount of your
en|labors. Here he comes now. Let _me_ manage this affair; I really ask it as
en|a favor.” “I’m much obliged, but I think--confound it!” ejaculated the young man, as his
en|companion hastened out of earshot and buttonholed Squire Tomple. Half smiling and half frowning Doughty
en|retired from the door, but took up a new position, from which he could see
en|the couple. To the eyes of the clerk, his employer seemed a rock in his
en|unchanging pose, while the old preacher, rich in many a grace not peculiar to country
en|storekeepers, yet utterly ignorant of business and such of its perversions as are called requirements,
en|seemed a mere lamb--a fancy which was strengthened by the incessant gesturing and change of
en|position in which he indulged when in conversation. The pair soon separated; the minister walked
en|away, his step seeming not so exultant as when he approached the merchant; while the
en|latter, appearing to his clerk to be broader, deeper, and more solid than ever, approached
en|the store, lifted up his head, displayed the face he usually wore when he found
en|he had made a bad debt, and said, “George, I wish you wouldn’t try to
en|talk about business to ministers. Old Wedgewell has just pestered me nearly to death; says
en|you complain of having too much to do, and that you have to drink to
en|keep up. It’ll be just like him to tell somebody else, and a pretty story
en|that’ll be to go around about the chairman of a temperance meeting.” “I didn’t mean
en|to say anything to him,” replied the clerk; “but he made me drop a shutter
en|on my toes, and I guess that loosened my tongue a little. I didn’t tell
en|him anything but the truth, though, Squire. I signed the pledge, last night, hoping you’d
en|help me through.” “What--what do you mean, George?” asked the merchant, in a tone which
en|defined the word “conservative” more clearly than lexicographer ever did. “I can’t work so many
en|hours a day without drinking sometimes,” replied the clerk. “What I ask of you is
en|to take a boy. If I could come in a couple of hours later every
en|morning--and there’s next to nothing done in the first two hours of the day--I could
en|have a decent amount of rest, not have to hurry so much, and wouldn’t break
en|down so often, and have to go to whisky to be helped up again.” “A
en|boy would have to be paid,” remarked the Squire in the tone he habitually used
en|when making a penitential speech in class-meeting; “and here’s summer-time coming; there isn’t much business
en|done in summer, you know.” “A boy won’t cost more than a dollar a week
en|the first year,” replied the clerk, “and you’d make that out of the people who
en|sometimes _have_ to go somewhere else and trade on days when you’re not here and
en|I’m too busy to wait on them. There _isn’t_ so much money made in summer;
en|but women come to the store then a good deal more than they do in
en|the winter, and they take up an awful amount of time. Besides, the store has
en|to be opened about two hours earlier every morning than it does in winter.” The
en|merchant pinched his gloomy brow and reflected. Doughty looked at him without much hopefulness. The
en|Squire’s heart might be all right, but his pocket-book was by far the more sensitive
en|and controlling organ. At last the Squire said, “Well, if it’s for _your_ good that
en|you want the boy, you ought to be willing to pay his salary. Besides----” “Excuse
en|me, Squire Tomple,” interrupted Doughty; “’tisn’t for my good alone. ‘Accursed be he who putteth
en|the bottle to his brother’s lips.’ I’ve heard you quote that to more than one
en|man right in this store. That’s what you’re doing to me if you keep on.
en|You sell half as much again as any other storekeeper in town, and why? Because
en|I am smart enough to hold custom. I haven’t cared to do anything else. I’ve
en|given myself up to making and holding custom for you, and I took to whisky
en|to keep me up to my work.” “Well, haven’t I paid you for all you’ve
en|done?” demanded the proprietor. “Yes; but now I ask you to pay a little more.
en|I’ve told you why; and now the case stands just here: which do you care
en|for most, the price of a boy or the soul of your faithful clerk? _You_
en|say a man’s soul’s in danger if he drinks.” “Well, I’ll tell you, George,” replied
en|the Squire, “I’ll think about it. I want to do what’s right; but I--I don’t
en|like to have other people’s sins fastened on me.” CHAPTER III. A WET BLANKET. The
en|first task to which the penitent Crupp devoted himself on the morning after the meeting
en|was hardly that which his new admirers had supposed he would attempt. They imagined he
en|would knock in the heads of his barrels, and allow the accursed contents to flood
en|his cellar; but Crupp, on the contrary, closed out the entire lot, for cash, at
en|the highest prices he could exact from dealers with whom he had lately been in
en|competition. “’Twas a splendid lot of liquors,” said Crupp, in the course of an explanatory
en|speech at the post-office, while every one was waiting for the opening of the regular
en|daily mail; “and though I _do_ feel above sellin’ ’em over the counter, they’re better
en|for men that _will_ drink than any that have ever come into Barton since I’ve
en|been here.” With easier mind and heavier pocket, the ex-rumseller then called upon the Rev.
en|Jonas Wedgewell. That good man’s domestic, although from an ever-green isle whose children do not
en|generally regard whisky with abhorrence, had sympathetically caught the spirit of her employers, and as
en|she had not heard of Mr. Crupp’s change of mind, she left him standing on
en|the piazza while she called Mr. Wedgewell. The divine descended the stairway two steps at
en|a time, dived into the parlor, and had a congratulatory speech half delivered before he
en|discovered that the new convert was not there. He wildly shouted, “Mr. Crupp!” traced the
en|penitent by his voice, escorted him to the parlor with a series of hand-shakings, shoulder-pattings,
en|and bows, and forcibly dropped him into an elegant chair which Mrs. Wedgewell had bought
en|only to show, and in which no member of the family had ever dared to
en|sit. “Ah, my valiant friend,” said the Rev. Jonas, hastily drawing a chair near Mr.
en|Crupp, and shedding upon him the full effulgence of a countenance beaming with enthusiastic adoration;
en|“the morning songs of the angels of God must have been sweeter this morning as
en|they thought of your noble deed. You have cast off the shackles of a most
en|accursed bondage. Doubtless you wish to fulfill all of the conditions of the liberty with
en|which Christ hath made you free. The church----” “Excuse me, parson,” interrupted Mr. Crupp; “but
en|I don’t want to join the church--not just now, anyhow. I----” “Wish to consecrate your
en|ill-gotten gains to the service of the Lord,” broke in the good pastor; but Mr.
en|Crupp frowned, then pouted, then compressed his lips tightly, and gave so sudden a twitch
en|as to wrench one of the joints of the sacred chair, as he replied: “No,
en|sir, I don’t, for I haven’t any ill-gotten gains. I never sold anything but good
en|liquor, and the price was always fair. I never sold any liquor to a drunken
en|man, either. What I came to you for is this: I know who drinks, when
en|they drink, what they take, and I know pretty well _why_ they drink. Some of
en|them signed the pledge last night, and they’re going to have an awful hard job
en|in keeping it.” “Prayer----” interrupted the minister, but the hard-headed Crupp quickly completed the sentence.
en|“Prayer never cured a dyspeptic stomach, that I’ve heard of, and I don’t believe it’ll
en|take away a man’s hunger for whisky. These fellows that’s been drinking, and have got
en|anything to ’em, _can_ be kept from falling into the old ways again; but they’ve
en|got to be handled carefully, and what I came to you for was to ask
en|who was going to do the handling? You know who’s free-handed with money in your
en|congregation, and free-handed men ought to be free-hearted. I’m going to Dominie Brown on the
en|same errand, and to the other preachers, too.” Mr. Crupp’s speech consumed only a moment
en|of time, but its effect upon the preacher was wonderful--and depressing. From being a mirror
en|of irrepressible Christian exultation, Mr. Wedgewell’s face became as solemn as it ever was when
en|he bemoaned from the pulpit the apathy of the elect. His eyes enlarged behind his
en|glasses, and he stared for a moment in an abstracted manner at a dreadful chromo
en|which hung upon his wall--a chromo at which no one in active possession of his
en|mental faculties could possibly have looked so long. But the old pastor had a heart
en|so great that even his theology had been unable to wall it in, and after
en|a moment of inevitable despondency he realized that Crupp was intent upon doing good. “Mr.
en|Crupp,” said he, turning his head suddenly, and regaining a portion of his earlier expression
en|of countenance, “I do not fully comprehend your intention, but I can see that it
en|is good. May I ask what the people of God can do for these beings
en|who have been under the dominion of alcohol?” “Well, it’s a long story,” replied the
en|old bartender. “Among them that signed, there isn’t one in ten that ever drank, and
en|of them that drank, half of ’em’ll take something before night.” “And break their solemn
en|vow! Awful! awful!” ejaculated the minister. “Yes,” said Crupp, “_’tis_ awful; but, on the other
en|hand, there’s some that’s in earnest. There’s Tom Adams, now--he that drives the brick-yard team.
en|Tom’s a good, square, honest fellow, and he loves his family, but I don’t see
en|how he’s going to stop drinking. He can’t work without it; leastways, he can’t work
en|along the way he’s working now. Deacon Jones ought to give him easier work to
en|do until he can bring himself around; but Deacon Jones won’t waste his money in
en|that way, if he _is_ a member of your church. Then there’s old Bunley: there
en|isn’t anything _to_ him. He’s been drinking and drinking and drinking this forty year, he
en|says, and yet he was well brought up, and he can’t keep himself from going
en|to church every Sunday. He’s got some children that ain’t grown yet, and if some
en|of the storekeepers would only give him credit without ever expecting to see their money
en|again, the old fellow wouldn’t get down-hearted so often, and maybe he could quit drinking.
en|As far as taking care of his family goes, he isn’t good for much the
en|way he is; he borrows from soft-hearted fellows who can’t afford to lose as well
en|as the storekeepers can, and _maybe_ he steals sometimes--I don’t say he _does_, mind. At
en|any rate, the biggest part of his support comes out of the public, and as
en|the public can’t help itself, it ought to be sensible enough to try to make
en|the old chap feel and act like a man.” “Bless me!” exclaimed Mr. Wedgewell, who
en|had through all Mr. Crupp’s delivery sat erect with his hands upon his knees, and
en|his eyes and mouth wide open. “I assure you, my dear sir, that I never
en|had an idea that the success of the temperance cause depended upon so many conditions,
en|and I also beg to assure you”--here the Reverend Jonas hastily proffered his right hand--“that
en|I appreciate and admire the spirit which has prompted you to examine this subject in
en|so many of its bearings, and to endeavor to throw light upon it. But surely
en|all the--the men who, as you express it, have been drinking--surely these cannot be constrained
en|to continue by conditions similar to those which you have instanced? There must be some
en|who, if only they exercise their will-power, will succeed in putting their vile enemy under
en|their feet?” “Yes,” replied Crupp, “there _are_ such. Lots of young fellows drink only because
en|they think it’s smart, and because they haven’t got man enough in them to stop
en|when they want to. They’re like a lot of wolves--plucky enough when they’re together, but
en|a live rooster could scare one of them if he caught him alone. _I’m_ going
en|to look out for _that_ crowd myself; they need somebody to preach to ’em wherever
en|he can catch ’em, and I know where they hang out. But I’m not through
en|with the other kind yet. There’s Fred Macdonald, he’s going to be the hardest man
en|to manage in the whole lot. Good family, you know--got a judge for a father,
en|and ambitious as the----ambitious as Napoleon Bonaparte. He’s in with all the steamboat fellows, and
en|whisky is an angel alongside of some things they carry. They’ll ruin him, sure. Steamboating
en|looks like something big to him, you know; it shows off better than country stores
en|and saw-mills. It’s no use talkin’ to him; I’ve tried it once or twice, for
en|I know the steamboat people of old; but he as good as told me to
en|mind my own business. Now if some of the business men could get up something
en|enterprising, and put Fred at the head of it, on condition that he wouldn’t drink
en|any more, they might make money and save him from going to the--the bad. _I’ll_
en|put some money into the thing, for I believe in Fred. Of course he’ll have
en|to be watched a little, for he may be too venturesome; but he can get
en|more trade and get more work out of his men than any other man in
en|this county.” “Mr. Crupp,” said the minister, again taking the hand of the newly-made reformer,
en|and laying his own left hand affectionately upon Mr. Crupp’s right elbow, “I cannot find
en|words adequate to the expression of my admiration of your earnestness in this great moral
en|movement. But I must confess that your treatment of the subject is one to which
en|I am utterly unaccustomed. I have been wont to regard intemperance solely as an indication
en|of an infirm will and a depraved appetite, but your theory seems plausible; indeed, I
en|do not see that either of our respective standpoints need be wrong. But, with regard
en|to the employment of the reformatory means you suggest, I am not a capable adviser.
en|It might be well for you to consult some of our leading business men.” “That’s
en|what I am going to do,” replied Crupp. “And I am going to see the
en|doctors, too, and all the other ministers. What I want of _you_ is, to back
en|me up; preach at these fellows that are well enough off to make themselves useful.”
en|“I’ll do it!” replied the minister with emphasis. “A suitable text has already providentially entered
en|my mind: ‘Am I my brother’s keeper?’ Three heads and application: _First_, demonstrate that every
en|man _is_ his brother’s keeper; _second_, show how in the divine economy it is wise
en|that this should be so; _third_, the example of Christ; _application_, our duty to the
en|needy in our midst. Another text suggests itself: ‘We, then, that are strong ought to
en|bear the infirmities of the weak.’ And yet another: ‘Give strong drink unto him that
en|is ready to perish;’ argument to be that if the Inspired Word justifies such action
en|as that implied by the text, and if alcohol is the demon we believe it
en|to be, it is our duty to prevent, by any means in our power, people
en|from reaching a condition in which such a terrible remedy must be used. I beg
en|your pardon, my dear Mr. Crupp,” exclaimed the minister, springing excitedly from his chair; “but
en|if you have any other calls to make, I will repair at once to my
en|study and prepare a discourse based upon one of these texts. Excuse my seeming rudeness
en|in thus abruptly closing our interview, but my soul is on fire--on fire with ardor
en|which I cannot but believe is from heaven.” “Oh, certainly,” replied Mr. Crupp, rising quite
en|briskly. “Business is business; it’s so in the liquor trade, I know, and I suppose
en|it is in preaching. I’ll go down and see Squire Tomple, I guess.” The Rev.
en|Jonas Wedgewell dropped abruptly into a chair, and the fire with which his soul had
en|been consuming seemed suddenly to expire. His face became blank and expressionless, his lower jaw
en|dropped a little, and he gasped, “Squire Tomple? I had a discouraging conversation with him
en|only yesterday morning on a subject involving very nearly the ideas which you have advanced.
en|His very estimable clerk, George Doughty, who signed the pledge at our meeting, asserted that
en|his work must decrease in volume in order that he might continue faithful; so I
en|made haste to intercede for him with his employer, but I did not meet with
en|that encouragement which I had hoped for. Brother Tomple intimated that temperance was temperance and
en|business was business, and even made some remarks which have since seemed to me to
en|contain implications that I was unduly concerned about his affairs.” “Tomple’s a--a hog, if he
en|_is_ a church member,” replied the irreverent Crupp; “but he’s got to make himself useful
en|if plain talk will do it. It takes all kinds of men to make a
en|world, parson, or to make men act like men to their neighbors. Perhaps if you
en|preachers come down on rich men who hoard their money, and poor men that are
en|about as stingy with how-d’ye-do’s, and if business men show the public that it’s as
en|cheap to reform a pauper as it is to support him, and that it isn’t
en|the thing to stand by, while a man’s killing himself, without sayin’ a word or
en|spendin’ a cent to prevent him--perhaps we can be of some use in the world.
en|Good day, parson.” CHAPTER IV. REFORM WITH MONEY IN IT. Tom Adams, driver of the
en|brick-yard wagon, and signer of one of the pledges circulated at the great temperance meeting,
en|was certainly a man worth saving. He had a wife and was rich in children.
en|His wife was faithful, good-natured, and industrious, and his children were of that bright, irrepressible
en|nature which is about the most valuable of inheritances in this land where other inheritances
en|do not average largely in money value. For the good of such a group it
en|was very desirable that the head of the family should be in the constant possession
en|of strong arms and all his wits. And even for his own sake Tom was
en|worth a great deal more attention than men of his kind ever receive. He was
en|perfectly honest, a hard worker, cheerier in temperament than any pastor in the village, quicker-witted
en|than most of the lawyers within the judicial circuit upon which the town of Barton
en|was situated, and more generous in proportion to his means than any of his well-to-do
en|fellow-citizens. During the season for making and delivering bricks he worked from sunrise to sunset,
en|rendered fair count to seller and buyer, and never abused his employer’s horses. His regular
en|pay was seventy-five cents per day, which sum, in a land where flour was sold
en|at two cents per pound and meat was only twice as high as flour, and
en|a comfortable house could be hired at four dollars per month, paid his family expenses.
en|But the season at the brick-yard lasted only during six months of the twelve. During
en|the remaining six months Tom gladly did any work he could find: he drove teams
en|where any hauling was to be done, chopped wood, worked in the pork-houses where merchants
en|prepared for the Southern market the fatted hogs which were the principal legal-tenders for the
en|indebtedness of farmer customers, formed part of the crew of one of the many flatboats
en|which conveyed the meat to market, and did whatever other work he could find. But
en|in the winter season, when the family appetite was most industrious, Tom could not find
en|employment for all his time, while the merchants who trusted him made more frequent requests
en|for money than Tom was able to honor. When he was idle, he found himself
en|more welcome at the liquor-shops than anywhere else; when he grew despondent at his inability
en|to pay, he sought solace at these same places; when in the steady work and
en|long hours of the summer season he became gradually “worked out” and “used up”--experiences not
en|infrequent with Tom--he went to the liquor-shops for the only relief he had ever been
en|able to find. His experience did not differ greatly from that of men of higher
en|social standing, who, under similar mental and physical conditions, drink high-priced wines. He gradually increased
en|the quantity of his potations, and went through the successive experiences of being unmanned by
en|liquor, striving to rebuild himself by the power which had broken him, becoming by turns
en|gay, silly, boisterous, pugnacious, sullen, apathetic, and finally penitent. Each of his sprees cost him
en|several days in time and several dollars in money--a fact which no one realized more
en|clearly than Tom himself; yet the feeling which had made him take the first drinks
en|of these frightful series was one which had its seat in his own better nature,
en|and which he had many times found more powerful than every influence he could bring
en|to bear against it. He had listened to many a private lecture on the subject
en|of his weakness, and had honestly admitted the truth of all that was said to
en|him on the subject; he had signed many a pledge in the most agonized earnest,
en|and had broken every one of them. On the Monday which followed the temperance meeting
en|Tom Adams was nearly frantic with his old longing. The rest of Sunday had been
en|a hindrance rather than a help to him, for he had already suffered several days
en|from the effects of abstaining from his usual after-dinner and after-supper potations. The amount usually
en|drank on these occasions had not been great, but the habit had for some years
en|been so regular that his amazed and indignant physique protested against the change. Had he
en|been capable of spiritually withdrawing himself from the world on the day of the Lord,
en|he might have found help and strength; but he was as incapable of such a
en|thing as were nine-tenths of the church-members in Barton. While he remained at home, his
en|children were noisy enough to have hurried a rapt seer back to the realization of
en|earthly things; when he went abroad he could not, as was his usual Sunday habit,
en|step quietly into the back door of Bayne’s liquor-store. He strolled down to the stable-yard
en|of the Barton House, hoping to find some one with whom he could talk horse;
en|but the hostler was not in sight, and the stable-boy, who had been heard to
en|say he “didn’t count much on them fellers what signed the pledge and went back
en|on their friends,” eyed him with evident disgust. In the street he met people going
en|to and from church and Sunday-school, and they looked at him as if their eyes
en|were asking, “Are you keeping your pledge?” Then, to crown all, his wife gave him
en|such a beseeching and yet doubting look every time he left the house and returned
en|to it that he almost hated the good woman for her affectionate anxiety. Tom was
en|up bright and early Monday morning, and though he soon mounted his wagon and left
en|his wife’s eyes behind him, he found his longing for liquor as close to him
en|as ever. Reaching the brick-yard, he was rather startled to find there Deacon Jones, his
en|employer, and owner of a store as well as the kilns. The deacon looked at
en|him as all the religious people had done on Sunday, and Tom inwardly cursed him.
en|“How are you, Tom?” inquired the deacon, and then, without waiting for a reply, remarked:
en|“There’s somethin’ I’ve been a-wantin’ to talk to you ’bout, Tom, an’ I was sure
en|o’ catchin’ you here, so I came over before breakfast. You signed the pledge t’other
en|night.” This latter clause was delivered with an accompanying glance which caused Tom to put
en|a great deal of anger into his reply, although his words were few. “Yes, an’
en|kep’ it, too.” “I’m glad of it, Tom. There’s been times when you didn’t, you
en|know. Well, what I want to say is this: Some folks say that some men
en|drink because they have to work too hard, an’ because they have trouble. Now, mebbe--I
en|only say mebbe, mind--_mebbe_ that’s what upset you those other times. Now, if I was
en|to give you work all the year round at seventy-five cents a day, an’ not
en|work you more’n ten hours a day, would it help you to keep straight?” “Would
en|it?” said Tom, scratching his head, wrinkling his brows, and eying the deacon incredulously “Why,
en|of course it would.” “Well, then,” said the deacon, “I’ll do it. As long as
en|the brick business is good you can work at haulin’ from seven to twelve, an’
en|one to six. Don’t you s’pose you could put two or three hundred more brick
en|on a load without hurtin’ the hosses? I don’t want to lose any more’n I
en|can help, you know, by cuttin’ down your time. Rainy days I’ll keep you busy
en|at the store some way; them’s the days farmers can’t do much on the farm,
en|so they bring their butter and eggs to town, and there’s a sight of measurin’
en|an’ weighin’ to be done. An’ after the brick season’s over I’ll find you somethin’
en|to do at the store. You can put the pork-house an’ warehouse to rights before
en|the packin’ season begins, an’ you can weigh the corn an’ wheat an’ oats an’
en|pork when they come in, and mend bags, and work in the pork-house three months
en|out of the six. You wouldn’t object to takin’ night-spells in the pork-house instead of
en|day-spells, would you, when we have to work day _and_ night? Night-wages costs us most,
en|you know, an’ you ought to help us make up what we lose on you
en|when there’s nothin’ doin’.” “Just as _you_ say,” replied Tom. He did not clasp the
en|deacon in a grateful embrace, for the deacon had, in his thrifty way, prevented Tom
en|from feeling especially grateful. The owner of the brick-yard had intimated that the new arrangement
en|was for Tom’s especial benefit, but his later remarks caused this feature of the arrangement
en|to speedily disappear from view. But, although not doubting for an instant that the deacon
en|meant to get his money back with usury, Tom felt his heart growing lighter every
en|moment. At the same time he felt angry at the deacon’s occasional suggestions that the
en|arrangements were partly of the nature of charity. So he replied: “Just as _you_ say;
en|but, deacon, I ain’t the feller that wants money for work I don’t do, _you_
en|know that. The arrangement suits me first-rate, but I’m goin’ to work hard for my
en|money; you can bet all your loose change on _that_.” “Thomas!” ejaculated the deacon sternly,
en|“I am not in the habit of betting. It’s a careless, foolish, wasteful, sinful way
en|of using money.” “That’s so,” replied Tom reflectively; “unless,” he continued, “you’re one of the
en|winnin’ kind.” “It is a business I don’t intend to go into, so the less
en|said of it the better. So my offer suits you, does it?” “I’ll shake hands
en|on it,” replied Tom, extending his hand. “Wait a moment,” said the deacon, retiring his
en|own right hand to a conservative position behind his back. “If it suits you,” continued
en|the deacon impressively, “you agree to stick to your pledge; no foolin’ with whisky again,
en|mind.” “Nary drop,” said Tom, with great emphasis. “Ten minutes ago I wouldn’t have given
en|a pewter dime for my chance of sticking it out through the day, but now
en|I wouldn’t give a cent for a barr’l full of ten-year-old rye.” “All right, then--shake
en|hands. And we begin to-day--or say to-morrow--there’s lots of bricks wanted to-day--here’s the orders. And
en|may the Lord help you, Thomas--help you to hold out steadfast unto the end. Now
en|I reckon I’ll get home to breakfast.” As the deacon walked off he soliloquized in
en|this manner: “There! I wonder if that’ll suit Crupp an’ Brother Wedgewell? What a queer
en|team them two fellows make! Queer that Crupp should have bothered me two hours Saturday
en|night, an’ the preacher should have come out so strong about bein’ our brothers’ keepers
en|the very next day. ’Twas a Christian act for me to do, too. ‘He that
en|converteth a sinner from the error of his ways’--ah! blessed be the promises. An’ I
en|won’t lose a cent by the operation--_I_ can keep him busy enough. When folks know
en|what I’ve done an’ what I done it for, I guess they’ll think I’ve got
en|my good streaks after all. I declare, I ought to have told him I couldn’t
en|pay for days when he was sick; ’tain’t too late yet, though--he won’t back out
en|on _that_ account. Mebbe I can talk him into j’ining the church, too--who knows, an’
en|some day in ’xperience meetin’ mebbe he’ll tell how it all came about through me.
en|He must bring his dinners with him when he’s workin’ about the store. I ought
en|to have done that with my clerk before he took to lunchin’ off the crackers
en|and cheese busy days--these little things all cost. But it _does_ make a man feel
en|good to do kindnesses to his fellow-men.” As for Tom Adams, he mounted the wagon,
en|seized the reins, and exclaimed, “By thunder! ’fore I haul a durned brick, I’ll just
en|drive home by the back way and tell the old woman. Reckon she won’t look
en|at me any more in _that_ way then. Like enough he’s right when he says
en|_some_ says mebbe workin’ too hard makes fellows drink. It never got into _my_ head
en|before, though.” As Tom drove through a back street in which Mr. Crupp lived, that
en|worthy stared at the empty wagon inquiringly. “The old man’s engaged me for a year,
en|at six bits a day, and only ten hours a day to work,” shouted Tom
en|in explanation. “The devil!” replied the new reformer, and seizing his hat he hurried off
en|to the Rev. Jonas Wedgewell. The pastor was discovered through an open window at his
en|matutinal repast, and the eager Crupp thrust his head in the window and shouted, “First
en|blood, parson! Old Jones has hired Tom for a year, and he’s only got ten
en|hours a day to work.” The holy man raised his hands, despite the incumbrances of
en|half a biscuit and a coffee cup, and exclaimed, “Bless the Lord for the first
en|fruits of the seed so newly sown. Who would have thought so undemonstrative a man
en|would have been the first to heed the word of exhortation?” “He’s the first to
en|see money in it--that’s why,” explained Crupp. “My dear sir, do you really ascribe Deacon
en|Jones’s meritorious action to sordid motives?” asked the old pastor, opening his mouth and eyes
en|as if the answer for which he waited was to come through them. “Hum--well, no--I
en|reckon ’twas a little mixed,” replied Mr. Crupp, meditatively analyzing a blossom of a honeysuckle
en|growing by the pastor’s window. “I dinged at him, you preached at him, he thought
en|it over, and whatever Jonathan Jones thinks over long is pretty sure to have money
en|in it somewhere in the end. He’ll make mor’n he’ll lose on Tom, an’ it’s
en|best he should--he’ll have a better heart to try another experiment of the same sort
en|one of these days. But I didn’t mean to interrupt your breakfast--beg your pardon, Mrs.
en|Wedgewell and young ladies, for not ringing the bell, but I was too full of
en|the news to behave myself. Good by.” And Mr. Crupp started for his own breakfast-table,
en|while the Reverend Jonas’s eyes seemed directed at some object just out of sight, as
en|he abstractedly raised his coffee cup to his lips. CHAPTER V. AN ASTONISHED VIRGINIAN. Why
en|old Bunley had made Barton his place of residence nobody knew. The most plausible theory
en|ever advanced on the subject came from the former proprietor of the Barton House, who
en|said that Bunley, happening to be traveling that way, had found the brandy at the
en|Barton House so good that he hadn’t the heart to leave it. The brandy lasted
en|so long that old Bunley--then twenty years younger--while consuming it became acquainted with nearly everybody
en|in the town; and as he had no engagements that restrained him from making himself
en|agreeable, he found himself well liked, and entreated to make his home at Barton. He
en|reported--and his report was afterward verified--that he was the son of a Virginia planter, and
en|was unpopular at home because he had made a runaway match with a splendid girl,
en|whose only fault was that her family did not rank very high. Bunley’s father had
en|cut his son off with a thousand dollars, but had considerately sent the money with
en|the letter of dismissal; so the happy couple were leisurely spending the money and waiting
en|for the old gentleman to relent, as irate fathers always do in books. But while
en|Bunley was enjoying the hospitalities of Barton, annoyed only by the fact that his purse
en|was growing light, he heard of his father’s sudden death and of the inheritance by
en|an unloving brother of the entire estate. Then the young bridegroom attempted to obtain money
en|by borrowing, for this was the only method of money-getting he understood; but the small
en|success which attended his efforts did not pay for the annoyance which his soulless creditors
en|gave him. Then he tried gambling, and, by devoting his mind to it, succeeded so
en|well that no one but an occasional commercial traveler, to whom Bunley’s ways were unknown,
en|would play with him. Then, under the guise of being clerk of the Barton House,
en|he became its actual barkeeper, and attracted so much custom away from the other liquor-sellers
en|that the grateful proprietor took him into partnership, and, dying a year later, bequeathed the
en|whole business to him. But the good brandy which had first persuaded Bunley to stop
en|at Barton continued its fascinations, and the new proprietor of the Barton House, while liked
en|by all travelers, grew so unpopular with purveyors of flour, meat, and other hotel necessities
en|that the sheriff was finally called upon to settle the differences between them by disposing
en|of the hotel property at auction. After that Bunley ran to seed, to use an
en|expression common in Barton. How he lived during the twenty years which followed was not
en|well understood. His wife died, and it was understood that he married some money the
en|second time; but it was none the less whispered about town that Bunley had been
en|seen at night to borrow at woodpiles whose owners he had not consulted. He went
en|upon mighty sprees, and carried the bouquet of liquor wherever he went. He started a
en|small groggery of his own, in which many bright boys learned to drink. He had
en|long since ruined the credit which he obtained on the strength of his second wife’s
en|property, for he never paid an account. And yet the most aggrieved of Bunley’s creditors
en|could not help being soft-hearted when they saw the old man in church, as he
en|was every Sunday morning with his two boys. The gentleman which was in old Bunley
en|then showed itself in his face and manner, and it _did_ seem too bad that
en|any one who could look and act so much like a man should not be
en|trusted to the extent of a dollar’s worth of sugar or a hundred pounds of
en|flour. Squire Tomple had thought so one Sunday, and as the Squire strove to keep
en|worldly thoughts out of his mind on the Lord’s day, his mind became filled with
en|old Bunley--so much so, that on the following Monday he decoyed Bunley into his store,
en|and talked so pleasantly to him that the old gentleman actually made the request for
en|which the Squire hoped. He bought rather more than the Squire had meant to sell
en|him on credit, but his promise of early payment was so distinct and emphatic that
en|the Squire’s doubt was not fairly established for many months. This story in all its
en|details was told by the Squire to Mr. Crupp, after that gentleman announced to him
en|that something should be done for old Bunley. “That was because you didn’t go about
en|the job in the right way,” said Crupp. “He’s got just enough conceit to suppose
en|that he’s going to pay all his bills some day, and he feels that when
en|the time comes your profit’ll pay for your kindness. That conceit of his is just
en|what needs to be taken down--it’s got to be done kindly--so that he understands that
en|whatever he gets comes out of pure charity and the desire to make him comfortable,
en|even at a loss. Now, he and his little family can live on about a
en|dollar a day. I’ll stand half the expense of supporting him for three months if
en|you’ll do the other half, and we’ll talk plain, good-natured English to him, and let
en|him understand he’s a pauper. That’ll put him on his mettle. What do you say?”
en|The Squire looked grave at once--as grave as he had appeared when an uninsured hogshead
en|of sugar belonging to him had fallen from a steamboat gang-plank into the river, and
en|melted. The proposition seemed to take his breath away, in fact; but in a moment
en|or two he regained it. “Look here, Crupp,” said he, “temperance is all very well;
en|but I don’t think it’s my business to stand part of the expenses of reforming
en|everybody, when I haven’t had anything to do with making drunkards. With you the case
en|is different. You say your liquors were always good; but, like enough, that made men
en|all the fonder of drinking the infernal things. You’re a public-spirited citizen, but you can’t
en|deny that you’ve had a thousand times more to do with making drunkards than I
en|have. The very fact that you _are_ a decent fellow yourself has made drinking halfway
en|respectable in Barton. The crime’s right at your own door, and you ought to pay
en|for it. You----” The Squire paused. Mr. Crupp’s face was very white and his teeth
en|were tightly set. Mr. Crupp _had_ been known to throw a disorderly visitor at his
en|bar halfway across the street; and although the Squire knew that his own avoirdupois was
en|too great to be treated so contemptuously, he had no desire to feel the weight
en|of Crupp’s fist. Besides, Crupp was a customer who bought a great deal and paid
en|promptly, and the Squire did not like to offend him and lose his custom. So
en|the Squire paused. “Go right on,” said Mr. Crupp very quietly. “I’ll not bear any
en|malice. I’ve said a great many worse things to myself. Don’t hold in anything you’ve
en|got on your mind.” “I’m done,” said the Squire, looking relieved and extending his hand.
en|“Crupp, I think a good deal of you, and I’m ashamed of myself for boiling
en|over as I did. But folks talk to me as if I was made of
en|money. I paid out a good deal on the expense of the meeting; the parson’s
en|been at me to help every lazy drunkard to get work; George Doughty wants more
en|pay or less work, so he won’t have such a hankering after liquor; and now
en|to be asked to help old Bunley, that’s owed me money a long time and
en|never paid it, that came near helping one of my boys to a taste for
en|liquor, that helps himself at my woodpile--it’s _too_ much, that’s all.” “Squire,” said Crupp, “isn’t
en|there something in your Bible that’s not complimentary to men who say to the needy,
en|‘Depart: be ye warmed and fed,’ but don’t put their hands into their pockets to
en|help the poor wretches along? I tell you that a man that’s got the love
en|of drink fixed in every muscle in his body and every drop of his blood
en|is worse off than any cold and hungry man you ever saw. Such men _sometimes_
en|help themselves out of their trouble, and stick to cold water; but the man that
en|does it is more of a hero, and he’s got better stuff in him, than
en|any other sort of sinner that ever repents. He’s got to be helped just like
en|drowning men have to be, and you’ve got to take hold of him just as
en|you do of a drowning man, by whatever part you can get the tightest grip
en|on. Bunley’s pride’s the only handle you can find on _him_, and you can’t get
en|at _that_ except by showing that you think enough of him to sink money in
en|him.” The Squire cast about in his mind for some argument in defense of his
en|money; but, as he found none, he acted like a good diplomatist, and started to
en|talk against time by uttering some promising generalizations. “I always meant, and I still mean,”
en|said he, “to do good with my money. That’s what it was given me for.
en|I’m only the Lord’s steward----” “And right here in Barton is where the Lord put
en|you to do it,” said Crupp. “Here’s where you made your money; here are the
en|people who know you and don’t suspect you of caring any less for your money
en|than other folks do for theirs; here are the people you know all about; you
en|know their weaknesses and their good points, and every dollar you spend on them you
en|can watch, and see that it does its duty.” “When I _know_ that helping a
en|man will be sure to reform him,” began the Squire, when again his companion interrupted
en|him: “Did you ever read of Christ’s letting a man suffer for fear that if
en|he cured him or fed him he might get sick or hungry again? If I
en|read straight, _he_ helped everybody that came to him, and everybody that needed help. I
en|suppose loafers were as thick in Judæa as they are in Barton; why, when he
en|healed those ten lepers there was only one of them decent enough to come back
en|and say “Thank you.” _I’ve_ got money enough to take Bunley on my own shoulders
en|for a little while, and I’m going to spend a good deal on such fellows;
en|but they want to see that they’re thought something of by men who never sold
en|whisky, who never made anything out of them, who are enough in earnest to do
en|something for them that costs more than talk does. I know it isn’t easy, but
en|it’s got to be done--that is, if Christianity is true.” Crupp’s last shot told. Squire
en|Tomple was orthodox, but he was not without reflective capacity, and many had been his
en|twinges of conscience at his practical rejection of undoubted deductions which he had drawn from
en|Christ’s teachings and example. But on this particular occasion, as on many others, he was
en|not defeated; he was only temporarily demoralized. In a moment he was on the defensive
en|again, and suddenly raised his head and opened his lips; but, whatever his idea was,
en|it remained unspoken; for in the eye of Crupp, which had been intently scrutinizing his
en|face and through it his heart, he detected a softness and haziness unusual in the
en|eyes of men. The Squire, not without a struggle, became at once shamefaced and obedient,
en|and said hurriedly, “Crupp, you’re a good, square man; I’m proud to know you, and
en|I’ll do what you like--for old Bunley, that is.” Great was the surprise of Bunley
en|himself, when he answered a knock at his door a few minutes later, to find
en|Squire Tomple and Mr. Crupp upon his front stoop, both of them looking and acting
en|as if extremely embarrassed. But old Bunley never forgot his Virginia breeding, not even before
en|a couple of creditors; so he invited both gentlemen to seats on the top step,
en|and then sat down between them. The Squire looked appealingly at Crupp; Crupp winked encouragingly
en|at the Squire; the Squire coughed feebly; Crupp plucked a stem of timothy grass, and
en|gazed at it as if he had never seen such a thing before; the Squire
en|took out a pocket-knife, and began to scrape his finger-nails, and then Crupp remarked that
en|it was a fine day. Bunley having cheerfully assented to this expression of opinion, there
en|was a moment or two of awkward silence, which was finally relieved by Bunley, who
en|drew from his pocket a plug of tobacco, from which he took a bite, after
en|first offering it to his visitors. A little more facial pantomime went on between Tomple
en|and Crupp, and then the Squire spoke. “Bunley,” said he, “you don’t seem to get
en|along very fast in the world.” “That’s a fact,” answered Bunley with hearty emphasis. “Luck
en|seems to go against me, no matter how I lay myself out. There ain’t a
en|man in this town that wants to do the right thing any more than I
en|do, but somehow I don’t get the chance. I signed the pledge t’other night at
en|the meetin’; but how I’m goin’ to stick to it, with all the trouble I’m
en|in, is more than I can see through.” “We’ve come down to help you do
en|it,” said the Squire. “To help you with money--not talk,” supplemented Crupp. Bunley looked at
en|both men quickly, from under the extreme inner edge of his upper eyelid. “We propose,
en|between us, to show you that we’re in dead earnest to help you keep the
en|pledge,” continued the Squire. “We’re going to give you, week after week, whatever you need
en|to live on for the next three months, so you won’t have any excuse for
en|drinking to drown trouble, and so you’ll have a chance to find something to do.”
en|Old Bunley sprang to his feet. “Gentlemen,” said he, “you’re--you’re _gentlemen_. It’s the first time
en|in my life that anybody ever cared _that_ much for me, though. You shan’t lose
en|anything by it, I promise you _that_; I’ll pay you back again the first chance
en|I get to make anything.” “We don’t _want_ it back,” said Crupp. “We won’t _take_
en|it back. We want to _give_ it to you, out and out----” “To show you
en|that it’s _you_ that we’re interested in, not ourselves,” interrupted the Squire. Then Old Virginia
en|came to the surface again; Bunley seemed to grow an inch or two, and to
en|swell several more as he replied, “I’m not a pauper, gentlemen.” “Certainly not,” said the
en|Squire hastily; “but you can’t pay your debts nor your current expenses, and Crupp and
en|I are a little ahead in the world, and willing to give you a hundred,
en|say--a little at a time.” “You’ve got a couple of boys to bring up, you
en|know, Bunley,” suggested Crupp. “And they ought to go among the best people, too,” said
en|the Squire. “You came of a good family----” “And their mother was a lady, too--every
en|inch of her!” exclaimed Bunley. “Of course she was,” said Crupp. “But, to come back
en|to business, we don’t want you to have any excuse to touch whisky again, and
en|we want you to live on us for the next three months as a personal
en|favor. After that, if you make any money, I s’pose the Squire’ll be glad to
en|sell you anything he keeps in his store; I know _I_ will, if I’m in
en|business then. But you mustn’t talk about paying now, ’cause it’s all nonsense. Come up
en|to the Squire’s store when you want anything. Good-by.” Bunley drew himself up with great
en|solemnity and old-time courtesy as he shook hands with both men. When his visitors reached
en|the friendly angle of an old, abandoned barn, both turned hastily, gazed through cracks between
en|the boards, and saw the old man sitting in a meditative attitude, with his lower
en|jaw in both his hands. “_Don’t_ that look good?” whispered Crupp, his face all animation.
en|“It does that,” replied the Squire; “there’s no dodging the question; it _does_ look good.”
en|CHAPTER VI. A COURSE NEVER SMOOTH. On a pleasant August evening, at that particular portion
en|of the day in which twilight shades into night, Fred Macdonald left his father’s house
en|and walked toward the opposite portion of the village. From his leisurely, elastic gait, the
en|artistic effect of his necktie, the pose of his hat, the rose-bud in his button-hole,
en|and the graceful carriage of his cane, it was very evident that Frederick’s steps did
en|not tend toward the fulfillment of any prosaic business engagement. It was not so dark
en|that he could not recognize, in occasional unlighted windows, certain faces well known, some of
en|them handsome, all of them pleasing; nor was it too dark, just after Fred had
en|bestowed a bow and a smile upon the occupant of each of these windows, and
en|passed on, for one to discern, by the expressions upon most of the faces that
en|slowly turned and looked after the young man, that Fred need not have gone farther
en|in search of a cordial welcome. But he walked on until he reached the residence
en|of the Rev. Jonas Wedgewell. To any one not a resident of Barton the house
en|might have seemed a strange one to be visited by a young man fond of
en|liquor and the company frequently found on Western steamboats; and the stranger’s surprise might have
en|increased, at finding that Fred had been so frequent a visitor that even the house
en|itself seemed glad to see him, and that the heavy old door seemingly opened of
en|its own accord, before Fred’s fingers had time to touch its antique knocker. But had
en|the supposititious observer possessed good eyes, whose actual powers were temporarily increased by the stimulus
en|of curiosity, his bewilderment would have ended a second later; for, as Fred stepped inside
en|the hall, there came from behind the door a small hand, and then a dainty
en|ruffle, and then a muslin sleeve, and these all took their direction toward the shoulder
en|of Fred’s coat; while there followed a profile which the beholder would have willingly gazed
en|upon longer, had it not almost instantaneously disappeared behind that side of Fred’s face which
en|was farthest from the door. Could the observer’s gaze have penetrated the window shades of
en|Parson Wedgewell’s little parlor, he would have seen a face, not girlish or of regular
en|features, and yet so full of happiness that its effect was that of absolute beauty
en|and the innocence of youth. There were estimable maidens in Barton who, scorning the thought
en|that they could be either jealous or envious, had frequently remarked to their intimates that
en|they could _not_ see what men found in Esther Wedgewell to rave about, and it
en|was well known that the mystery had never been satisfactorily explained to such young ladies
en|as had become the wives of men who had been among Miss Esther’s admirers. It
en|is even to be doubted whether Fred Macdonald himself could have verbally elucidated the matter;
en|there _have_ been such cases where long and joyous lifetimes have not sufficed in which
en|to frame such an explanation, and when the person most blessed has had to journey
en|into another world in search of adequate power of expression. Ordinarily Esther Wedgewell was a
en|young lady the pleasantness of whose face did not hide the fact that its owner’s
en|forehead was too high, the nose too short, the mouth too large, and the complexion
en|too pale for perfect beauty. But somehow young men noticed first of all Miss Esther’s
en|eyes, and these, though neither of heavenly blue, nor violet, nor the brownness of nuts,
en|nor large, nor melting, but only plain gray, were so honest in themselves, and so
en|sympathetic for others, that no one of any character cared to gaze from them to
en|any other of the young woman’s features. What Fred and Esther said to each other
en|during the first few minutes after their meeting, was of a nature which never shows
en|to full advantage in print; besides, it was in the nature of things that they
en|should say very little. In spite of the experience accumulated during a hundred or more
en|of just such meetings, it seemed necessary that a few minutes should be consumed by
en|Fred in assuring himself that it was really Esther who sat in the rocking-chair in
en|front of him; and the same time was used by the lady in determining that
en|the handsome, intelligent face in front of her was that of the only lover she
en|had ever accepted. Gradually, however, the sentences spoken by the couple became longer and more
en|frequent; their subjects were ordinary enough; being the mutual acquaintances they had met during the
en|day; the additions which had been made to the embroidery on the pair of slippers
en|which Esther, after the manner of most other betrothed maidens in America, had begun to
en|make for her lover; the quality of the singing in church on the preceding Sunday;
en|the latest news from Captain Hall’s expedition to the North Pole; the character of Shakespeare’s
en|Portia; and yet one would have supposed, from the countenances of both of these young
en|people, that in each of these topics there was some underlying motive of the most
en|delightful import; while their remarks seemed to indicate that there was but one side to
en|either of the subjects discussed, and that both Fred and Esther saw it with the
en|extreme clearness of earthly comprehension. Then, in a lull in the conversation, Fred asked, with
en|a courtesy and minuteness inherited from aristocratic parents, about Mr. and Mrs. Wedgewell, and elicited
en|the information that Esther’s father was composing a second sermon on intemperance. “Your father undoubtedly
en|is himself the best judge of the needs of his congregation,” said Fred, dropping his
en|eyes a little and playing with a bit of paper; “but I can’t help feeling
en|that he is wasting his fine talents in preaching on intemperance. If his sermons could
en|be heard and applied by the proper persons, they might do a great deal of
en|good; but what drunkard goes to church? Only moderate drinkers and people who don’t drink
en|at all ever hear your father’s sermons, and none of them have any need for
en|such instructions.” Esther brushed an imaginary thread or mote from her dress, and said, with
en|some embarrassment, “Father believes that the moderate drinkers are those who most need to be
en|warned.” “Why, Ettie!” exclaimed Fred, “how can he believe that? He must know that I
en|occasionally--that is, he knows that I am not one of the Sons of Temperance; yet
en|he gave me you”--here conversation ceased a moment as Fred stepped toward Esther, conveying unto
en|that lady an affectionate testimonial whose exact nature will be understood--“and he certainly would not
en|have done so had he supposed I was in any danger of being injured by
en|liquor.” Esther did not wait even until she had finished rearranging a disordered tress or
en|two to reply. “He said ‘yes,’ only after I told him of your promise to
en|me that you would not drink any more after we were married. He said you
en|were the best born and best bred young man he had ever met--as if I
en|didn’t already know it, you dear boy--but that he would rather bury me than let
en|me marry a drinking man.” During the delivery of this short speech Fred looked by
en|turns astonished, sober, flattered, sullen, indignant, and finally business-like and judicial. Then he said: “Darling,
en|you must let me believe that your father is not fully posted about men who
en|take an occasional glass. It’s no fault of his; he probably never tasted a drop
en|of liquor in his life--he may never have felt the need of it. But believe
en|me when I tell you that many of the smartest men drink sometimes, and are
en|greatly helped by it. A business man whose daily life can’t help being often irregular,
en|sometimes finds he can’t get along without something to help him through the day. Why,
en|a few days ago I helped Sam Crayme, captain of the “Excellence,” you know, at
en|a difficult bit of business; I worked thirty-six hours on a stretch, and made fifty
en|dollars by it. That’s more money than any of your young temperance men of Barton
en|ever make in a month, but I never could have done it if it hadn’t
en|been for an occasional drink.” “But,” said Esther, “you know I don’t say it by
en|way of complaint, Fred dear, but for a week after that you felt dull and
en|didn’t say much, and didn’t care to read, and one evening when I expected you
en|you didn’t come.” “But think how tired a man must be after such a job,
en|Ettie,” pleaded Fred in an injured tone. “You poor old fellow, I know it,” said
en|Esther; “but you wouldn’t have been so if you hadn’t done the work, and you
en|yourself say you couldn’t have done the work if you hadn’t drunk the liquor, and
en|you know you didn’t need the money so badly as to have had to do
en|so much. Any merchant in the town would be glad to give you employment at
en|which you would be your own natural self.” “And I would always be a poor
en|man if I worked for our plodding, small-paying merchants,” said Fred. “Why, Ettie, who own
en|the handsomest houses in town, who have the best horses, who set the best tables,
en|whose wives and children wear the best clothes? Why, Moshier and Brown and Crayme and
en|Wainright, every one of them moderate drinkers; I never in my life saw one of
en|them drunk.” “And I would rather be dead than be the wife of any one
en|of them,” said Esther with an energy which startled Fred. “Mrs. Moshier used to be
en|such a happy-looking woman, and now she is so quiet and has such sad eyes.
en|Brown seems to spend no end of money on his family; but his children are
en|always put to bed before he comes home, because he is as likely as not
en|to be cross and unkind to them; when they meet him on the street they
en|never shout ‘Papa!’ and rush up to him as your little brothers and sisters do
en|to _your_ father; but they look at him first with an anxious look that’s enough
en|to break one’s heart, and as likely as not cross the street to avoid meeting
en|him. Mrs. Crayme was having _such_ a pleasant time at Nellie Wainright’s party the other
en|night, when her husband, who she seldom enough has a chance to take into society
en|with her, said such silly things and stared around with such an odd look in
en|his eye that she made some excuse to take him home. And Nellie Wainright--she was
en|my particular friend before she was married, you know--was here a few days ago, and
en|I was telling her how happy I was, when suddenly she threw both arms around
en|my neck and burst out crying, and told me that she hoped that my husband
en|would never drink after I was married. She insists upon it that her husband is
en|the best man that ever lived, and that if she only mentions anything she would
en|like, she has it at once if money can buy it, and yet she is
en|unhappy. She says there’s always a load on her heart, and though she feels real
en|wicked about it, she can’t get rid of it.” Fred Macdonald was unable for some
en|moments to reply to this unexpected speech; he arose from his chair, and walked slowly
en|up and down the room, with his hands behind him, and with the countenance natural
en|to a man who has heard something of which he had previously possessed no idea.
en|Esther looked at him, first furtively, then tenderly; then she sprang to his side and
en|leaned upon his shoulder, saying, “Dear Fred, I know _you_ could never be that way;
en|but then all these women were sure they knew just the same about their lovers,
en|before they were married.” “Well, Ettie,” said Fred, passing an arm about the young lady,
en|“I really don’t know what’s to be done about it, if drinking moderately is the
en|cause of all these dreadful things; I’m bound to _be_ somebody; I’m in the set
en|of men that make money; they like me, and I understand them. But they all
en|take something, and you don’t know how they look at a man who refuses to
en|drink with them; all of them think he don’t amount to much, and some of
en|them actually feel insulted. What is a fellow to do?” “Go into some other set,
en|I suppose,” said Esther very soberly. “You don’t know what you’re saying, my dear girl,”
en|said Fred. “What else is there for a man to do in a dead-and-alive place
en|like Barton? you don’t want to be the wife of a four-hundred-dollar clerk, and live
en|in part of a common little house, do you?” “Yes,” said Esther, showing her lover
en|a rapturous face whose attractiveness was not marred by a suspicion of shyness. “I do,
en|if Fred Macdonald is to be my husband.” “Then if either of us should have
en|a long illness, or if I should lose my position, we would have to depend
en|on your parents and mine,” said Fred. “Let us wait, then,” said Esther, “until you
en|can have saved something, before we are married.” “And be like Charley Merrick and Kate
en|Armstrong, who’ve been engaged for ten years, and are growing old and doleful about it.”
en|“_I’ll_ never grow old and doleful while waiting for _my_ lover to succeed,” said Esther,
en|in a tone which might have carried conviction with it had Fred been entirely in
en|a listening humor. But as Fred imagined himself in the position of the many unsuccessful
en|young men in Barton, and of the anxious-looking husbands who had once been as spirited
en|as himself, he fell into a frame of mind which was anything but receptive. In
en|his day-dreams marriage had seemed made up of many things beside the perpetual companionship of
en|Esther: it had among its very desirable components a handsome, well-furnished house, a carriage of
en|the most approved style, an elegant wardrobe for Esther, and one of faultless style for
en|himself, a prominent pew in church, and, not least of all, a sideboard which should
en|be better stocked than that of any of his friends. To banish these from his
en|mind for a moment, and imagine himself living in two or three rooms; cheapening meat
en|at the butcher’s; never driving out but when he could borrow somebody’s horse and antiquated
en|buggy; wearing a suit of clothes for two or three years in succession, while Esther
en|should spend hours in making over and over the dresses of her unmarried days; all
en|this made him almost deaf to Esther’s loyal words, and nearly oblivious to the fact
en|that the wisest and sweetest girl in Barton was resting within his arm. Suddenly he
en|aroused himself from his revery, and exclaimed, in a tone which Esther did not at
en|first recognize as his own, “Ettie, your ideas are honest and lofty, but you must
en|admit that I know best about matters of business. I can’t deliberately throw away everything
en|I have done, and form entirely different business connections. I’ve always regretted my promise to
en|stop drinking after our marriage; but I’ve trusted that you, with your unusual sense, would
en|see the propriety of absolving me from it.” Esther shrank away from Fred, and hid
