en|her face in her hands, whispering hoarsely, “I can’t. I can’t, and I never will.”
en|She dropped into a chair and burst into tears. Fred’s momentary expression of anger softened
en|into sorrow, but his business instinct did not desert him. “Ettie,” said he tenderly, “I
en|thought you trusted me.” “You _know_ I do, Fred,” said the weeping girl; “but my
en|lover and the Fred who drinks are two different persons, and I _can’t_ trust the
en|latter. Don’t think me selfish: be always your natural self, and there’s no poverty or
en|sorrow that I won’t endure to be always with you. Do you think I hope
en|to marry you for the sake of living in luxury, or that any pleasures that
en|money will buy will satisfy me any more than they do Nellie Wainright and Mr.
en|Moshier’s wife? Or do you, professing to love me, ask me to run even the
en|slightest risk of ever being as unhappy as the poor women we have been talking
en|about are with their husbands, who love them dearly? You _must_ keep that promise, or
en|I must love you apart from you--until you marry some one else! Even then I
en|could only stop, it seems to me, by stopping to live.” Fred’s face, while Esther
en|was speaking, was anything but comely to look upon, but his intended reply was prevented
en|by a violent knock at the door. Esther hurriedly dried her eyes, and prepared to
en|vanish, if necessary, while Fred regained in haste his ordinary countenance; then, as the servant
en|opened the door, the lovers heard a voice saying, “Is Fred Macdonald here? He must
en|come down to George Doughty’s right away. George is dying!” Fred gave Ettie a hasty
en|kiss and a conciliatory caress, after which he left the house at a lively run.
en|CHAPTER VII. SOME NATURAL RESULTS. George Doughty lay propped up in bed; standing beside him,
en|and clasping his hand tightly, was his wife; near him were his two oldest children,
en|seemingly as ignorant of what was transpiring as they were uncomfortable on account of the
en|peculiar influence which pervaded the room. On the other side of the bed, and holding
en|one of the dying man’s hands, knelt Parson Wedgewell; beside him stood the doctor; while
en|behind them both, near the door, and as nearly invisible as a man of his
en|size could be, was Squire Tomple. The Squire’s face and figure seemed embodiments of a
en|trembling, abject apology; he occasionally looked toward the door, as if to question that inanimate
en|object whether behind its broad front he, the Squire, might not be safe from his
en|own fears. It was very evident that the Squire’s conscience was making a coward of
en|him; but it was also evident, and not for the first time in the world’s
en|history, that cowardice is mightily influential in holding a coward to the ground that he
en|hates. Had any one spoken to him, or paid him the slightest attention, the Squire
en|would have felt better; nothing turns cowards into soldiers so quickly as the receipt of
en|a volley; but no such relief seemed at all likely to reach him. The doctor,
en|like a true man, having done all things, could only stand, and stand he did;
en|Parson Wedgewell, feeling that upon his own efforts with the Great Physician depended the sick
en|man’s future well-being, prayed silently and earnestly, raising his head only to search, through his
en|tears, the face of the patient for signs of the desired answer to prayer. Mrs.
en|Doughty was interested only in looking into the eyes too soon to close forever, and
en|the faces of the two children were more than a man could intentionally look upon
en|a second time. So when Doughty’s baby, who had been creeping about the floor, suddenly
en|beholding the glories of the great seal which depended from the Squire’s fob-chain, tried to
en|climb the leg of the storekeeper’s trousers, the Squire smiled, as a saint in extremity
en|might smile at the sudden appearance of an angel, and he stooped--no easy operation for
en|a man of Squire Tomple’s bulk--and, lifting the little fellow in his arms, put kisses
en|all over the tiny face, which, in view of the relations of cleanliness to attractiveness,
en|was not especially bewitching. A moment later, however, a muffled but approaching step brought back
en|to the Squire his own sense of propriety, and he dropped the baby just in
en|time to be able to give a hand to Fred Macdonald, as that young man
en|softly pushed open the door. The Squire’s face again became apologetic. “How did it happen?”
en|whispered Fred. “Why,” replied the Squire, “the doctor says it’s a galloping consumption; _I_ never
en|knew a thing about it. Doctor says it’s the quickest case he ever knew; he
en|never imagined anything was the matter with George. If _I’d_ known anything about it, I’d
en|have had the doctor attending him long ago; but George isn’t of the complaining kind.
en|The idea of a fellow being at work for me, and dying right straight along.
en|Why, it’s awful! He says he never knew anything about it himself, so I don’t
en|see how _I_ could. He was at the store up to four or five days
en|ago, then his wife came around one morning and told me that he didn’t feel
en|fit to work that day, but she didn’t say what the matter was. I’ve been
en|thinking, for two or three weeks, about giving him some help in the store; but
en|you know how business drives everything out of a man’s head. First I thought I’d
en|stay around the store myself evenings, and let George rest; but I’ve had to go
en|to lodge meetings and prayer meetings, and my wife’s wanted me to go out with
en|her, and so my time’s been taken up. Then I thought I’d get a boy,
en|and--well, I didn’t know exactly which to do; but if I’d known----” “But can’t something
en|be done to brace him up for a day or two?” interrupted Fred; “then I’ll
en|take him out driving every day, and perhaps he’ll pick up.” The Squire looked twenty
en|years older for a moment or two as he replied, “The doctor says he hasn’t
en|any physique to rally upon; he’s all gone, muscle, blood, and everything. It’s the queerest
en|thing I ever knew; he hasn’t had anything to do, these past few years, but
en|just what _I_ did when I was a young man.” The dying man turned his
en|eyes inquiringly, and asked in a very thin voice, “Isn’t Fred here?” Fred started from
en|the Squire’s side, but the storekeeper arrested his progress with both hands, and fixing his
en|eyes on Fred’s necktie, whispered, “You don’t think _I’m_ to blame, do you?” “Why--no--I don’t
en|see how, exactly,” said Fred, endeavoring to escape. “Fred,” whispered the Squire, tightening his hold
en|on the lapels of Fred’s coat, “tell _him_ so, won’t you? I’ll be your best
en|friend forever if you will; it’s dreadful to think of a man going up to
en|God with such an idea on his mind, even if it _is_ a mistake. Of
en|course, when he gets there he’ll find out he’s wrong, _if_ he is, as----” Fred
en|broke away from the storekeeper, and wedged himself between the doctor and pastor. Doughty withdrew
en|his wrist from the doctor’s fingers, extended a thin hand, and smiled. “Fred,” said he,
en|“we used to be chums when we were boys. I never took an advantage of
en|you, did I?” “Never,” said Fred; “and we’ll have lots of good times again, old
en|fellow. I’ve just bought the best spring wagon in the State, and I’ll drive you
en|all over the country when you get well enough.” George’s smile became slightly grim as
en|he replied, “I guess Barker’s hearse is the only spring wagon I’ll ever ride in
en|again, my boy.” “Nonsense, George!” exclaimed Fred heartily. “How many times have I seen you
en|almost dead, and then put yourself together again? Don’t you remember the time when you
en|gave out in the middle of the river, and then picked yourself up, and swam
en|the rest of the way? Don’t you remember the time we got snowed in on
en|Raccoon Mountain, and we both gave up and got ready to die, and how you
en|not only came to, but dragged me home besides? The idea of _you_ ever dying!
en|I wish you’d sent for me when you first took the silly notion into your
en|head.” Doughty was silent for a moment; his eyes brightened a little and a faint
en|flush came to his cheeks; he looked fondly at his wife, and then at his
en|children; he tried to raise himself in his bed; but in a minute his smile
en|departed, his pallor returned, and he said, in the thinnest of voices, “It’s no use,
en|Fred; in those days there was something in me to call upon at a pinch;
en|now there isn’t a thing. I haven’t any time to spare, Fred; what I want
en|to ask is, keep an eye on my boys, for old acquaintance’ sake. Their mother
en|will be almost everything to them, but she can’t be expected to know about their
en|ways among men. I want somebody to care enough for them to see that they
en|don’t make the mistakes I’ve made.” A sudden rustle and a heavy step was heard,
en|and Squire Tomple approached the bedside, exclaiming, “_I’ll_ do that!” “Thank you, Squire,” said George
en|feebly; “but you’re not the right man to do it.” “George,” said the Squire, raising
en|his voice, and unconsciously raising his hand, “I’ll give them the best business chances that
en|can be had; I can do it, for I’m the richest man in this town.”
en|“You gave _me_ the best chance in town, Squire, and this is what has come
en|of it,” said Doughty. The Squire precipitately fell back and against his old place by
en|the wall. Doughty continued, “Fred, persuade them--tell them that I said so--that a business that
en|makes them drink to keep up, isn’t business at all--it’s suicide. Tell them that their
en|father, who was never drunk in his life, got whisky to help him use more
en|of himself, until there wasn’t anything left to use. Tell them that drinking for strength
en|means discounting the future, and that discounting the future always means getting ready for bankruptcy.”
en|“I’ll do it, old fellow,” said Fred, who had been growing very solemn of visage.
en|“They shan’t ask you for any money, Fred, explained Doughty, when the Squire’s voice was
en|again heard saying, “And they shan’t refuse it from me.” “Thank you, Squire,” said George.
en|“I do think you owe it to them, but I guess they’ve good enough stuff
en|in them to refuse it.” “George,” said the Squire, again approaching the bedside, “I’m going
en|to continue your salary to your wife until your boys grow big enough to help
en|her. You know I’ve got plenty of money--’twon’t hurt me; for God’s sake make her
en|promise to take it.” “She won’t need it,” said Doughty. “My life’s insured.” “Then what
en|_can_ I do for her--for them--for you?” asked the Squire. “George, you’re holding your--sickness--against me,
en|and I want to make it right. I can’t say I believe I’ve done wrong
en|by you, but you think I have, and that’s enough to make me want to
en|restore good feeling between us before--in case anything should happen. Anything that money _can_ do,
en|it _shall_ do.” “Offer it to God Almighty, Squire, and buy my life back again,”
en|said Doughty. “If you can’t do that, your money isn’t good for anything in this
en|house.” The doctor whispered to his patient that he must not exert himself so much;
en|the Squire whispered to the doctor to know what else a man in his own
en|position could do? Fred Macdonald could think of no appropriate expression with which to break
en|the silence that threatened. Suddenly Parson Wedgewell raised his head, and said, “My dear young
en|friend, this is a solemn moment. There are others who know and esteem you, beside
en|those here present; have you no message to leave for them? Thousands of people rightly
en|regard you as a young man of high character, and your influence for good may
en|be powerful among them. I should esteem it an especial privilege to announce, in my
en|official capacity, such testimony as you may be moved to make, and as your pastor,
en|I feel like claiming this mournful pleasure as a right. What may I say?” “Say,”
en|replied the sick man, with an earnestness which was almost terrible in its intensity; “say
en|that whisky was the best business friend I ever found, and that when it began
en|to abuse me, no one thought enough of me to step in between us. And
en|tell them that this story is as true as it is ugly.” As Doughty spoke,
en|he had raised himself upon one elbow; as he uttered his last word, he dropped
en|upon his pillow, and passed into a land to which no one but his wife
en|manifested any willingness to follow him. CHAPTER VIII. AN ESTIMABLE ORGANIZATION CRITICISED. The funeral services
en|of George Doughty were as largely attended as the great temperance meeting had been, and
en|the attendants admitted--although the admission was not, logically, of particular force--that they received the worth
en|of their money. The pall-bearers, twelve in number, were all young men who had been
en|in the habit of drinking, but who had signed the pledge, some of them having
en|appended signatures to special pledges privately prepared on the evening before the service. The funeral
en|anthem was as doleful as the most sincere mourner could have wished, the music having
en|been composed especially for the occasion by the chorister of Mr. Wedgewell’s church. As for
en|the sermon, it was universally voted the most powerful effort that Parson Wedgewell had ever
en|made. Day and night had the good man striven with Doughty’s parting injunction, determined to
en|transmit the exact spirit of it, but horrified at its verbal form. At last he
en|honestly made George’s own words the basis of his whole sermon; his method being, first,
en|to show what would have been naturally the last words of a young man of
en|good birth and Christian breeding, and then presenting George’s moral legacy by way of contrast.
en|To point the moral without offending Squire Tomple’s pride, and without inflicting useless pain upon
en|the Squire’s sufficiently wounded heart, was no easy task; but the parson was not lacking
en|in tact and tenderness, so he succeeded in making of his sermon an appeal so
en|powerful and all-applicable that none of the hearers found themselves at liberty to search out
en|those to whom the sermon might seem personally addressed. Among the hearers was Mr. Crupp,
en|and no one seemed more deeply interested and affected. He followed the funeral cortege to
en|the cemetery; but, arrived there, he halted at the gate, instead of following the example
en|of the multitude by crowding as closely as possible to the grave. The final services
en|were no sooner concluded, however, than the object of Mr. Crupp’s unusual conduct became apparent
en|to one person after another, the disclosure being made to people in the order of
en|their earthly possessions. The parson was shocked at learning that Mr. Crupp was importuning every
en|man of means to take stock in a woolen mill, to be established at Barton;
en|but a whispered word or two from Crupp caused the parson to abate his displeasure,
en|and finally to stand near Crupp’s side and express his own hearty approbation of the
en|enterprise proposed. Then Mr. Crupp whispered a few words to Squire Tomple, and the Squire
en|subscribed a hundred shares at ten dollars each, information of which act was disseminated among
en|business men and well-to-do farmers by Parson Wedgewell with an alacrity which, had modern business
en|ideas prevailed at Barton, would have laid the parson open to a suspicion of having
en|accepted a few shares, to be paid for by his own influence. Then Deacon Jones
en|subscribed twenty shares, and Judge Macdonald, Fred’s father, promised to take fifty; Crupp’s name already
en|stood at the head of the list for a hundred. No stock-company had ever been
en|organized at Barton before, and the citizens had always manifested a laudable reluctance to allow
en|other people to handle their money; but this case seemed an exception to all others;
en|confidence in the enterprise was so powerfully expressed, alike by the mercantile community, the bar,
en|the church, and the unregenerate (the last-named class being represented by the ex-vender of liquors),
en|that people who had any money made haste to participate in what seemed to them
en|a race for wealth with the odds in everybody’s favor. Crupp neglected no one; he
en|scorned no subscription on account of its smallness; before he left the cemetery gate nearly
en|half the requisite capital had been pledged, and before he slept that night he found
en|it necessary to accept rather more than the twenty thousand dollars which, it had been
en|decided two days before, would be needed. Several days later a board of directors was
en|elected; two or three of the directors informally offered the superintendency of the mill to
en|Fred Macdonald, on condition that he would pledge himself to abstain from the use of
en|intoxicating beverage while he held the position, and then Fred was elected superintendent in regular
en|form and by unanimous vote of the board of directors. Great was the excitement in
en|Barton and the tributary country when it was announced that the mill needed no more
en|money, and that, consequently, no more stock would be issued. In that mysterious way in
en|which such things always happen, the secret escaped, and encountered every one, that his new
en|position would prevent Fred Macdonald from drinking; non-stockholders had then the additional grievance that they
en|had been deprived of taking any part in an enterprise for the good of a
en|fellow-man, and all because the rich men of the village saw money in it. None
en|of these injured ones dared to express their minds on this subject to Squire Tomple,
en|to whom so many of them owed money, or to Judge Macdonald, who, in his
en|family pride, would have laid himself liable to action by the grand jury, had any
en|one suggested that his oldest son had ever been in any danger of becoming a
en|drunkard. But to Mr. Crupp they did not hesitate to speak freely; Crupp owned no
en|mortgages, no total abstainers owed him money; besides, he not only was not a church
en|member, but he had been in that most infernal of all callings, rum-selling. So it
en|came to pass that when one day Crupp went into Deacon Jones’s store for a
en|dollar’s worth of sugar, and was awaiting his turn among a large crowd of customers,
en|Father Baguss constituted himself spokesman for the aggrieved faction, and said, “It ’pears to me,
en|Mr. Crupp, as if reformin’ was a payin’ business.” Crupp being human, was not saintly,
en|so he flushed angrily, and replied, “It _ought_ to be, if the religion you’re so
en|fond of is worth a row of pins; but I don’t know what you’re driving
en|at.” “Oh! of course you don’t know,” said Father Baguss; “but everybody else does. You
en|don’t expect to make any money out of that woolen mill, do you?” “Yes I
en|do, too,” answered Crupp quickly. “I’ll make every cent I can out of it.” “Just
en|so,” said Father Baguss, consoling himself with a bite of tobacco; “an’ them that’s borne
en|the burden and heat of the day can plod along and not make a cent
en|’xcept by the hardest knocks. I’ve been one of the Sons of Temperance ever since
en|I was converted, an’ that’s nigh onto forty year; I don’t see why I don’t
en|get _my_ sheer of the good things of this world.” “If you mean,” said Crupp,
en|with incomparable deliberation, “that my taking stock in the mill is a reward to me
en|for dropping the liquor business, you’re mightily mistaken. I’d have taken it all the same
en|if anybody had put me up to it when I was in the liquor business.”
en|“Yes,” sighed Father Baguss, “like enough you would; as the Bible says, ‘The children of
en|this world are wiser in their generation than the children of light.’ I can’t help
en|a-gettin’ mad, though, to think it has to be so.” Two or three unsuccessful farmers
en|lounging about the stove sighed sympathetically, but Crupp indulged in a sarcastic smile, and remarked,
en|“_I_ always supposed it was because the children of light had got their treasure laid
en|up in heaven, and were above such worldly notions.” The late sympathizers of Father Baguss
en|saw the joke, and laughed with unkind energy, upon which the good old man straightened
en|himself and exclaimed, “The children of the kingdom have to earn their daily bread, I
en|reckon; manna don’t fall nowadays like it used to do for the chosen people.” “Exactly,”
en|said Crupp, “and them that ain’t chosen people don’t pick up their dinners without working
en|for them either, without getting into jail for it. But, say! I didn’t come in
en|here to make fun of you, Father Baguss. If you want some of that mill
en|stock so bad, I’ll sell you some of mine--that is, if you’ll go into temperance
en|with all your might.” The old man seemed struck dumb for a moment but when
en|he found his tongue, he made that useful member make up for lost time. “Go
en|into temperance!” he shouted. “Did anybody ever hear the like of that? I that’s been
en|a “Son” more’n half my life; that’s spent a hundred dollars--yes, more--in yearly dues; that’s
en|been to every temperance meetin’ that’s ever been held in town, even when I’ve had
en|rheumatiz so bad I could hardly crawl; that kept the pledge even when I was
en|out in the Black Hawk War, where the doctors themselves said that I _ort_ to
en|have drank; that’s plead with drinkers, and been scoffed an’ reviled like my blessed Master
en|for my pains; that’s voted for the Maine Liquor Law; that’s been dead agin lettin’
en|Miles Dalling into the church because he brews beer for his own family drinkin’, though
en|he’s a good enough man every other way, as fur as I can see; I
en|that went to see every member of our church, an’ begged an’ implored ’em not
en|to sell our old meetin’-house to the feller that’s since turned it into a groggery;
en|I to be told by a feller like you, that’s got the guilt of uncounted
en|drunkards on your soul----” Crupp, with a very white face, advanced a step or two
en|toward the old man; but the participator in the Black Hawk War was not to
en|be frightened, especially when he was so excited as he was now; so he roared,
en|“Come on! come on! perhaps you want _my_ blood on your soul, with all the
en|others; but just let me tell you, it isn’t easy to get!” Crupp recovered himself
en|and replied, “Father Baguss, all that you’ve done is very well in its way, but
en|it wasn’t going into temperance. You’ve been a first-rate talker, I know, but talk isn’t
en|cider. Why, there’s been lots of men in my store after listenin’ to one of
en|your strong temperance speeches, and laughed about what they’ve heard. I’ve told them they ought
en|to be ashamed of themselves--don’t shake your head--I _have_, and all they’d say would be,
en|‘Talk don’t cost anything, Crupp.’ But if you’d followed up your tongue with your brains,
en|and most of all your pocket, not one of them chaps would have opened his
en|head about you.” “Money!” exclaimed the old man; “didn’t I tell you that division dues
en|alone had cost me more’n a hundred dollars; not to speak of subscriptions to public
en|meetin’s?” “And every cent that didn’t go to pay ‘division’ expenses, that is--for keeping a
en|lodge-room in shape for you to meet in, and such things--went to pay for more
en|talk. Did you Sons of Temperance ever _buy_ a man away from his whisky? It
en|_might_ have been done--done cheap too--in almost any week since I’ve been in Barton, by
en|helping down-hearted men along. Did you ever do it yourself?” Father Baguss was nonplussed for
en|a moment, noting which a bystander, also a Son of Temperance, came valiantly to the
en|rescue of his order, by exclaiming, “Tongues was made to use, and the better the
en|cause, the more it needs to be talked about.” “There’s no getting away from that,”
en|said Crupp. “Talk’s all right in its place; but when anybody’s sick in your family,
en|you don’t hire somebody to come in and talk him well, do you?” The auxiliary
en|replied by pressing perceptibly closer to the bale of blankets against which he had been
en|leaning, and Crupp was enabled to concentrate his attention upon Father Baguss. But the old
en|soldier had in his military days unconsciously acquired a tactical idea or two which were
en|frequently applicable in real life. One of them was that of flanking, and he straightway
en|attempted it by exclaiming, “I’d use money quick enough on drunkards, if I saw anybody
en|fit to use it on,” said he; “it would do my old soul good to
en|find a drinking man that I could be sure money would save. But they’re a
en|shiftless, worthless pack of shotes, all that I see of ’em. There _wuz_ a young
en|fellow--Lije Mason his name was--that I once thought seriously of doin’ somethin’ fur; but he
en|went an’ signed the pledge, an’ got along all right by himself.” “But there’s your
en|own neighbors, old Tappelmine and his family--they all drink; what have you done for ’em?”
en|asked Crupp. “A lot of Kentucky poor white trash!” exclaimed Father Baguss. “What _could_ anybody
en|do for ’em? Besides, they do for ’emselves; they’ve stole hams out of my smoke-house
en|more’n once, an’ they know _I_ know it, too.” “Poor white trash is sometimes converted
en|in church, isn’t it?” asked Mr. Crupp; “and what’s to keep poor white trash from
en|stopping drinking? what but a good, honest, religious, rum-hating neighbor that looks at ’em so
en|savagely and lets ’em alone so hard that they’d take pains to get drunk, just
en|to worry him? I know how you feel toward them; I _saw_ it once: one
en|Sunday I passed you on the road just opposite their place; you was in your
en|wagon takin’ your folks to church, and I--well, I was out trying to shoot a
en|wild turkey, which I mightn’t have been on a Sunday. They were all laughin’ and
en|cuttin’ up in the house--it’s seldom enough such folks get anything to laugh about--and I
en|could just _see_ you groan, and your face was as black as a thunder cloud,
en|and as savage as an oak knot soaked in vinegar. The old man came out
en|just then for an armful of wood, and nodded at you pleasant enough; but that
en|face of yours was too much for him, and pretty soon he looked as if
en|he’d have liked to throw a chunk of wood at your head. I’d have _done_
en|it, if I’d been him. The old man was awfully drunk when I came back
en|that way, two or three hours later. That was a pretty day’s work for a
en|Son of Temperance, wasn’t it--and Sunday, too?” The casing to Father Baguss’s conscience was not
en|as thick as that to his brain, and he was silent; perhaps the prospect of
en|getting some mill stock aided the good work in his heart. Crupp continued: “I’m a
en|‘Son’ myself, now, and I know what a man agrees to when he joins a
en|division. If you think you’ve lived up to it--you and the other members of the
en|Barton Division--I suppose you’ve a right to your opinion; but if my ideas, picked up
en|on both sides of the fence, are worth anything to you, they amount to just
en|this: the Sons of Temperance in this town haven’t done anything but help each other
en|not to get back into bad ways again, and to give a welcomin’ hand to
en|anybody that’s strong enough in himself to come into the division with you; and that
en|isn’t the spirit of the order.” Crupp got his sugar, and no one pressed him
en|to stay longer; but, as he slowly departed, as became a soldier who was not
en|retreating but only changing his base, Father Baguss followed him, touched his sleeve as soon
en|as he found himself outside the store door, and said, “Say, Crupp, I’ll try to
en|do something for Tappelmine, though I don’t know yet what it’ll be, an’ I don’t
en|care if you _do_ let me have about five sheers of that mill stock; I
en|s’pose you won’t want more than you paid for it?” CHAPTER IX. SOME VOLUNTEER SHEPHERDS.
en|The mail-stage did not make its appearance at the usual hour on the day following
en|Crupp’s conversation with Father Baguss, and during a lull in the desultory conversation which prevailed
en|among those who were waiting for the mail, the postmaster displayed at his window his
en|large, round face, devoid of its habitual jolly smile, and remarked, “Too bad about Wainright,
en|isn’t it?” “What’s that?” asked half a dozen at once. The postmaster looked infinitely more
en|important all in a second. It is but seldom in this world that a man
en|can tell a bit of news to an assembled crowd; and in an inland town,
en|before the day of the omnipresent telegraph pole, the chances were proportionately fewer than elsewhere.
en|The postmaster had a generous heart, however, and at the risk of losing his importance
en|he opened his treasure-house all at once: “He’s been pretty high on whiskey for two
en|or three days,” said he, “and they say he’s got snakes in his boots now;
en|anyhow, he’s made a sudden break for Louisville; he started on foot, an hour or
en|two ago, for Brown’s Landing, seven miles below here, to catch a down-river steamboat; he
en|was clear-headed enough to find out first that it wasn’t likely that the _Excellence_, that’s
en|about due, wouldn’t have any freight to stop for here. His wife’s half wild about
en|it, but there’s nothing the poor thing can do.” “Poor, misguided man!” sighed Parson Wedgewell,
en|who had arrived just in time to hear the story. “The ways of Providence are
en|undoubtedly wise, but they are indeed mysterious. Judging according to our finite capacities, it would
en|be natural to suppose that capabilities so unusual as those of Mr. Wainright would be
en|divinely guided.” “I saw him coming down the walk,” observed Squire Tomple, “and I thought
en|he looked rather peculiar, so I just stepped across the street; I don’t like to
en|get into a row with men in that fix.” “Of course getting into a row
en|was the only thing that could be done,” said Crupp, who had apparently been carefully
en|reading a posted notice of a sheriff’s sale. The Squire did not enjoy the tone
en|in which Crupp’s remark was delivered; but before he could reason with the new reformer,
en|the Reverend Timotheus Brown dashed into the fray in defense of a beloved idea, which
en|the rival pastor had seemed covertly to assail. “The reason such natures aren’t divinely guided,”
en|said he, in a voice which suggested nutmeg-graters to the acute sensibilities of Parson Wedgewell,
en|“is that they don’t implicitly submit themselves to the Divine will.” “A man can do
en|nothing unless the Spirit draw him,” said Parson Wedgewell valiantly. “That’s rather hard on a
en|fellow, though, isn’t it?” soliloquized Fred Macdonald. “Not a bit of it,” spoke out Father
en|Baguss, who had been scenting the battle from an inner room. “Bless the Lord! the
en|parables of the lost sheep that the shepherd left the rest of the flock to
en|look for, and the lost coin that the woman hunted for, wasn’t told for nothin’.
en|The Lord knows how to ’tend to his own business.” “And nobody else can do
en|a thing to help the Lord along, can he?” said Crupp, passing his arm through
en|the postmaster’s window, and extracting from his box a copy of the Louisville _Journal_ (then
en|the only paper of prominence in a large section of Western country); “all that men
en|have to do in such cases is just to talk.” Crupp departed, encountering on the
en|way the wide-open countenance of Tom Adams, who was waiting for Deacon Jones’s mail. The
en|two pastors preserved silence, that of Mr. Brown being extremely dignified, with a visible trace
en|of acerbity, while that of Mr. Wedgewell was strongly suggestive of mental unquiet. The distribution
en|of the small mail, which had arrived soon after the conversation began, gave everybody an
en|excuse to depart--an excuse of which most of them availed themselves at once, Squire Tomple
en|having first changed the direction of the conversation by inquiring particularly of Father Baguss as
en|to the number and probable weight of the porkers which the old man was fattening
en|for the winter market. The subject lasted only until the two men reached the door,
en|however, and then each sympathized with the other over the wounds received at the hands,
en|or tongue, of the unsentimental and irreligious Crupp. Yet the more they talked of Crupp,
en|the less they seemed to realize their pain. Tom Adams went straight to his employer’s
en|store, and exclaimed, not in his usual ingenuous manner, “Deacon, old Berry won’t take that
en|load of bricks unless he gets ’em right off; I guess I’ll take ’em right
en|out to him. It’s a long trip, but there’s three hours yet ’fore dark.” “Be
en|sure you do, then, Thomas,” said the deacon. Tom was soon in his wagon, and
en|going toward the brick-yard at a livelier rate than was consistent with the proper care
en|of horses with a long, heavy pull before them. The bricks were loaded with apparent
en|regard to count, but not in good order, and, as Tom followed the road to
en|old Berry’s, he soliloquized: “I ort to be able to ketch him after I deliver
en|the bricks, but what in thunder am I to say to him? Like enough he’ll
en|knock me down if I don’t look out. That’s just the notion, I _de_-clare! I
en|can knock _him_ down, and put him right in the wagon and bring him back;
en|the joltin’ would fetch him to and clear his head, like it’s done mine often
en|enough when I’ve been in his fix. But, hang it, what a ridick’lus goose-chase it
en|does look like!” Meanwhile the Reverend Timotheus Brown had limped down the main street, looking
en|a little more unapproachable than usual. As he reached the edge of the town, however,
en|where there began the low plain which led to the river, he quickened his pace
en|somewhat, and he did not stop until he reached the river. Upon a raft sat
en|a man fishing, and near by a canoe was tied; in this latter the preacher
en|seated himself, having first untied it. “Hello, there! What are you a-doin’ with my dug-out?”
en|shouted the fisherman. “The Lord hath need of it!” roared the old divine, picking up
en|the paddle. “Well, I’ll be----!” exclaimed the man; “if that _ain’t_ the coolest! The Lord’ll
en|get a duckin’, I reckon, for that’s the _wobbliest_ canoe. I don’t know, though; the
en|old fellow paddles as if he were used to it.” Away down the river went
en|the Reverend Timotheus; at the same time Fred Macdonald, on horseback, hailed the ferry-boat, crossed
en|the river, and galloped down the opposite bank, and Crupp, a half an hour later,
en|might have been seen lying on his oars in a skiff in a shallow a
en|mile above the town, waiting to board the _Excellence_, as she came down the stream.
en|“’Pears to me preachers are out for a walk to-day,” said one old lady to
en|another across a garden fence, in one edge of the town. “I saw Mr. Brown
en|’way down the street ever so far to-day, an’ now here’s Brother Wedgewell ’way out
en|here. I thought like enough he was goin’ to call, but he went straight along
en|an’ only bowed, awful solemn.” Parson Wedgewell certainly walked very fast, and the more ground
en|he covered the more rapidly his feet moved, and not his feet only. In long
en|stretches of road shut in by forest trees he found himself devoid of a single
en|mental restraint, and he thought aloud as he walked. “Rebuked by a sinner! O God!
en|with my whole heart I have sought thee, and thou hast instead revealed thyself not
en|only unto babes and sucklings, but unto one who is certainly not like unto one
en|of these little ones. Teach me thy will, for verily in written books I fear
en|I have found it not. What if the boat reaches the landing before I do,
en|and this lost sheep escapes me? Father in Heaven, the shepherd is astray in his
en|way, even as the sheep is; but O thou! who didst say that the race
en|is not to the swift, nor the battle to the strong, make the feeble power
en|of man to triumph over great engines and the hurrying of mighty waters. Fulfill thy
en|promise, O God, for the sake of the soul thou hast committed to my charge!”
en|Then, like a man who believed in helping his own prayers along, the parson snatched
en|off his coat and hat and increased his speed. He was far outside of his
en|own parish, for most of his congregation were townsmen, and the old pastor knew no
en|more of the geography of the country about him than he did of Chinese Tartary.
en|He had taken what was known as the “River Road,” and thus far his course
en|had been plain; now, however, he reached a place where the road divided, and which
en|branch to take he did not know. Ordinary sense of locality would have taught him
en|in an instant, but the parson had no such sense; there was no house in
en|sight at which he could ask his way, and, to add to his anxiety, the
en|_Excellence_ came down the river to his left and rear, puffing and shrieking as if
en|the making of hideous noises was the principal qualification of a river steamer. The old
en|man fell upon his knees, raised his face and hands toward heaven, and exclaimed, “The
en|hosts of hell are pressing hard, O God! Thou who didst guide thy chosen people
en|with a pillar of fire, show now to thy unworthy servant that thou art God!”
en|What the parson saw he never told, but he sprang to his feet and went
en|down the left-hand road at a lively run, a moment after Tom Adams, half a
en|mile in the rear, had shaded his eyes and exclaimed, “Blamed if there isn’t a
en|feller a-prayin’ right out in the road; if he wants anything _that_ bad, I hope
en|he’ll get it. Travel, Selim--_get_ up, Bill!--let’s see who he is.” CHAPTER X. BRINGING HOME
en|THE SHEEP. Speaking after the manner of the flesh, the Reverend Timotheus Brown had found
en|only plain sailing on the river; spiritually, he had a very different experience. “As stubborn
en|as a mule” was the most common of the current estimates of Pastor Brown’s character;
en|and if the conscientious old preacher had ever personally heard this opinion of himself, the
en|verbal expression thereof would have given him but slight annoyance, compared with that which he
en|experienced from his own inner man as he paddled down the stream. To forcibly resist
en|something so satisfied the strongest demand of his nature that neither shortening breath nor blistering
en|hands caused him to slacken the speed with which he forced his paddle against the
en|water. But another contest was going on, and in this the consistent theologian was not
en|so triumphant as he liked always to be. Harry Wainright was one of the ungodly;
en|that he owned (and frequently occupied) a high-priced pew in Mr. Brown’s own church was
en|only another reason why the preacher should quote concerning him, “He that being often reproved
en|hardeneth his neck----”--what if the conclusion of the same passage--“shall suddenly be destroyed, and that
en|without remedy,” should apply? What could prevent its doing so, if Wainright had fulfilled the
en|description in the first half? Had not the same God inspired the whole passage? If
en|so, what right had any man, least of all a minister of the Gospel, to
en|try to set at naught the Divine will? Harry Wainright was, according to the decrees
en|of an unchangeable God, one of the lost--as much so as if he were already
en|in the bottomless pit. And still the old man’s paddle flew; once on the trip
en|he had felt as if the weakness of the arm of flesh would decide the
en|case for him, and in favor of the Word whose expounder he was; he found
en|himself wishing that it might, so that he could feel that although God had overruled
en|him, he might have comfort in the assurance that he had not proved indifferent to
en|his sudden emotion of yearning for his fellow man. But that mysterious physical readjustment, known
en|in animals as “second breath,” came to the rescue of his fainting frame, and then
en|it seemed as if no watery torrent could prevail against the force of his arm.
en|Oh! if he might but talk to some one of the fathers of the church;
en|that he might be, even for ten minutes, back in his own library! But no
en|father of the church resided along the Reverend Brown’s nautical course, nor was there a
en|theological library nearer than his own, and there he was, actually bent upon saving one
en|whom the Eternal pronounced lost! Lost? Hold! “For the Son of Man is come into
en|the world to save them that are lost.” If Christ had a right to save
en|the lost, had not an ambassador of Christ the same privilege? was not an ambassador
en|one who stood in the place--who fulfilled the duties--of an absent king? “Glory be to
en|God on high!” shouted the Reverend Timotheus, and the dense woods echoed back “God on
en|high!” as the old man, forty years a conscientious pastor, but only that instant converted
en|to Christianity, drove his paddle into the water with a force that nearly threw the
en|canoe into the air. As for Parson Wedgewell, whom we left arising from his knees
en|after asking information from his Divine guide, he found himself upon the right road. The
en|river was nearer than he had dared to hope; a run of half a mile
en|brought him into a clearing, in which stood Brown’s warehouse, near the river. The _Excellence_
en|had just put her nose against the bank, and the clerk at the warehouse was
en|tired of wondering why Fred Macdonald, on the opposite bank, was shouting so impatiently to
en|the ferryman, and why an old man in a canoe should be coming down the
en|river at the rate of fifty-paddle strokes per minute, when he saw Parson Wedgewell, coatless,
en|hatless, with open shirt, disordered hair, and face covered with dirt deposited just after an
en|unlucky stumble, come flying along the road, closely followed by Tom Adams, who was lashing
en|his horses furiously. A happy inspiration struck the clerk; he shouted “Horse thief!” and seized
en|the parson, and instantly received a blow under the chin which rendered him inactive and
en|despondent for the space of half an hour. The parson saw the gang-plank shoved out;
en|he saw Harry Wainright step aboard; he saw the Rev. Timotheus jump from his canoe
en|into water knee deep, dash up the plank, and throw his arm over Harry Wainright’s
en|shoulder; but only a second or two elapsed before Parson Wedgewell monopolized the runaway’s other
en|side, and then, as the three men stared at each other, neither one speaking a
en|word, and the two pastors bursting into tears, Tom Adams hurried aboard, and exclaimed, “Mr.
en|Wainright, Mrs. Wainright is particular anxious to see you this evenin’, for somethin’, I don’t
en|know what, an’ I hadn’t time to get any sort of a carriage for fear
en|I’d lose the boat; but there’s good springs to the seat of the brick-yard wagon,
en|an’ a new sheep-skin besides.” No other words coming to Tom’s mind, he abruptly walked
en|forward muttering, “That’s the cock-an’-bullest yarn I ever _did_ tell; I _knew_ I wouldn’t know
en|what to say.” As Tom meditated, he heard one “roustabout” say to another, “I say,
en|Bill, you know that feller that used to sell such bully whiskey in Barton? Well,
en|he’s around there on the guards, dancin’ like a lunatic. I shouldn’t wonder if that’s
en|what come of swearin’ off drinkin’.” “Mighty unsafe perceedin’,” replied Bill, eyeing Crupp suspiciously. Harry
en|Wainright made not the slightest objection to going back home, and he acted very much
en|like a man who was glad of the company in which he found himself. The
en|divine of the canoe looked at his blistered hands, and paid the resuscitated clerk to
en|send the boat back by the first steamer. While Fred Macdonald was crossing the river,
en|Tom Adams kindly drove back the road and recovered Parson Wedgewell’s coat and hat, and
en|the parson accepted the hospitalities of the boat to the extent of water, soap, and
en|towel. He attempted to make his peace with the injured clerk; but that functionary, having
en|already interviewed Tom Adams, insisted that no apology was necessary, and asked the old gentleman
en|in what church he preached. As the party started back, they saw, coming through a
en|cross-road, a buggy violently driven, and containing two men--who proved to be Squire Tomple and
en|Father Baguss--in a vehicle belonging to the latter; their air of having merely happened there
en|deceived no one, least of all Harry Wainright himself. Father Baguss did not live in
en|town, nor within four miles of it; but when Squire Tomple suggested that he would
en|beg a ride back in Tom Adams’s wagon, Father Baguss objected, and remarked that he
en|guessed he had business in town himself; so the Squire retained his seat, and Father
en|Baguss fell in behind the wagon as decorously as if he was taking part in
en|a funeral procession. Behind them came Fred Macdonald, who had good excuse to gallop back
en|to the peculiar attraction that awaited him in Barton, but preferred to remain in his
en|present company. As the party approached the town, Tom Adams considerately drove through the darkest
en|and most unfrequented streets, and stopped as near as possible to Wainright’s house. Wainright, politely
en|declining any escort, walked quietly home. Father Baguss stood up in his buggy, with his
en|hand to his ear, in the original position of attention: suddenly he exclaimed, “There! I
en|heard his door shut: _now_, brethren.” And Father Baguss started the doxology. “Praise God from
en|whom all blessings flow,” and the glorious harmonies of the old choral were proof even
en|against the tremendous but discordant notes which Tom Adams, with the most honorable intentions, interjected
en|in rapid succession. Then the party broke up. The two pastors escorted each other home
en|alternately and several times in succession, during which apparently meaningless proceeding they learned, each from
en|the other, how much of good intent had been stifled in both of them for
en|lack of prompt application. Crupp and Tomple talked but little, and no “Imaginary Conversation” would
en|be at all likely to reproduce what they said. Father Baguss made the whole air
en|between Barton and his own farm redolent of camp-meeting airs, and Fred Macdonald heard in
en|Parson Wedgewell’s parlor something sweeter than all the music ever written. As for Tom Adams,
en|he jogged slowly toward his employer’s stables, repeating to himself, “The bulliest spree I ever
en|went on--the _very_ bulliest!” CHAPTER XI. DOCTORS AND BOYS. Here were two elements of Barton
en|society with which Mr. Crupp had not been so successful as he had hoped; these
en|were the doctors, and that elastic body known as “the boys.” Individually, the physicians had
en|promised well at first; all of them but one were members of the Barton Division
en|of the Sons of Temperance, and the Division rooms afforded the only floor upon which
en|Dr. White, the allopathist, Dr. Perry, the homeopathist, and Dr. Pykem, the water-cure physician, ever
en|could meet amicably, for they belonged to separate churches. Old Dr. Matthews, who had retired
en|from practice, was not a “Son,” only because he was a conscientious opponent of secret
en|societies; but he had signed every public pledge ever circulated in Barton, and he had
en|never drunk a drop of liquor in his life. All the physicians freely admitted to
en|Mr. Crupp that alcohol was a never-failing cause of disease, or at least of physical
en|deterioration; all declared that no class of maladies were so incurable, and so depressing to
en|the spirits of the medical practitioner, as those to which habitual drinkers, even those who
en|were never drunk, were subject; but--they really did not see what more they, the physicians
en|of Barton, could do than they were already doing. Crupp discussed the matter with Parson
en|Wedgewell, and the parson volunteered to preach a sermon to physicians from the text, “Give
en|wine unto those that be of heavy hearts,” a text which had suggested itself to
en|him, or, rather, had been providentially suggested to him on the occasion of his very
en|first interview with Crupp, and which was outlined in his mind in a manner suggestive
en|of delightful subtleties and a startling application. But when Crupp sounded the doctors as to
en|whether such a discourse would be agreeable, Dr. White said he would be glad to
en|listen to the eloquent divine; but he was conscientiously opposed to appearing, even by the
en|faintest implication, to admit that the homeopathist was a physician at all. Dr. Perry felt
en|his need, as a partaker in the fall of Adam, to being preached to from
en|any portion of the inspired Word; but he could not sit in an audience to
en|which such a humbug as Pykem could be admitted in an official capacity; while Dr.
en|Pykem said that he would rejoice to encourage the preacher by his presence, if he
en|thought any amount of preaching would do any good to a remorseless slaughterer like White,
en|or an idiotic old potterer like Perry. Then Mr. Crupp tried another plan: he himself
en|organized a meeting in which the exercises were to consist of short addresses upon the
en|physical bearing of intemperance, the addresses to be made by “certain of our fellow-citizens who
en|have had many opportunities for special observation in this direction.” Even then Drs. White and
en|Perry objected to sitting on the same platform with Dr. Pykem, who had never attended
en|any medical school of any sort, and who would probably say something utterly ridiculous in
en|support of his own senseless theories, and thus spoil the effect of the physiological facts
en|and deductions which Drs. Perry and White each admitted that the other might be intellectually
en|capable of advancing. Crupp arranged the matter amicably, however, by having Pykem make the first
en|address, during which the other two physicians were to occupy back seats, where they might,
en|while unobserved, take notes of such of Pykem’s heresies as they might deem it necessary
en|to combat: he further arranged that, immediately after Pykem had concluded, he was to be
en|called away to a patient, provided for the occasion. Still more--and great would have been
en|the disgust of White and Perry had they known of it--Crupp laid so plainly before
en|Pykem the necessities of the community, and the duty, not only Christian, but of the
en|simplest manliness, also, that men of any intelligence owed to their fellow-men, that Pykem, who
en|with all his hobbies was a man of Christian belief and humane heart, confined himself
en|solely to the preventive efficacy of external applications of water, not unmixed with soap, in
en|the case of persons who felt toward alcohol a craving which they could not logically
en|explain; he thus delivered an address which might, with cause, be repeated in every community
en|in the United States. Then Dr. Perry, whose forte was experimental physiology, read whole tables
en|of statistics based upon systematic observations; and Dr. White unrolled and explained some charts and
en|plates of various internal organs, naturally unhandsome in themselves, which had been injured by alcohol.
en|It was declared by close observers that for a few days after this meeting the
en|demand for sponges and toilet soap exceeded the experience of the old and single apothecary
en|of the village, and that liquor-sellers looked either sober or savage, according to their respective
en|natures. But the boys! Crupp found himself in time really disposed to ask Pastors Wedgewell
en|and Brown whether there wasn’t Scriptural warrant for the supposition that Job obtained his sons
en|by marrying a widow with a grown-up family. “The boys” numbered about a hundred specimens,
en|ranging in age from fourteen years to forty; no two were alike in disposition, as
en|Crupp had long known; they came from all sorts of peculiar social conditions that warred
en|against their physical and moral well-being; some of them seemed wholly corrupt, and bent upon
en|corrupting others; many more exhibited a faculty for promising which could be matched in magnitude
en|only by their infirmity of performance. By a vigorous course of individual exhortation, the burden
en|of which was that everybody knew they drank because they were too cowardly to refuse,
en|and that nobody despised them so heartily as the very men who sold them the
en|rum, Crupp lessened the number of drinking boys by about one-fourth, thus rescuing those who
en|were easiest to save and most worth saving, but the remainder made as much trouble
en|as the collective body had done. Crupp scolded, pleaded, and argued; he hired some boys
en|to drop liquor for at least a stated time; he importuned some of the more
en|refined citizens to interest themselves socially in certain boys; he lent some of these boys
en|money with which to buy clothing which would bring their personal appearance up to the
en|Barton standard of respectability, and he covertly excited some of the merchants up to a
en|genuine interest in certain boys, by persuading them to sell to said boys coats, boots,
en|and hats on credits nominally short. He enjoyed the hearty co-operation of the village pastors,
en|all of whom preached sermons to young men and to parents; but his principal practical
en|assistance came, quite unexpectedly, from old Bunley. Bunley had not yet succeeded in finding anything
en|to do, and, as he had on his hands all of his time which was
en|not needed at the family woodpile, he went around talking to the boys. Bunley had
en|been, according to the Barton classification, a “boy” himself; he had drunk in a not
en|remote day with any boy who invited him; he knew more jolly songs than any
en|other half dozen inebriates in the village, and was simply oppressed with the load of
en|good (bad) stories which he never tired of telling; he had been always ready to
en|play cards with any boy, and had come to be regarded, among the youngsters, as
en|“the best fellow in the village.” Now that he had reformed, his success in reforming
en|boys was simply remarkable--so much so that Parson Wedgewell began to tremble over the thought
en|that Bunley, by the present results of the experience of his sinful days, might demonstrate,
en|beyond the hope of refutation, the dreadful proposition that it was better that a man
en|should be a sinner in his youth, so as to know how to be a
en|saint when he became old. This idea Parson Wedgewell laid, with much trepidation, before the
en|Reverend Timotheus Brown, and the two old saints and new friends had a delightfully doleful
en|time on their knees over it, until there occurred to the Reverend Timotheus Brown a
en|principle which he proceeded to formulate as follows: The greater the capacity of a misguided
en|faculty for evil, the greater the good the same faculty may accomplish when in its
en|normal condition. To be sure, the discovery was not original with him; the same statement
en|had been made by peripatetic phrenologists at Barton; indeed, it was visible, to one who
en|could read rather than merely repeat words, in every chapter of the Bible so dear
en|to this good old man; but the illusion under which Parson Brown was allowed to
en|labor worked powerfully for his own good and for that of the community, for from
en|that time forth both he and Parson Wedgewell displayed their greatest earnestness in work with
en|cases apparently the most hopeless. These they found among “the boys,” and harder work no
en|reformer ever laid out for himself. The ingenuity, the persistence, the determined brutality of some
en|of the boys, the logical acuteness displayed in varied fits of deception, only stimulated the
en|old man to greater industry, and slowly, after hard work, often after work that seemed
en|more like hard fighting, but yet surely, Parson Brown reformed one after another of several
en|hard cases. The villagers, most of whom considered that their whole duty consisted in critical
en|observation, applauded handsomely, and Bunley was astonished, and felt considerably mortified at the marked success
en|of his new rival, while Parson Wedgewell found it necessary to pray earnestly that unchristian
en|jealousy might be banished from his own mind. But to Parson Brown the greatest triumph
en|occurred when Crupp--Crupp, the literalist, the hard-headed, the man who trusted in the arm of
en|flesh, the man of action, he who slightingly received any suggestions of special thank-offerings of
en|prayer for special services received--Crupp came to him by night--it reminded Parson Brown of Nicodemus--and
en|exclaimed, “It’s no use, Parson; I’ve done my best on Frank Pughger, but he’s a
en|goner if God don’t put in a special hand. I’ll turn him over to you,
en|I guess.” CHAPTER XII. TWO SIDES OF A CLOUD. The holy hilarity which Father Baguss
en|enjoyed on his way home, after having assisted in bringing Harry Wainright back, did not
en|depart with the shades of night. The old man was out of bed at his
en|usual hour, and he took his spiritual songs to the barn with him, to the
en|astonishment of his mild-eyed cows and quick-eared horses; and when his drove of porkers demanded
en|their morning meal with the vocal power peculiar to a chorus of swine, the old
en|man defiantly jumped an occasional octave, and made the spiritual songs dominate over the physical.
en|He seemed _so_ happy that his single hired man could not resist the temptation of
en|asking for an increase of pay; but the sobriety to which this interruption and its
en|consequent refusal reduced Father Baguss was of only temporary duration, and the broken strain was
en|resumed with renewed energy. The ecstasy lasted into and through the old man’s matutinal repast,
en|and manifested itself by an occasional hum through the good man’s nose, which did the
en|duty ordinarily performed by a mouth which was now busied about other things; it caused
en|Father Baguss to read a glorious psalm as he officiated at the family altar after
en|breakfast; it made itself felt half way through the set prayer which the old farmer
en|had delivered every morning for forty years; but it seemed suddenly to depart as its
en|whilom possessor uttered the petition, “May we impart to others of the grace with which
en|thou hast visited us so abundantly.” For the Tappelmines had come suddenly into Father Baguss’s
en|mind, and as that receptacle was never particularly crowded, the Tappelmines made themselves very much
en|at home there. The prayer having ended, the old man loitered about the house instead
en|of going directly to the “clearing,” in which he had been getting out some oak
en|fence-rails; he stared out of the window, walked up and down the kitchen with his
en|hands in his pockets, lit a pipe, relit it half a dozen times at two
en|minute intervals, sighed, groaned, and at length strode across the room like a bandit coming
en|upon the boards of a theater, seized his hat, and started for the Tappelmine domicile.
en|As he plodded along over the rough road, he had two very distinct ideas in
en|his mind: one was, that he hadn’t the slightest notion of what to say to
en|Tappelmine; the other, and stronger, was, that it would be a relief to him to
en|discover that Tappelmine was away from home, or even sick in bed--yes, or even drunk.
en|But this hope was of very short duration, for soon the old man heard the
en|Tappelmine axe, and, as he rounded the corner of the miserable house, he saw Tappelmine
en|himself--a tall, gaunt figure in faded homespun, torn straw hat, and a tangled thicket of
en|muddy-gray hair. The face which Tappelmine turned, as he heard the approaching footsteps, was not
en|one to warm the heart of a man inspired only by an unwelcome sense of
en|duty; it was thin, full of vagrant wrinkles; the nose had apparently started in different
en|directions, and each time failed to return to its original line; the eyes were watery
en|and colorless, and the lips were thin and drawn into the form of a jagged
en|volcano crater. “The idee of doin’ anything for such!” exclaimed Father Baguss under his breath.
en|“O Lord! _you_ put me up to this here job--unless it was all Crupp’s work;
en|now see me through!” Then he said, “How are you, neighbor?” “Oh! off an’ on,
en|’bout as usual,” said Tappelmine, with a look which seemed to indicate that his usual
en|condition was not one upon which he was particularly to be felicitated. “How’d your crop
en|turn out?” asked Father Baguss, well knowing that “crop” was a terribly sarcastic word to
en|apply to the acre or two of badly cultivated corn which Tappelmine had planted, but
en|yet feeling a frantic need of talking against time. “Well, not over’n above good,” said
en|Tappelmine, as impervious to the innocent sarcasm as he would have been to anything but
en|a bullet or a glass of whiskey. “I dunno what would have ’come of us
en|ef I hadn’t knocked over a couple of deer last week.” “You might have given
en|a hint to your neighbors, if worst had come to worst,” suggested Father Baguss, perceiving
en|a gleam of light, but not so delighted over it as a moment or two
en|before he had expected to be. “Nobody’d have stood by an’ seen you starve.” “Glad
en|you told me,” said Tappelmine, abruptly raising his axe, and starting two or three large
en|chips in quick succession. The light seemed suddenly to be departing, and Father Baguss made
en|a frantic clutch at it. “You needn’t have waited to be told,” said he. “You
en|know well enough we’re all human bein’s about here.” “Well,” said Tappelmine, leaning on his
en|axe, and taking particular care not to look into his neighbor’s eye, “I used to
en|borry a little somethin’--corn, mebbe, or a piece of meat once in a while; but
en|folks didn’t seem over an’ above glad to lend ’em, an’ I’m one of the
en|kind of fellows that can take a hint, I am.” “That was ’cause you never
en|said a word ’bout payin’ back--leastways, you didn’t at _our_ house.” Tappelmine did not reply,
en|except by looking sullen, and Father Baguss continued: “Besides, it’s kinder discouragin’ to lend to
en|a feller that gets tight a good deal--gets tight sometimes, anyhow; it’s hard enough to
en|get paid by folks that always keep straight.” As Tappelmine could say nothing to controvert
en|this proposition, he continued to look sullen, and Father Baguss, finding the silence insupportably annoying,
en|said rather more than he had intended to say. There are natures which, while containing
en|noble qualities, are most awkward expositors of themselves, and that of Baguss was one of
en|this sort. Such people are given to action which is open to criticism on every
en|side; yet, in spite of their awkwardnesses, they find in their weakness the source of
en|whatever strength they discover themselves to be possessed of. Father Baguss was one of this
en|special division of humanity; but--perhaps for his own good--he was unconscious of his strength and
en|painfully observant of his weakness. Yet he continued as follows: “Look here, Tappelmine, I came
en|over here on purpose to find out if I could do anything to help you
en|get into better habits. You don’t amount to a row of pins as things are
en|now, and I don’t like it; it’s throwed up to me, because I’m your neighbor,
en|and there’s folks that stick to it that _I’m_ to blame. I don’t see how;
en|but if there’s any cross layin’ around that fits my shoulders, I s’pose I ought
en|to pick it up an’ pack it along. Now, why in creation don’t you give
en|up drinkin,’ an’ go to church, an’ make a crop, an’ do other things like
en|decent folks do? You’re bigger’n I am, an’ stouter, an’ your farm’s as good as
en|mine if you’d only work it. Now why you don’t do it, I don’t see.”
en|“Don’t, eh?” snarled Tappelmine, dropping his axe, and leaning against the house with folded hands.
en|“Well, ’cause I hain’t got any plow, nor any harrow, nor but one hoss, nor
en|rails enough to keep out cattle, nor seed-corn or wheat, nor money to buy it
en|with, nor anything to live on until the crop’s made, nor anything to prevent the
en|crop when it’s made from being grabbed by whoever I owe money to; _that’s_ why
en|I don’t make a crop. An’ I don’t go to church, ’cause I hain’t got
en|any clothes excep’ these ’uns that I’ve got on, an’ my wife’s as bad off
en|as _I_ be. An’ I don’t give up drinkin’, ’cause drinkin’ makes me feel good,
en|an’ the only folks I know that care anything for me drink too. You fellers
en|that only drink on the sly----” “I never touched a drop in all my life!”
en|roared Father Baguss. “That’s right,” said Tappelmine; “stick to it; there’s some that’ll believe that
en|yarn. But what I was goin’ to say was, folks that drink on the sly
en|know it’s comfortin’, an’ I don’t see what they go a-pokin’ up fellers that does
en|it fair an’ square for.” Father Baguss groaned, and some influence--the old man in later
en|days laid it upon the arch-enemy of souls--suggested to him the foolishness of having gone
en|into so great an operation without first counting the cost; hadn’t the great Founder of
en|the old man’s religious faith enjoined a counting of the cost of any enterprise before
en|entering upon it? Father Baguss wished _that_ chapter of Holy Writ might have met his
en|eye that morning at the family altar; but it had not, and, worse yet, Tappelmine
en|was becoming wide awake and excited. It was not what the drunkard had said about
en|drinking or church-going that troubled this would-be reformer; Tappelmine’s outline of his material condition was
en|what annoyed Father Baguss; for, in spite of an occasional attempt to mentally allay his
en|fears by falling back upon prayer, the incentive with which he had called upon Tappelmine
en|had taken strong hold of his conscience, and persisted in making its influence felt. Plows
en|and prayers, harrows and hopes, seed-corn and the seed sown by the wayside mixed themselves
en|inextricably in his mind, as parallels often do when men dream, or when they are
en|confronted by an emergency beyond the control of their own intellects. The old man prayed
en|silently and earnestly for relief, and his prayer was answered in a manner not entirely
en|according to his liking, for he felt moved to say, “_I’ll_ lend you seed, if
en|you’ll go to work an’ put it right in, an’ I’ll lend you a plow
en|and a team to break up the ground with--I mean, I’ll hire ’em to you,
en|an’ agree to buy your crop at rulin’ price, an’ pay you the difference in
en|cash.” “That sounds somethin’ like,” remarked Tappelmine, thrusting his hands into his trowsers’ pockets, and
en|making other preparations for a business talk; “but,” he continued, “what am I to live
en|on along till harvest? ’Tain’t even winter yet.” Father Baguss groaned, and asked, “What was
en|you a-goin’ to live on if I hadn’t offered seed and tools, Tappelmine?” “The Lord
en|knows,” answered the never-do-well, with unimpeachable veracity. “Then,” said the old farmer, “I guess he
en|knows what you’ll do in t’other case. You can work, I reckon. _I_ hain’t got
en|much to do, but you can do it, at whatever prices is goin’, an’ that’ll
en|help you get work of other folks; nobody can say I get stuck on the
en|men I hire. So they’re generally glad enough to hire ’em themselves.” Tappelmine did not
en|seem overjoyed at his prospects, but he had the grace to say that they were
en|better than he had expected. Father Baguss went home, feeling but little more comfortable than
en|when he had started on his well-intended mission. Tappelmine sauntered into his own cabin, wondering
en|how much of the promised seed-corn and wheat he could smuggle into town and trade
en|for whiskey; but he was rather surprised to have his wife, a short, thin, sallow,
en|uninteresting-looking woman, who had been listening at the broken window, approach him, throw her arms
en|about his neck, and exclaim, “Now, old man, we can be respectable, can’t we? The
en|chance has been a long time a-comin’, but we’ve got it now.” The surprise was
en|too great for Tappelmine, and he spent the remainder of the day in nursing his
en|knee on the single hearthstone of his mansion. He was not undisturbed, however, and as
en|men of his mental caliber hate persistent reason even worse than they do work, Mrs.
en|Tappelmine not only coaxed her lord into resolving to be respectable, but allowed that gentleman
en|to persuade himself that he had formed the resolution of his own accord. CHAPTER XIII.
en|A PHENOMENON IN EMBRYO. The superintendency of the Mississippi Valley Woolen Mills was a position
en|which exactly suited Fred Macdonald, and it gave him occasion for the expenditure of whatever
en|superfluous energy he found himself possessed of, yet it did not engross his entire attention.
en|The faculty which the busiest of young men have for finding time in which to
en|present themselves, well clothed and unbusiness-like, to at least one young woman, is as remarkable
en|and admirable as it is inexplicable. The evenings which did not find Fred in Parson
en|Wedgewell’s parlor were few indeed, and if, when he was with Esther, he did not
en|talk quite as sentimentally as he had done in the earlier days of his engagement,
en|and if he talked business very frequently, the change did not seem distasteful to the
en|lady herself. For the business of which he talked was, in the main, of a
en|sort which loving women have for ages recognized as the inevitable, and to which they
en|have subjected themselves with a unanimity which deserves the gratitude of all humanity. Fred talked
en|of a cottage which he might enter without first knocking at the door, and of
en|a partnership which should be unlimited; if he learned, in the course of successive conversations,
en|that even in partnerships of the most extreme order many compromises are absolutely necessary, the
en|lesson was one which improved his character in the ratio in which it abased his
en|pride. The cottage grew as rapidly as the mill, and on his returns from various
en|trips for machinery there came with Fred’s freight certain packages which prevented their owner from
en|appearing so completely the absorbed business man which he flattered himself that he seemed. Then
en|the partnership was formed one evening in Parson Wedgewell’s own church, in the presence of
en|a host of witnesses, Fred appearing as self-satisfied and radiant as the gainer in such
en|transactions always does, while Esther’s noble face and drooping eyes showed beyond doubt who it
en|was that was the giver. As the weeks succeeded each other after the wedding, however,
en|no acquaintance of the couple could wonder whether the gainer or the giver was the
en|happier. Fred improved rapidly, as the school-boy improves; but Esther’s graces were already of mature
en|growth, and rejoiced in their opportunity for development. Though she could not have explained how
en|it happened, she could not but notice that maidens regarded her wonderingly, wives contemplated her
en|wistfully, frowns departed and smiles appeared when she approached people who were usually considered prosaic.
en|Yet shadows sometimes stole over her face, when she looked at certain of her old
en|acquaintances, and the cause thereof soon took a development which was anything but pleasing to
en|her husband. “Fred,” said Esther one evening, “it makes me real unhappy sometimes to think
en|of the good wives there are who are not as happy as I am. I
en|think of Mrs. Moshier and Mrs. Crayme, and the only reason that I can see
en|is, their husbands drink.” “I guess you’re right, Ettie,” said Fred. “They didn’t begin their
en|domestic tyranny in advance, as _you_ did--bless you for it.” “But why _don’t_ their husbands
en|stop?” asked Esther, too deeply interested in her subject to notice her husband’s compliment. “They
en|must see what they’re doing, and how cruel it all is.” “They’re too far gone
en|to stop; I suppose that’s the reason,” said Fred. “It hasn’t been easy work for
en|_me_ to keep my promise, Ettie, and I’m a young man; Moshier and Crayme are
en|middle-aged men, and liquor is simply necessary to them.” “That dreadful old Bunley wasn’t too
en|old to reform, it seems,” said Esther. “Fred, I believe one reason is that no
en|one has asked them to stop. See how good Harry Wainright has been since he
en|found that so many people were interested in him that day!” “Ye----es,” drawled Fred, evidently
en|with a suspicion of what was coming, and trying to change the subject by suddenly
en|burying himself in his memorandum book. But this ruse did not succeed, for Esther crossed
en|the room to where Fred sat, placed her hands on his shoulders, and a kiss
en|on his forehead, and exclaimed, “Fred, _you’re_ the proper person to reform those two men!”
en|“Oh, Ettie,” groaned Fred, “you’re entirely mistaken. Why, they’d laugh right in my face, if
en|they didn’t get angry and knock me down. Reformers want to be older men, better
en|men, men like your father, for instance, if people are to listen to them.” “Father
en|says they need to be men who understand the nature of those they are talking
en|to,” replied Esther; “and you once told me that you understood Moshier and Crayme perfectly.”
en|“But just think of what they are, Ettie,” pleaded Fred. “Moshier is a contractor, and
en|Crayme’s a steamboat captain; _such_ men never reform, though they always are good fellows. Why,
en|if I were to speak to either of them on the subject, they’d laugh in
en|my face, or curse me. The only way I was able to make peace with
en|them for stopping drinking myself was to say that I did it to please my
en|wife.” “Did they accept that as sufficient excuse?” asked Esther. “Yes,” said Fred reluctantly, and
en|biting his lips over this slip of his tongue. “Then you’ve set them a good
en|example, and I can’t believe its effect will be lost,” said Esther. “I sincerely hope
en|it won’t,” said Fred, very willing to seem a reformer at heart; “nobody would be
en|gladder than I to see those fellows with wives as happy as mine seems to
en|be.” “Then why don’t you follow it up, Fred, dear, and make sure of your
en|hopes being realized? You can’t imagine how much happier _I_ would be if I could
en|meet those dear women without feeling that I had to hide the joy that’s so
en|hard to keep to myself.” The conversation continued with considerable strain to Fred’s amiability; but
en|his sophistry was no match for his wife’s earnestness, and he was finally compelled to
en|promise that he would make an appeal to Crayme, with whom he had a business
en|engagement, on the arrival of Crayme’s boat, the _Excellence_. Before the whistles of the steamer
en|were next heard, however, Esther learned something of the sufferings of would-be reformers, and found
en|cause to wonder who was to endure most that Mrs. Crayme should have a sober
en|husband, for Fred was alternately cross, moody, abstracted, and inattentive, and even sullenly remarked at
en|his breakfast-table one morning that he shouldn’t be sorry if the _Excellence_ were to blow
en|up, and leave Mrs. Crayme to find her happiness in widowhood. But no such luck
en|befell the lady: the whistle-signals of the _Excellence_ were again heard in the river, and
en|the nature of Fred’s business with the captain made it unadvisable for Fred to make
en|an excuse for leaving the boat unvisited. It _did_ seem to Fred Macdonald as if
en|everything conspired to make his task as hard as it could possibly be. Crayme was
en|already under the influence of more liquor than was necessary to his well-being, and the
en|boat carried as passengers a couple of men, who, though professional gamblers, Crayme found very
en|jolly company when they were not engaged in their business calling. Besides, Captain Crayme was
en|running against time with an opposition boat which had just been put upon the river,
en|and he appreciated the necessity of having the boat’s bar well stocked and freely opened
en|to whoever along the river was influential in making or marring the reputation of steamboats.
en|Fred finally got the captain into his own room, however, and made a freight contract
en|so absent-mindedly that the sagacious captain gained an immense advantage over him; then he acted
en|so awkwardly, and looked so pale, that the captain suggested chills, and prescribed brandy. Fred
en|smiled feebly, and replied, “No, thank you, Sam; brandy’s at the bottom of the trouble.
en|I”--here Fred made a tremendous attempt to rally himself--“I want _you_ to swear off, Sam.”
en|The astonishment of Captain Crayme was marked enough to be alarming at first; then the
en|ludicrous feature of Fred’s request struck him so forcibly that he burst into a laugh
en|before whose greatness Fred trembled and shrank. “Well, by thunder!” exclaimed the captain, when he
en|recovered his breath; “if that isn’t the best thing I ever heard yet! The idea
en|of a steamboat captain swearing off his whiskey! Say, Fred, don’t you want me to
en|join the church? I forgot that you’d married a preacher’s daughter, or I wouldn’t have
en|been so puzzled over your white face to-day. Sam Crayme brought down to cold water!
en|Wouldn’t the boys along the river get up a sweet lot of names for me--the
en|‘Cold-water Captain,’ ‘Psalm-singing Sammy’! and then, when an editor or any other visitor came aboard,
en|_wouldn’t_ I look the thing, hauling out glasses and a pitcher of water! Say, Fred,
en|does your wife let you drink tea and coffee?” “Sam!” exclaimed Fred, springing to his
en|feet, “if you don’t stop slanting at my wife, I’ll knock you down.” “Good!” said
en|the captain, without exhibiting any signs of trepidation. “_Now_ you talk like yourself again. I
en|beg your pardon, old fellow; you know I was only joking, but it _is_ too
en|funny. You’ll have to take a trip or two with me again, though, and be
en|reformed.” “Not any,” said Fred, resuming his chair; “take your wife along, and reform yourself.”
en|“Look here, now, young man,” said the captain, “_you’re_ cracking on too much steam. Honestly,
en|Fred, I’ve kept a sharp eye on you for two or three months, and I
en|am right glad you can let whiskey alone. I’ve seen times when I wished I
en|were in your boots; but steamboats can’t be run without liquor, however it may be
en|with woolen mills.” “That’s all nonsense,” said Fred. “You get trade because you run your
en|boat on time, charge fair prices, and deliver your freight in good order. Who gives
en|you business because you drink and treat?” The captain, being unable to recall any shipper
en|of the class alluded to by Fred, changed his course. “’Tisn’t so much that,” said
en|he; “it’s a question of reputation. How would I feel to go ashore at Pittsburg
en|or Louisville or Cincinnati, and refuse to drink with anybody? Why, ’twould ruin me. It’s
en|different with you who don’t have to meet anybody but religious old farmers. Besides, you’ve
en|just been married.” “And you’ve been married for five years,” said Fred, with a sudden
en|sense of help at hand. “How do you suppose _your_ wife feels?” Captain Crayme’s jollity
en|subsided a little, but with only a little hesitation he replied, “Oh! she’s used to
en|it; she doesn’t mind it.” “You’re the only person in town that thinks so, Sam,”
en|said Fred. Captain Crayme got up and paced his little state-room two or three times,
en|with a face full of uncertainty. At last he replied, “Well, between old friends, Fred,
en|I don’t think so very strongly myself. Hang it! I wish I’d been brought up
en|a preacher, or something of the kind, so I wouldn’t have had business ruining my
en|chances of being the right sort of a family man. Emily _don’t_ like my drinking,
en|and I’ve promised to look up some other business; but ’tisn’t easy to get out
en|of steamboating when you’ve got a good boat and a first-rate trade. Once she felt
en|so awfully about it that I _did_ swear off--don’t tell anybody, for God’s sake! but
en|I did. I had to look out for my character along the river, though; so
en|I swore off on the sly, and played sick. I’d give my orders to the
en|mates and clerks from my bed in here, and then I’d lock myself in, and
en|read novels and the Bible to keep from thinking. ’Twas awful dry work all around;
en|but ‘whole hog or none’ is _my_ style, you know. There was fun in it,
