en|though, to think of doing something that no other captain on the river ever did.
en|But, thunder! by the time night came, I was so tired of loafing that I
en|wrapped a blanket around my head and shoulders, like a Hoosier, sneaked out the outer
en|door here, and walked the guards, between towns; but I was so frightened for fear
en|some one would know me that the walk did me more harm than good. And
en|blue! why a whole cargo of indigo would have looked like a snow-storm alongside of

en|Toward the next night I was just afraid to go to sleep; so I made
en|up my mind to get well, go on duty, and dodge everybody that it seemed

en|about my little game; I poured my couple of fingers, and dropped it down as

en|I’d have either killed somebody or killed myself if I’d gone on a day or

en|instead of lose it: you’ll be as famous as the Red River Raft, or the

en|business,” exclaimed the captain, springing to his feet. “Let me draw up a pledge,” said

en|captain gently, and pushing Fred out of the room and upon the guards. “Emily shall
en|do that. Below there!--Perkins, I’ve got to go up town for an hour; see if
en|you can’t pick up freight to pay laying-up expenses somehow. Fred, go home and get
en|your traps; ‘now’s the accepted time,’ as your father-in-law has dinged at me, many a
en|Sunday, from the pulpit.” CHAPTER XIV. SAILING UP STREAM. As Sam Crayme strode toward the
en|body of the town, his business instincts took strong hold of his sentiments, in the
en|manner natural alike to saints and sinners, and he laid a plan of operations against
en|whiskey which was characterized by the apparent recklessness but actual prudence which makes for glory
en|in steamboat captains, as it does in army commanders. As was his custom in business,
en|he first drove at full speed upon the greatest obstacles; so it came to pass
en|that he burst into his own house, threw his arm around his wife with more
en|than ordinary tenderness, and then looking into her eyes with the daring born of utter
en|desperation, said, “Emily, I came back to sign the strongest temperance pledge that you can
en|possibly draw up; Fred Macdonald wanted to write out one, but I told him that
en|nobody but you should do it; you’ve earned the right to, poor girl.” No such
en|duty and surprise having ever before come hand in hand to Mrs. Crayme, she acted
en|as every true woman will imagine that she herself would have done under similar circumstances,
en|and this action made it not so easy as it might otherwise have been to
en|see just where the pen and ink were, or to prevent the precious document, when
en|completed, from being disfigured by peculiar blots which were neither finger-marks nor ink-spots, yet which
en|in shape and size suggested both of these indications of unneatness. Mrs. Crayme was not
en|an adept at literary composition, and, being conscious of her own deficiencies, she begged that
en|a verbal pledge might be substituted; but her husband was firm. “A contract don’t steer
en|worth a cent unless it’s in writing, Emily,” said he, looking over his wife’s shoulder
en|as she wrote. “Gracious, girl, you’re making it too thin; _any_ greenhorn could sail right
en|through that and all around it. Here, let _me_ have it.” And Crayme wrote, dictating
en|aloud to himself as he did so, “And the--party--of the first part--hereby agrees to--do everything--else
en|that the--spirit of this--agreement--seems to the party--of the second--part to--indicate or--imply.” This he read over
en|to his wife, saying, “That’s the way we fix contracts that aren’t ship-shape, Emily; a
en|steamboat couldn’t be run in any other way.” Then Crayme wrote at the foot of
en|the paper, “Sam. Crayme, Capt. Str. _Excellence_,” surveyed the document with evident pride, and handed
en|it to his wife, saying, “Now, you see, you’ve got me so I can’t ever
en|get out of it by trying to make out that ’twas some other Sam Crayme
en|that you reformed.” “O husband!” said Mrs. Crayme, throwing her arms about the captain’s neck,
en|“_don’t_ talk in that dreadful business way! I’m too happy to bear it. I want
en|to go with you on this trip.” The captain shrank away from his wife’s arms,
en|and a cold perspiration started all over him as he exclaimed, “Oh, don’t, little girl!
en|Wait till next trip. There’s an unpleasant set of passengers aboard; the barometer points to
en|rainy weather, so you’d have to stay in the cabin all the time; our cook
en|is sick, and his cubs serve up the most infernal messes; we’re light of freight,
en|and have got to stop at every warehouse on the river, and the old boat’ll
en|be either shrieking, or bumping, or blowing off steam the whole continual time.” Mrs. Crayme’s
en|happiness had been frightening some of her years away, and her smile carried Sam himself
en|back to his pre-marital period as she said, “Never mind the rest; I see you
en|don’t want me to go,” and then she became Mrs. Crayme again as she said,
en|pressing her face closely to her husband’s breast, “but I hope you won’t get _any_
en|freight, _anywhere_, so you can get home all the sooner.” Then the captain called on
en|Dr. White, and announced such a collection of symptoms that the doctor grew alarmed, insisted
en|on absolute quiet, conveyed Crayme in his own carriage to the boat, saw him into
en|his berth, and gave to Fred Macdonald a multitude of directions and cautions, the sober
en|recording of which upon paper was of great service in saving Fred from suffering over
en|the Quixotic aspect which the whole project had begun, in his mind, to take on.
en|He felt ashamed even to look squarely into Crayme’s eye, and his mind was greatly
en|relieved when the captain turned his face to the wall and exclaimed, “Fred, for goodness’
en|sake get out of here; I feel enough like a baby now, without having a
en|nurse alongside. I’ll do well enough for a few hours; just look in once in
en|a while.” During the first day of the trip, Crayme made no trouble for himself
en|or Fred: under the friendly shelter of night, the two men had a two-hour chat
en|which was alternately humorous, business-like, and retrospective, and then Crayme fell asleep. The next day
en|was reasonably pleasant out of doors, so the captain wrapped himself in a blanket and
en|sat in an extension-chair on the guards, where with solemn face he received some condolences
en|which went far to keep him in good humor after the sympathizers had departed. On
en|the second night the captain was restless, and the two men played cards. On the
en|third day the captain’s physique reached the bottom of its stock of patience, and protested
en|indignantly at the withdrawal of its customary stimulus; and it acted with more consistency, though
en|no less ugliness, than the human mind does when under excitement and destitute of control.
en|The captain grew terribly despondent, and Fred found ample use for all the good stories
en|he knew. Some of these amused the captain greatly, but after one of them he
en|sighed, “Poor old Billy Hockess told me that the only time I ever heard it
en|before, and _didn’t_ we have a glorious time that night! He’d just put all his
en|money into the _Yenesei_--that blew up and took him with it only a year afterward--and
en|he gave us a new kind of punch he’d got the hang of when he
en|went East for the boat’s carpets. ’Twas made of two bottles of brandy, one whiskey,
en|two rum, one gin, two sherry, and four claret, with guava jelly, and lemon peel
en|that had been soaking in curaçoa and honey for a month. It looks kind of
en|weak when you think about it, but there were only six of us in the
en|party, and it went to the spot by the time we got through. Golly, but
en|didn’t we make Rome howl that night!” Fred shuddered, and experimented upon his friend with
en|song; he was rewarded by hearing the captain hum an occasional accompaniment; but, as Fred
en|got fairly into a merry Irish song about one Terry O’Rann, and uttered the lines
en|in which the poet states that the hero “--took whiskey punch Ivery night for his
en|lunch,” the captain put such a world of expression into a long-drawn sigh that Fred
en|began to feel depressed himself; besides, songs were not numerous in Fred’s repertoire, and those
en|in which there was no allusion to drinking could be counted on half his fingers.
en|Then he borrowed the barkeeper’s violin, and played, one after another, the airs which had
en|been his favorites in the days of his courtship, until Crayme exclaimed, “Say, Fred, we’re
en|not playing church; give us something that don’t bring all of a fellow’s dead friends
en|along with it.” Fred reddened, swung his bow viciously, and dashed into “Natchez Under the
en|Hill,” an old air which would have delighted Offenbach, but which will never appear in
en|a collection of classical music. “Ah! that’s something like music,” exclaimed Captain Crayme, as Fred
en|paused suddenly to repair a broken string. “I never hear that but I think of
en|Wesley Treepoke, that used to run the _Quitman_; went afterward to the _Rising Planet_, when
en|the _Quitman’s_ owners put her on a new line as an opposition boat. Wess and
en|I used to work things so as to make Louisville at the same time--he going
en|up, I going down, and then turn about--and we always had a glorious night of
en|it, with one or two other lively boys that we’d pick up. And Wess had
en|a fireman that could fiddle off old ‘Natchez’ in a way that would just make
en|a corpse dance till its teeth rattled, and that fireman would always be called in
en|just as we’d got to the place where you can’t tell what sort of whiskey
en|’tis you’re drinking, and I tell you, ’twas so heavenly that a fellow could forgive
en|the last boat that beat him on the river, or stole a landing from him.
en|And _such_ whiskey as Wess kept! used to go cruising around the back country, sampling
en|little lots run out of private stills. He’d always find nectar, you’d better believe. Poor
en|old boy! the tremens took him off at last. He hove his pilot overboard just
en|before he died, and put a bullet into Pete Langston, his second clerk--they were both
en|trying to hold him, you see--but they never laid it up against him. I wish
en|I knew what became of the whiskey he had on hand when he walked off--no,
en|I don’t, either; what am I thinking about? But I do, though--hanged if I don’t!”
en|Fred grew pale: he had heard of drunkards growing delirious upon ceasing to drink; he
en|had heard of men who, in periods of aberration, were impelled by the motive of
en|the last act or recollection which strongly impressed them; what if the captain should suddenly
en|become delirious, and try to throw _him_ overboard or shoot him? Fred determined to get
en|the captain at once upon the guards--no, into the cabin, where there would be no
en|sight of water to suggest anything dreadful--and search his room for pistols. But the captain
en|objected to being moved into the cabin. “The boys,” said the captain, alluding to the
en|gamblers, “are mighty sharp in the eye, and like as not they’d see through my
en|little game, and then where’d my reputation be? Speaking of the boys reminds me of
en|Harry Genang, that cleaned out that rich Kentucky planter at bluff one night, and then
en|swore off gambling for life and gave a good-by supper aboard the boat. ’Twas just
en|at the time when Prince Imperial Champagne came out, and the whole supper was made
en|of that splendid stuff. I guess I must have put away four bottles, and if
en|I’d known how much he’d ordered, I could have carried away a couple more. I’ve
en|always been sorry I didn’t.” Fred wondered if there was any subject of conversation which
en|would not suggest liquor to the captain; he even brought himself to ask if Crayme
en|had seen the new Methodist Church at Barton since it had been finished. “Oh, yes,”
en|said the captain; “I started to walk Moshier home one night, after we’d punished a
en|couple of bottles of old Crow whiskey at our house, and he caved in all
en|of a sudden, and I laid him out on the steps of that very church
en|till I could get a carriage. Those were my last two bottles of Crow, too;
en|it’s too bad the way the good things of this life paddle off.” The captain
en|raised himself in his berth, sat on the edge thereof, stood up, stared out the
en|window, and began to pace his room with his head down and his hands behind
en|his back. Little by little he raised his head, dropped his hands, flung himself into
en|a chair, beat the devil’s tattoo on the table, sprang up excitedly, and exclaimed, “I’m
en|going back on all the good times I ever had.” “You’re only getting ready to
en|try a new kind, Sam,” said Fred. “Well, I’m going back on my friends.” “Not
en|on all of them; the dead ones would pat you on the back, if they
en|got a chance.” “A world without whiskey looks infernally dismal to a fellow that isn’t
en|half done living.” “It looks first-rate to a fellow that hasn’t got any back-down in
en|him.” “Curse you! I wish I’d made _you_ back down when you first talked temperance
en|to me.” “Go ahead! Then curse your wife--don’t be afraid; you’ve been doing it ever
en|since you married her.” Crayme flew at Macdonald’s throat; the younger man grappled the captain
en|and threw him into his bunk. The captain struggled and glared like a tiger; Fred
en|gasped, between the special efforts dictated by self-preservation, “Sam, I--promised to--to see you--through--and I’m--going to--do
en|it, if--if I have to--break your neck.” The captain made one tremendous effort; Fred braced
en|one foot against the table, put a knee on the captain’s breast, held both the
en|captain’s wrists tightly, looked full into the captain’s eyes, and breathed a small prayer--for his
en|own safety. For a moment or two, perhaps longer, the captain strained violently, and then
en|relaxed all effort and cried, “Fred, you’ve whipped me!” “Nonsense! whip yourself,” exclaimed Fred, “if
en|you’re going to stop drinking.” The captain turned his face to the wall and said
en|nothing; but he seemed to be so persistently swallowing something that Fred suspected a secreted
en|bottle, and moved an investigation so suddenly that the captain had not time in which
en|to wipe his eyes. “Hang it, Fred,” said he, rather brokenly; “how _can_ what’s babyish
en|in men whip a full-grown steamboat captain?” “The same way that it whipped a full-grown
en|woolen-mill manager once, I suppose, old boy,” said Macdonald. “Is that so?” exclaimed the captain,
en|astonishment getting so sudden an advantage over shame that he turned over and looked his
en|companion in the face. “Why--how are you, Fred? I feel as if I was just
en|being introduced. Didn’t anybody else help?” “Yes,” said Fred, “a woman; but--you’ve got a wife,
en|too.” Crayme fell back on his pillow and sighed. “If I could only _think_ about
en|her, Fred! But I can’t; whiskey’s the only thing that comes into my mind.” “Can’t
en|think about her!” exclaimed Fred; “why, are you acquainted with her yet, I wonder? _I’ll_
en|never forget the evening you were married.” “That _was_ jolly, wasn’t it?” said Crayme. “I’ll
en|bet such sherry was never opened west of the Alleghanies, before or----” “_Hang_ your sherry!”
en|roared Fred; “it’s your wife that I remember. _You_ couldn’t see her, of course, for
en|you were standing alongside of her; but the rest of us--well, I wished myself in
en|your place, that’s all.” “Did you, though?” said Crayme, with a smile which seemed rather
en|proud; “well, I guess old Major Pike did too, for he drank to her about
en|twenty times that evening. Let’s see; she wore a white moire antique, I think they
en|called it, and it cost twenty-one dollars a dozen, and there was at least one
en|broken bottle in every----” “And I made up my mind she was throwing herself away,
en|in marrying a fellow that would be sure to care more for whiskey than he
en|did for her,” interrupted Fred. “Ease off, Fred, ease off now; there wasn’t any whiskey
en|there; I tried to get some of the old Twin Tulip brand for punch, but----”
en|“But the devil happened to be asleep, and you got a chance to behave yourself,”
en|said Fred. Crayme looked appealingly. “Fred,” said he, “tell me about her yourself; I’ll take
en|it as a favor.” “Why, she looked like a lot of lilies and roses,” said
en|Fred, “except that you couldn’t tell where one left off and the other began. As
en|she came into the room _I_ felt like getting down on my knees. Old Bayle
en|was telling me a vile story just then, but the minute _she_ came in he
en|stopped as if he was shot.” “He wouldn’t drink a drop that evening,” said Crayme,
en|“and I’ve puzzled my wits over that for five years----” “She looked _so_ proud of
en|_you_,” interrupted Fred with some impatience. “Did she?” asked Crayme. “Well, I guess I _was_
en|a good-looking fellow in those days: I know Pike came up to me once, with
en|a glass in his hand, and said that he ought to drink to _me_, for
en|I was the finest-looking groom he’d ever seen. He was so tight, though, that he
en|couldn’t hold his glass steady; and though you know I never had a drop of
en|stingy blood in me, it _did_ go to my heart to see him spill that
en|gorgeous sherry.” “She looked very proud of _you_,” Fred repeated; “but I can’t see why,
en|for I’ve never seen her do it since.” “You _will_, though, hang you!” exclaimed the
en|captain. “Get out of here! I can think about her _now_, and I don’t want
en|anybody else around. No rudeness meant, you know, Fred.” Fred Macdonald retired quietly, taking with
en|him the keys of both doors, and feeling more exhausted than he had been on
en|any Saturday night since the building of the mill. CHAPTER XV. A FIRST INWARD PEEP.
en|Among the Barton people who had actually made any effort for the sake of temperance,
en|no one found greater comfort in contemplative retrospects of his own work than Deacon Jones.
en|True, his contributions to the various funds which Crupp, Tomple, Wedgewell, and Brown devised had
en|not been as great as had been expected of him; nor had such moneys as
en|he finally gave been obtained from him without an amount of effort which Crupp declared
en|sufficient to effect the extraction, from the soil, of the stump of a centenarian oak;
en|but when the money had left his pocket, and was absolutely beyond recall, the deacon
en|made the most he could out of it by the only method which remained. His
en|contributions gave him an excuse for talk and exhortation, and, next to money-making, there was
en|no operation which the deacon enjoyed as much as that of exhorting others to good
en|deeds. Until there broke out in Barton the temperance excitement alluded to in our first
en|chapter, Deacon Jones’s hortatory efforts had been principally of a religious nature; he believed in
en|religion, and he occasionally extracted enjoyment from it; besides, his thrifty soul had always been
en|profoundly moved by the business-like nature of the Scripture passage, “Whoso shall convert a sinner
en|from the error of his ways, shall save a soul from death and cover a
en|multitude of sins.” Many had been the unregenerate in Barton with whom the deacon had
en|labored, generally with considerable tact, as to occasion and language, and sometimes with success. His
en|orthodoxy was acceptable to every pastor in the village, for he was an extreme believer
en|in every religious tenet which either pastor declared necessary to salvation; and his frequent inability
en|to reconcile such of these ideas as conflicted with each other only led the ministers
en|to accord new admiration to a faith which was appalled by nothing. Up to the
en|time when he took active part in the temperance movement, one of his favorite injunctions
en|had been, “Lay up your treasure in heaven;” when, however, he found himself suddenly and
en|frequently called upon for contributions, he dropped this injunction in favor of that one which
en|reads, “Give to him that asketh of thee.” It had been a matter of considerable
en|sorrow to the deacon that his first knowledge of this passage had been derived from
en|St. Luke instead of St. Matthew, and that he had many times been compelled to
en|say “Give to _every man_,” etc., which quotation had reacted upon him in a manner
en|which caused him to quote to himself, “Many are the afflictions of the righteous,” and
en|to suffer some terrible flounderings in the twin pits of logic and casuistry; but when
en|he corrected himself according to Matthew, his heart was gladdened, and his restraint removed. The
en|old man talked a great deal out of honest delight in righteousness and humanity; but
en|he was never moved to reticence by the thought that if his scattered seed produced
en|a fair share of grain, the demands upon his own precious store would be lessened.
en|Besides, the deacon could, with propriety, urge a more conspicuous form of well-doing than mere
en|contributions of currency ever attained to. Had not he himself taken upon his shoulders Tom
en|Adams, driver of the brick-yard team? If any one doubted it, or had never been
en|made acquainted with the fact, the deacon gave him no excuse for farther ignorance. One
en|after another of the well-to-do merchants, professional men, and farmers, were urged by the deacon
en|to take entire charge of some unfortunate soul, after the manner of the deacon himself
en|with Tom, and to all of these he insisted that what he had done for
en|Tom he had been richly paid for by the approving smiles of his own conscience.
en|Shrewd judges of human nature were convinced that if such payment was made to the
en|deacon, he was doubly paid, for Tom Adams had been a treasure of a workman
en|ever since he had stopped drinking; but, with the marvelous blindness of the man who
en|objects to seeing, the deacon clearly comprehended both aspects of the situation, without ever once
en|allowing them to interfere with each other. He was pursuing his favorite line of argument
en|in his store one afternoon, before Parson Brown, Lawyer Bottom, the postmaster, Dr. White, and
en|two or three others who were not active customers at that immediate moment, and, as
en|all his hearers but the parson were in good circumstances, the deacon felt called upon
en|to make an unusual effort. “Tell you what it is, gentlemen,” said he, “there’s nothin’
en|like puttin’ your hand in your pocket to show you what doin’ good is. Here
en|I’ve been thinkin’ all my life that I was doin’ good by subscribin’ to Bible
en|Societies, Missionary Societies, an’ all such things, and yet there was _the_ chance right in
en|my own hands, and I was too blind to see it. I done it at
en|last on a risk, as if God didn’t know best when he inspires men to
en|righteous deeds; an’ I was fearful, time an’ again, that it mightn’t turn out well;
en|but I’ve been more abundantly blessed at it than I ever expected to be. It
en|makes a man feel kind of like Christ must have felt, to be able to
en|help a fellow-creature out of his troubles and sins. Look at Tom Adams now! he’s
en|always sober, his children go to Sunday-school, and he’s never around looking as if you’d
en|rather not meet him, and _I_, thank the Lord! feel even better over it than
en|_he_ does.” The postmaster slyly tipped a grave wink at Lawyer Bottom, and the lawyer
en|sagely laid a wise forefinger athwart his own nose. Dr. White dropped a short bark,
en|intended for a cough, which somehow provoked a smile all around. Suddenly a small boy
en|rushed into the store, exclaiming, “O Deacon Jones! Tom Adams fell out of the wagon
en|and broke his leg!” The deacon’s ecstatic expression instantly vanished into thin air, and he
en|asked, with a face full of misery, “And the horses ran away?” “No,” said the
en|boy. “_They’re_ all right.” Dr. White sprang up, seized his cane, and asked, “Where is
en|he?” “That’s so,” asked the deacon, still more sorrowful of countenance, as he continued, “just
en|as corn’s beginnin’ to come in, too, an’ needin’ to be measured an’ sacked; that’s
en|just the way things go in this wicked world!” Lawyer Bottom, who did not believe
en|much in God, and believed still less in the deacon, asked, “Well, deacon, then you
en|wouldn’t advise me to take somebody on my hands for the sake of the spiritual
en|payment I’ll be likely to get out of the operation?” The deacon rallied himself by
en|a tremendous effort, but his countenance did not indicate that the answer he was about
en|to make would be of that softness that turns away wrath; he was saved from
en|disgracing himself, however, by still another boy, who came flying through the main street on
en|horseback, shouting, “Fire! fire! The woolen mill! Fire!” The deacon’s store emptied in an instant
en|of every one but Parson Brown, for all the other listeners were men of some
en|means, and stockholders in the mill. “Here!” shouted the deacon, cutting the cords of a
en|“nest” of pails; “take buckets along with you; like enough it’ll need everybody’s help, and
en|the mill’s only half insured, too! Parson, would you mind sittin’ here until my boy
en|gets back? I’m losin’ enough to-day without having to shut up store, too.” “Certainly, I’ll
en|stay,” said the old preacher, limping to the front of the store, and laying his
en|hand on the shoulder of the troubled storekeeper; “but, Brother Jones, if the light of
en|that burning mill should show you anything inside of yourself, _don’t_ cover your eyes. It’s
en|for righteousness’ sake I ask it.” “All right, Brother Brown,” whispered the deacon hoarsely, as
en|he started off with two water-pails in each hand, and murmuring, “What did the old
en|fellow mean by that, I wonder?” Across the street was Squire Tomple, just jumping into
en|his buggy, and the deacon made haste to accept an invitation to a seat beside
en|his fellow-sufferer. The two stockholders did not lack company; Crupp, Judge Macdonald, and most of
en|the other stockholders, either preceded or followed them, and on the road were hundreds of
en|men and boys, full of an enterprising desire to see the largest fire that had
en|ever occurred in Barton, and already experiencing such of the pleasures of anticipation as a
en|heavy column of smoke could create. Coming in sight of the mill itself, the deacon
en|groaned, and the Squire assisted him, for flames were bursting from every window, and the
en|men who had been passing pails of water up ladders and through the stairways had
en|been driven from their work, and had formed a circle which was slowly but steadily
en|widening. Considerable of the wool had been removed and stacked outside the building, and it
en|now became necessary to move this still farther away, but so many hands were ready
en|to seize it that Deacon Jones could not relieve his feelings even by attempting to
en|save property; so he stood still and looked at the fire, as he estimated his
en|losses. Such a day he had not known since he had lost considerable uninsured stock
en|by the explosion of a river steamer. Sidling uneasily about among the crowd, he found
en|several stockholders anxiously comparing pencil notes, and the figures were anything but consolatory supposing all
en|the stock to be saved, there was yet the mill and machinery--value, about ten thousand
en|dollars--which would be totally lost; insurance, five thousand dollars; dead loss, ditto; which left the
en|Squire out of pocket to the extent of a quarter of his subscription. The small
en|profit which had already accrued would not more than cover the loss of the interest
en|on the remaining capital until the mill could be rebuilt, if it seemed advisable to
en|rebuild it. “Who’s to blame for all this?” asked the deacon angrily. “We haven’t learned
en|yet,” said the judge, “and I’m afraid it won’t help matters any to know all
en|about it. There goes the last of it!” As the judge spoke, the blazing frame
en|fell, the small boys shouted “Oh----h!” in chorus, and the deacon’s heart sank like lead
en|as he turned away. He had lost, say, a hundred and fifty dollars by the
en|fire, and Tom Adams’s misfortune would entail additional loss upon him, for a new man
en|would have to be watched and taught and helped, whereas Tom worked as easily as
en|the wheel of a machine. It was but right that the deacon should regret his
en|losses; for though he was a man of considerable property, a dollar looked very large
en|to him, for the reason that his first dollars had each one represented an enormous
en|amount of labor. But when Lawyer Bottom, who had invested in mill stock only with
en|the hope of profit, approached the deacon, and asked, with more curiosity than malice, “How
en|about temperance now, deacon?” the facial contortions which the deacon offered in reply sent the
en|lawyer away in an ecstasy of unholy glee, which almost eradicated his own sense of
en|loss, and which dispelled for a time such little belief as he had in the
en|transforming power of religion. But what is one man’s poison is another’s food. The lawyer’s
en|question was not entirely disposed of by the deacon’s ungracious reply; it repeated itself time
en|and again to the old man, and at the most inopportune times and places; it
en|came to him behind the counter, and made him give wrong weights and measures, with
en|the balance not always in his favor; it came to him when he was making
en|entries in his day-book, and caused him to forget certain items; at his own dinner-table
en|it suddenly made itself heard, and interfered with his relish of the good viands which
en|he so much enjoyed; it dropped in upon him in his dreams, when he could
en|not be on his guard against his better self, and extracted from his conscience a
en|provoking line of answers which in his waking hours he could not gainsay. For three
en|days this depressing experience continued, and then there occurred, at the regular weekly prayer-meeting of
en|Parson Wedgewell’s church, an episode which for months caused mournful reflections in the minds of
en|such of Parson Wedgewell’s parishioners as were not in the habit of attending prayer-meeting. It
en|was noticed by the faithful that Deacon Jones looked unusually solemn and sensitive as he
en|entered the room, and that he did not, as had been hitherto his habit, start
en|the second hymn. This omission having been made good by some enterprising member, however, the
en|deacon got upon his feet and said: “Brethren, during the past few days my eyes
en|have been opened, and what I have seen hasn’t been pleasant to look upon. It
en|is indeed true, my dear friends, that Satan sometimes appears as an angel of light.
en|For months I’ve been feeling, and real happily, too, what a glorious thing it was
en|to do good; I had been instrumental in saving one man from destruction by keeping
en|him busy, and I’d helped save another”--here the deacon paused suddenly and looked around to
en|make sure that Judge Macdonald was not in the room--“I’d helped save another by taking
en|an interest in the mill. But within a few days I’ve learned that my own
en|righteousness was as filthy rags; ’twas even worse than that, brethren, for the worst rags
en|are worth so much a pound, but I can’t find that my righteousness is worth
en|anything at all. I’ve fought it out with myself, brethren, an’ I believe I’ve conquered;
en|but it makes my heart sick to see what my enemy looks like, an’ to
en|think I’ve got to carry him around with me through the rest of my days.
en|Doin’ good’s all right, even if it _does_ pay in dollars and cents, brethren; but
en|doin’ good for the sake of what it’ll bring is the quickest way of makin’
en|a hypocrite that I ever found, an’ I’m beginnin’ to think that I’ve found a
en|good many ways in myself, my friends. I ask an interest in the prayers of
en|God’s people, an’ I assure ’em that there’s no danger of any of their prayers
en|bein’ wasted.” The deacon dropped into his seat, and the silence that prevailed for a
en|moment was simply inevitable in a little company that had never before heard such an
en|extraordinary confession; as one of the members afterward remarked, it sounded like a murderer’s last
en|dying speech. Then good Parson Wedgewell sprang to his feet, and, with streaming eyes and
en|rapid utterances, offered a prayer such as had never been heard in that room before.
en|The songs and prayers which followed were not those to which the meeting were accustomed,
en|and when at last the assemblage separated, there could not be heard from the home-wending
en|couples any critiques of the language or garb of any one who had been present.
en|As for Deacon Jones, he continued his new fight most valiantly by visiting Tom Adams
en|that very evening, and assuring him that, their supplementary agreement to the contrary notwithstanding, he
en|would continue Tom’s pay during his confinement, and would pay his doctor’s bill also. CHAPTER
en|XVI. A REFORMER DISAPPOINTED. During the day or two which followed his interview with Tappelmine,
en|Father Baguss was consumed with conflicting emotions. He could not deny that his offer to
en|help Tappelmine had taken an unpleasant load off of his own heart; but it was
en|equally certain that the contemplation of the possible results of the arrangement gave him a
en|sense of oppression, which differed from the first in quality, but of which the quantity
en|was far too great to be endured with comfort. To find a way of getting
en|out of the whole matter was a suggestion which came frequently to the heart of
en|the old man, and was not as rigidly excluded as it would have been from
en|that of the reader; but fortunately for the honesty of Father Baguss, his ingenuity was
en|of the lowest order conceivable; so he did as thousands of his betters have done
en|when unable, by any abandonment of self-respect, to avoid the inevitable: he submitted, and groaned
en|frequently to the Lord. Sometimes these efforts before the Unseen increased the old man’s lugubriousness;
en|at other times, a song came to his rescue, followed by a troop of its
en|own kind; but so uncertain were his moods that Mrs. Baguss, who never before had
en|occasion to suppose that there was a single nerve in her husband’s body, began to
en|complain that she didn’t “believe in this thing of lookin’ out for other folks, if
en|it makes you cranky with your own.” The old man’s trouble increased on the third
en|day, for Tappelmine dropped in and hinted vaguely that it was not yet too late
en|to plant winter wheat. The old man went into Tappelmine’s field with his own team,
en|and plowed; he worked his horses longer hours than he ever did on his own
en|ground; he lent an extra horse to work with Tappelmine’s own before a harrow; he
en|himself sowed the wheat, casting now plentifully, as he thought of what Tappelmine might owe
en|him by harvest-time, and now scantily, as he thought of what might be his own
en|fate if the crop should be troubled with rust, or blight, or rain, or drought.
en|And all the while, as he followed his horses, the old man kept uttering short
en|petitions for Tappelmine and himself; and all the while his soul was full of unspoken
en|prayers for heavy rains or sudden cold, so that the work might be stopped by
en|the hand of Providence himself. But no such fortune befell the good old man: such
en|an open fall had not been known since the settlement of Barton; even the Indian
en|summer lasted so long that the poet of the Barton _Register_ found opportunity to publish,
en|in three successive weekly numbers, “odes,” which could be read in the weather which suggested
en|them. When a heavy rain at last put an end to field work, there were
en|twenty-seven acres in wheat on the Tappelmine estate. Father Baguss ached in soul and body,
en|but the wheat-field work was but the beginning of sorrow. The Tappelmine larder was bareness
en|itself; there was not a porker in the Tappelmine pen; there was not even corn
en|enough in the Tappelmine crib to feed the family horse, let alone to send to
en|mill, and be ground into the meal which the Tappelmines fortunately preferred to fine flour.
en|Father Baguss sold the necessities of life in small quantities to his neighbor, with the
en|understanding that they were to be repaid by the labor of Tappelmine, who was to
en|get out material for barrel-staves and wheelwright’s spokes on the old man’s woodland; but, by
en|the time the wheat was planted, Tappelmine, who, under the eye of Baguss, did more
en|work in a month than he had done in the whole of the year which
en|preceded, and who during the month had been pretty effectually kept from his accustomed stimulant,
en|fell sick. Then the cup of misery which Father Baguss had put to his own
en|lips was full; as the old man, in his homely way, explained to his own
en|pastor, it didn’t run over, and that was just the trouble; he had to drink
en|it all. He sought for sympathy among his neighbors and acquaintances, but without much success;
en|the Barton postmaster expressed the sentiment of the township, when he said that “no one
en|but a thick-headed blunderer like Baguss would attempt to reform a dead-and-gone soaker like Tappelmine.”
en|Besides, most of the inhabitants wanted to see how the case was going to turn
en|out, and all of them instinctively understood that the best point of view is always
en|at a respectable distance from the object to be looked at. The sorrowing philanthropist went
en|to Crupp, Tomple, and Deacon Jones; but these three reformers, knowing that Baguss could afford
en|the loss, quietly agreed with each other that it would be indeed consolatory to have
en|a companion in experience; so they made excuses, and quoted figures in evidence, and Father
en|Baguss went home with the settled conviction that he would have to look to Providence
en|for his only assistance. But while Providence was thus reforming Father Baguss, Tappelmine was growing
en|steadily weaker, and Baguss found his causes of discomfort increased by a debate, which lasted
en|long in his mind, whether it might not be better, for the sake of the
en|drunkard’s family, to let Tappelmine die, and then lease the farm himself at a price
en|which would support the widow. While one phase of the case was present in his
en|mind, he would suggest to the doctor that medicine didn’t seem to do any good--which
en|was certainly true--and that he didn’t believe it would pay to come so often; when,
en|on the contrary, conscience would argue for its own side, the old man would have
en|all three of the physicians visit Tappelmine in rapid succession. The doctors disagreed, as any
en|one but Father Baguss would have known. Perry suggested electrical treatment, which would necessitate the
en|purchase of a battery, no such piece of mechanism having ever been seen in the
en|town except in a locked cabinet of the Barton High School. Dr. White outlined a
en|course of treatment which seemed reasonable to Father Baguss, but which, put into practice, did
en|neither good nor harm; while Pykem arranged for certain inexpensive applications of water, with results
en|which were in the main encouraging. But Tappelmine was unable to leave his bed for
en|three months, and when he was at all fit to work, he could labor for
en|but two or three hours a day. And so Father Baguss found himself brought down
en|to the position of a man who was spending money without knowing what he was
en|to get for it. Such a position he had never occupied before, and no one
en|could wonder that he felt uncomfortable in it; but the duration of the period was
en|such that the victim succumbed to the steady pressure of truths which, in their abstract
en|form, would have been as ineffective against him as against an acute logician whose intellect
en|had been trained by his pocket. But Father Baguss was not the only instrument of
en|the salvation of Tappelmine. In existence, but scarcely known of or recognized, there was a
en|Mrs. Tappelmine. With face, hair, eyes, and garments of the same color, the color itself
en|being neutral; small, thin, faded, inconspicuous, poorly clad, bent with labors which had yielded no
en|return, as dead to the world as saints strive to be, yet remaining in the
en|world for the sake of those whom she had often wished out of it, Mrs.
en|Tappelmine devoted herself to the wreck of what was once a hope over which her
en|eyes had been of a luster which high-born maidens had envied, and a hope in
en|which her heart had throbbed with a joy which had seemed too great for life
en|to hold. About the bedside of her husband she hovered day and night. When she
en|slept no one but herself knew, and she herself did not care. When Tappelmine made
en|his verbal agreement with Father Baguss, she had listened with a joy whose earnestness was
en|as nothing compared with her resolution. She had hurried away from the broken window to
en|a corner where her dirty children were at quarrelsome play, and she had bestowed upon
en|each of them a passionate caress which startled even the little wretches themselves into wondering
en|silence. From that moment she watched her husband’s every movement, and Tappelmine, like a true
en|Pike--for the Pike, like the Transcendentalist, existed ages before he found his way into literature--Tappelmine
en|subjected himself into his wife’s dominion. He made numberless excuses to go to some place
en|where liquor could be found; she, with the wisdom of the serpent, yet the gentleness
en|of the dove, prevented him. As, through the course of her husband’s labors, under the
en|eye of Baguss, he had grown more silent than ever, she had increased her exertions
en|for his comfort; when, finally, the task was completed, and Tappelmine, with thinner face and
en|hollower eyes than ever, fell heavily upon his rude bed and uttered--almost screamed--the single word
en|“Whiskey!” she was on her knees beside him in an instant. “Jerry,” she exclaimed, “you’ve
en|got the better of whiskey these late days.” “Just a drop more--to keep me from
en|dying,” gasped Tappelmine. “Don’t, Jerry,” she pleaded. “Let me hold you tight, so you _can’t_
en|die.” “Just a drop, for God’s sake, Mariar!” said Tappelmine imploringly. “O Jerry!” replied the
en|wife, “don’t--for the children’s sake; _they’re_ more to you than God is. I hope he’ll
en|forgive me for sayin’ it.” “Only a single mouthful, Mariar,” said Tappelmine, “to keep me
en|from sinkin’.” “You’re not sinkin’, old man--Jerry, dear; you’re gittin’ _up_. _Keep_ up, Jerry.” “I’ll
en|be all right in a day or two, Mariar, if I only get a taste.
en|You don’t want a sick man a-layin’ around, not fit to do for his young
en|ones?” “You don’t need to, Jerry. _I’ll_ do for ’em, if you’ll only--only make ’em
en|proud of you.” “It’ll make me good for more to _you_, old woman--one single mouthful
en|will,” said Tappelmine. “You’ve been better to me these three weeks than you ever was
en|before, Jerry; keep on bein’ so, won’t you? It puts me in mind of old
en|times--times when you used to laugh, an’ kiss me.” “I’d be that way again,” said
en|Tappelmine, “if I could only pick up stren’th.” “You’re that way now, Jerry, if you
en|only stay as you are.” “_You’ll_ die, Mariar,” said the man, “if I don’t get
en|out of this bed some way--you an’ the young uns.” “I’d be glad enough,” said
en|the woman, “if you’d only stay, Jerry.” “An’ the boys an’ girls?” queried Tappelmine. “Would
en|be better off alongside of me in the ground, rather than have their dad go
en|backwards again,” said Mrs. Tappelmine. “People turn up their noses at ’em now, Jerry.” “What
en|are you drivin’ at, Mariar?” “Why, Jerry, when the children go ’long the road--God knows
en|I don’t let ’em do it oftener than I can help--folks see ’em dirty, an’
en|wearin’ poor clothes, an’ not lookin’ over an’ above fed up, an’ they can’t help
en|kind o’ twitchin’ up their faces at ’em once there was a time when I
en|couldn’t have helped doin’ it to young ones lookin’ that way.” “_Curse_ people!” exclaimed Tappelmine.
en|“They do it to me, too,” continued the woman. Tappelmine sprang up, and exclaimed fiercely,
en|“What for?” “’Cause--’cause you’ve made ’em, I reckon, Jerry,” answered Mrs. Tappelmine with some difficulty,
en|occasioned by some choking sobs which nearly took exclusive possession of her. “You know, Jerry,
en|I don’t say it to complain--complainin’ never seems to bring one any good to a
en|woman like me; but--if you only knowed how folks look at me in--in stores, an’
en|everywhere else, you--wouldn’t blame me for not likin’ it. _I_ didn’t ever do anything to
en|bring it about, unless ’twas in marryin’ _you_, and I _ain’t_ sorry I did _that_;
en|but I wish I didn’t ever have to see anybody again, if you’re goin’ to
en|keep on drinkin’.” The sick man fell back and was silent; his wife threw herself
en|beside him, crying, “Don’t get mad at me, Jerry; God knows it’s the deadest truth.”
en|After a moment or two Tappelmine laid a hand on his wife’s cheek, where it
en|had not been before for twenty years; once its touch had brought blushes; now, tears
en|hurried down to meet it, and yet Mrs. Tappelmine was happier than when she had
en|been a pretty Kentucky girl, twenty years before. “Mariar,” said Tappelmine at last, “I’ve dragged
en|you all down.” “No, you haven’t, Jerry,” asserted Mrs. Tappelmine, with a lie which she
en|could not avoid. “If dyin’ll help you up again, I’m willin’,” continued Tappelmine. The apartments
en|in the Tappelmine mansion were so few that it was impossible for anything unusual to
en|transpire without attracting the attention of all the inmates; so it followed that the children,
en|beholding the actions of their parents, had gradually approached the bed with countenances whose blankness
en|was painfully eloquent to the sick man. Tappelmine looked at them, and grew more miserable
en|of visage; he hid his face beside his wife, groaned “No more whiskey if I
en|die for it!” and jumped up and kissed each of his children, while Mrs. Tappelmine
en|sobbed aloud, and Father Baguss, who, coming over a few moments before to talk business,
en|had heard the simple word “whiskey,” and had since been jealously listening under the window,
en|sneaked away muttering to himself, “After all I’ve done for him, I can’t even say
en|to myself that _I_ saved him.” CHAPTER XVII. THE CONCLUSION OF THE WHOLE MATTER The
en|fire which destroyed the Mississippi Valley Woolen Mills did such damage in the ranks of
en|the temperance reformers that for a few months Crupp, Tomple, and several others had frequent
en|cause to feel lonesome, while poor Father Baguss fell back upon the church for that
en|comfort which, just after his first effort with Tappelmine, and before the fire, he had
en|frequently found in the society of his self-approving brother stockholders. The mill was rebuilt, only
en|a few of the owners of stock refusing to be assessed for their proportion of
en|the loss; the mill made a very prosperous winter, and interested persons were not averse
en|to talking about it; but after Deacon Jones’ speech was noised abroad, the mill was
en|no longer a semi-holy topic of conversation, which was allowable even on the church steps
en|on Sundays. Some of the men whose eyes had been opened toward themselves, on the
en|occasion of the fire, were honest enough to confess to themselves, and to bring forth
en|fruits meet for repentance; but the majority took refuge either in open or secret sophistry,
en|with the comforting impression that they blinded others as effectually as they did themselves. The
en|mass of the people, however--those who neither subscribed to temperance funds, nor mill stock, nor
en|anything else, still looked on, and were plethoric of encouragement and criticism. When appealed to
en|for help, their logic was simply bewildering, and almost as depraved as the same defensive
en|and offensive weapon is in politics. Tomple was the man to do such work, said
en|some, for he was the rich man of the village, and rich men are only
en|God’s stewards; others suggested Captain Crayme, who had money, and who should be willing to
en|spend considerable of it as a thank-offering for his own providential deliverance from the thraldom
en|of drink. The irreligious thought that all such work should be done by the church,
en|if churches were good for anything but to shout in; while the religious felt that
en|the irreligious, among whom could be found nearly every drinker in the village, should expend
en|whatever money was needed for the physical reformation of their kind. Where none of these
en|excuses seemed available, or wherever two or three conservatives of differing views met together, there
en|was always Crupp to fall back upon; each man could grasp his own pocket-book with
en|tender tenacity, and declare to a sympathetic audience that the man who had coined his
en|money out of widows’ tears and orphans’ groans should by rights take care of all
en|the drunkards in the county, even until he was so reduced in means as to
en|be dependent upon public charity for his own support. Thus matters stood when a year
en|had elapsed since the memorable temperance meeting, and Parson Wedgewell suggested that an anniversary service
en|would be only an ordinary and decent testimonial of respect to Providence for his special
en|mercies during the year. To the parson’s surprise, Crupp who--though he had during the winter
en|surprised every one by joining Parson Wedgewell’s church, in spite of a very severe course
en|of questioning by the Examining Committee--was still a man of action and a contemner of
en|mere words--Crupp not only failed to oppose such a meeting, but volunteered himself to write
en|for Major Ben Bailey, the gifted orator who had addressed the earlier meeting, and to
en|pay the orator’s expenses. Such offers were rarely made, even by the Barton reformers, so
en|by unanimous consent Crupp wrote to the great lecturer, it being admitted by Tomple, Wedgewell,
en|Baguss, and Jones, that Crupp’s idea of informing the Major what had been done during
en|the year was a good one, and that it would enable the orator to modify
en|his address with special reference to existing circumstances. But Squire Tomple and the parson were
en|considerably astonished to see Crupp dash into the Squire’s store one day, exhibiting an unusual
en|degree of excitement, as he unfolded a letter and remarked, “He won’t come! Just listen
en|to what he says!” And while the two other reformers stood as if they saw
en|the sky falling and did not despair of catching it in their eyes and mouths,
en|Crupp read: “In replying to Mr. Crupp’s favor of the --th, Major Bailey can only
en|say, that while he should be glad to again meet the people among whom so
en|great an amount of good has been accomplished within the year, he cannot see that
en|he can render any service. Major Bailey’s efforts are confined solely to the awakening of
en|an interest in temperance; the condition of affairs which Mr. Crupp reports as existing in
en|Barton, however, indicates a degree of interest which cannot be heightened by any effort which
en|the writer could put forth. What seems desirable at Barton is such an informing of
en|the general populace upon what has been accomplished, upon the manner in which the work
en|has been done, and the comparatively small number of persons who have actively participated in
en|it, as shall convince the inhabitants that they did not fulfill their whole duty toward
en|temperance when a year ago they applauded the utterances of the writer of these lines.
en|Briefly, Major Bailey feels that if he attended, he could contribute only such efforts as,
en|under the circumstances, would be entirely out of place.” “Astonishing!” exclaimed Parson Wedgewell, with the
en|eye of a man who dreams. “Threw away a job!” said Tomple, like the thrifty
en|business man that he was. But the meeting was planned and widely advertised, and when,
en|on the evening appointed, the attendants looked over the room, they found occasion for considerable
en|attentive reflection. Except that Major Ben Bailey, the gifted orator, was not present, the meeting
en|presented the same attractions which had drawn such a crowd to its predecessor. The Barton
en|Brass Band was there, and with some new airs learned during the year; the Crystal
en|Spring Glee Club was there; there were the pastors of the four churches in Barton,
en|and Squire Tomple was in the chair as before. Besides, there were additional attractions: Crupp,
en|a year before, the man who was lending to liquor selling an air of respectability,
en|was upon the platform to the left and rear of Squire Tomple; old Bunley, who
en|a year before had been responsible only as a container of alcohol, but now a
en|respectable citizen and book-keeper to Squire Tomple, occupied the secretary’s chair; Tom Adams acted as
en|usher in one of the side-aisles, and dragged all the heavy drinkers up to front
en|seats; Harry Wainright was there, with a wife whose veil was not thick enough to
en|hide her happiness; Fred Macdonald, who had spent the evening of the other meeting in
en|the Barton House bar-room, was there; so was Tappelmine, appearing as ill at ease as
en|a porker in a strange field, but still there; while in a side seat, close
en|to the wall, sitting as much in the shadow of his wife as possible, so
en|as to guard his professional reputation, was Sam Crayme, captain of the steamer _Excellence_. A
en|number of “the boys” were there also, and yet the church was not only not
en|crowded, but not even full. During the year temperance had been guided from the hearts
en|to the pockets of a great many, and this radical treatment had been fatal to
en|many an enthusiastic soul that had theretofore been blameless in its own eyes. Those who
en|attended heard some music, however, which was not deficient in point of quality; they heard
en|a short but live address from old Parson Fish on the moral beauty of a
en|temperate life, and an earnest prayer from that one of the Barton pastors who had
en|during the year done nothing which justified the mention of his name in this history,
en|and then the audience saw Mr. Crupp advance to the front of the platform and
en|unfold a large sheet of paper, which he crumpled in one hand as he spoke
en|as follows: “Ladies and gentlemen: having been requested, by the chairman of the last meeting,
en|to collect some statistics of the work accomplished in Barton, during the past year, in
en|the cause of temperance, I invite your attention to the following figures: “Population of township
en|last year, three thousand two hundred and sixty-five. Signatures to pledge, at last meeting, six
en|hundred and twenty-seven [applause]; signatures of persons who were in the habit of drinking at
en|time of signing, two hundred and thirty-one; number of persons who have broken the pledge
en|since signing, one hundred and sixty [sighs and groans]; number of persons who have kept
en|their pledges, seventy-one [applause]; number reclaimed by personal effort since meeting, forty-six [applause]; amount of
en|money subscribed and applied strictly for the good of the cause, and without hope of
en|pecuniary gain [a faint hiss or two], five thousand one hundred and ninety dollars and
en|thirty-eight cents [tremendous applause]; amount which has been returned by the beneficiaries without solicitation, twenty-seven
en|dollars [laughter, hisses, and groans]. Of the amount subscribed, _six-sevenths_ came from _five_ persons, who
en|own less than _one-fiftieth_ part of the taxable property of the township.” The quiet which
en|prevailed, as Mr. Crupp spoke these last words and took his seat, was, if considered
en|only _as_ quiet, simply faultless; but its duration was greater and more annoying than things
en|purely faultless usually are, and there was a general sensation of relief when Squire Tomple,
en|who during the year had not made any public display of his charities, and who
en|was popularly supposed to care as much for a dollar as any one, slowly got
en|upon his feet. “My friends,” said the Squire, “I’m more than ever convinced that temperance
en|is a good thing [hearty applause], and the reason I feel so is, that during
en|the year I’ve put considerable money into it; and where the treasure is there shall
en|the heart be also [dead silence]. I’ve made up my mind, that hurrahing and singing
en|for temperance will make a hypocrite out of a saint, if he don’t use money
en|and effort at the same time. I like a good song and a good time
en|as much as anybody, but I can’t learn of a single drinking man that they
en|have reformed. At our last meeting there was some good work _started_, by the use
en|of songs and speeches, and you have learned, from the report just presented, how much
en|lasting good they did. Money and work have done the business, my friends; talk has
en|helped, but alone by itself it’s done precious little. This lesson has cost _me_ a
en|great deal; and as a business man, who believes that _every_ earthly interest is in
en|some way a business interest, I advise you to learn the same lesson for yourselves
en|before it is too late.” Such a pail of cold water had never before been
en|thrown upon Barton hearts aglow with confidence, it struck the leader of the band so
en|forcibly that he rattled off into “Yankee Doodle,” to aid the meeting in recovering its
en|spirits; even after listening to this inspiriting air, however, it was with a wistfulness almost
en|desperate that the audience scanned the countenance of Parson Wedgewell as he stepped to the
en|front of the platform. “Beloved friends,” said the parson, “the result of the past year’s
en|work in this portion of the Lord’s vineyard has indeed been richly blessed, and I
en|shall ever count it as one of the precious privileges of my life that I
en|have been permitted to take part in it. [‘Hurrah for the parson!’ shouted a man,
en|who had but a moment before worn a most lugubrious countenance.] I rejoice, not only
en|that I have seen precious sheaves brought to our Lord’s granary, but also because I
en|have beheld going into the field those who have heretofore stood idly in the market-place,
en|and because I have beheld the reapers themselves receiving the reward of their labors. They
en|have received souls for their hire, dear friends, and I feel constrained to admit that
en|if each of those who came in at the eleventh hour received as much as
en|us, who have apparently borne the burden and heat of the day, they were fully
en|entitled to it by reason of the greater intelligence and industry which they have displayed.
en|For many years, my dear friends, I have been among you as one sent by
en|the Physician of souls; but it is only within the past year that I have
en|begun to comprehend that the soul may be treated--very often _should_ be treated--through the body;
en|and that, though the fervent effectual prayer of the righteous man availeth much, the exercise
en|of that which was made in the likeness and image of God is not to
en|be idle. The mammon of unrighteousness has been made the salvation of many, my dear
en|friends; and it has, I verily believe, guided toward heavenly habitations those who have applied
en|it to the necessities of others. But, dear brethren, the harvest truly is plenteous, but
en|the laborers are few; pray ye, therefore, the Lord of the harvest that he will
en|send forth laborers unto his harvest; but take heed that ye follow the example of
en|him, who, as he commanded us thus to petition the throne of grace, ceased not
en|to labor in the harvest field himself; who fed when he preached, and healed when
en|he exhorted.” Harry Wainright pounded on the floor with his cane, hearing which, Tom Adams
en|brought his enormous hands together with great emphasis, and his example was dutifully followed by
en|the whole of his own family, which filled two short side seats. Father Baguss shouted
en|“Glory to God!” and Deacon Jones ejaculated “That’s so!” but the hearers seemed disposed to
en|be critical, although the parson’s address had been couched in language almost exclusively Scriptural. While
en|they were engaged in contemplation, however, old Bunley dropped a mellow cough and stepped to
en|the front. “Ladies and gentlemen,” said he, “it’s the style in this town, and everywhere
en|else, I suppose, to kick a man when he’s down, and then to trample on
en|him. I know _one_ man that’s been there, and knows all about it. ’Twas his
en|own fault he got there, and there were plenty who told him he ought to
en|get up; but how kicking and trampling were to help him do it he could
en|never see, and he made up his mind, that folks did as they did because
en|it suited them, not because it was going to do _him_ any good. So he’s
en|been hating the whole townful for years, and doing all the harm he could, not
en|because he liked doing harm, but because he never got a chance to do anything
en|else. Suddenly, a couple of gentlemen--I won’t mention names--came along, and gave the poor fellow
en|a hand, and gave him the first chance he’s had in years to believe in
en|human nature at all. And, all this time, everybody else around him was acting in
en|the way that this same poor fellow would have acted himself, if he had wanted
en|to play devil. The same couple of gentlemen went for a good many other people,
en|and acted in a way that you read about in novels and the Bible (but
en|mighty seldom see in town); and those fellows believe in these two gentlemen, now, but
en|they hate all the rest of you like poison. I don’t suppose you like it,
en|but truth is truth; you might as well know what it is.” Several people got
en|up and went out, carrying very red faces with them; but Fred Macdonald stood up
en|and clapped his hands, and the Adams family and Wainright helped him, while the broad
en|boots of Father Baguss raised a cloud of dust, which formed quite an aureole about
en|Baguss himself as he got up and remarked: “Brethren and sisters: Squire Tomple hit the
en|nail exactly on the head when he said that hollerin’ an’ singin’ makes a hypocrite
en|of a man if he don’t open his pocket-book. If you don’t believe it, remember
en|me. If anybody ever liked his own more’n I did, he’s a curiosity. I don’t
en|_hate_ money a bit now, an’ I’m not goin’ to try to; but the hardest
en|case I ever got acquainted with was me, Zedekiah Baguss, when I couldn’t dodge it
en|any longer that I ought to spend money for a feller-critter. I won’t name no
en|names, brethren an’ sisters; but if you’re huntin’ for any such game, don’t go to
en|lookin’ up drunkards until you smell around near home fust.” “Reputation be blowed higher than
en|a kite!” exclaimed Captain Crayme, springing to his feet; “but I’ve got to say just
en|a word here. Gentlemen, I’m off my whiskey, and I’m going to stay off; but
en|I might be drinking yet, and have kept on forever, for all that any of
en|you that’s so pious and temperate ever cared. But one man thought enough of me
en|to come and talk to me--talk like a man, and not preach a sermon; more
en|than that, he not only talked--which the biggest idiot here might have done just as
en|well--but he stuck by me, and he brought me through. Any of you might have
en|done it, but none of you cared enough for me, and yet I’m a business
en|man, and I’ve got some property. How any _poor_ fellow down in the mud is
en|ever to get up again, in such a place, I don’t see; and yet Barton’s
en|as good a town as I ever touch at.” The interest of the meeting was
en|departing, so were the attendants; but the Reverend Timotheus Brown limped forward and exclaimed: “Hear,
en|then, the conclusion of the whole matter: ‘Not every one that sayeth Lord, Lord, shall
en|inherit the kingdom of heaven, but him that doeth the will of my Father which
en|is in heaven.’ There has been a blessed change wrought in this town within a
en|year, and work has done it all. He who taught us to say ‘Our Father,’
en|made of every man his brother’s keeper, and no amount of talk can undo what
en|He did. A few men in our midst have recognized their duty and have done
en|it, or are doing it; most of them, among them him who addresses you, have
en|learned that the beginning is the hardest part of the work, and that the laborer
en|receives his hire, though never in the way in which he expects it. Much remains
en|to be done, not only in raising the fallen, but in reforming the upright; and,
en|to get a full and fair view of the latter, there is no way so
en|successful as to go to work for others.” Squire Tomple announced that the meeting was
en|still open for remarks; but, no one else availing themselves of the privilege offered, the
en|evening closed with a spirited medley from the brass band. Not every one was silent
en|and dismal, however; as the church emptied, Tomple, Bunley, Crupp, Wedgewell, Brown, and the other
en|pastors came down from the platform, and were met at the foot of the steps
en|by Baguss and Deacon Jones, and there was a general hand-shaking. Tom Adams stood afar
en|off, looking curiously and wistfully at the party, noticing which, Parson Wedgewell danced excitedly up
en|to him, and dragged him into the circle; there Tom received a greeting which somehow
en|educated him, in two or three minutes, to a point far beyond any that his
en|head or heart had previously reached. Then Fred Macdonald, who had intended to avoid any
en|action which might seem to make him one of the “old fellows” of the village,
en|suddenly lost his head in some manner which he could not explain, and hurried off,
en|caught Sam Crayme’s arm, and destroyed such reputation as remained to the captain along the
en|river, by bringing the enterprising navigator into such a circle as he had never entered
en|before, but in which he soon found himself as much at home as if he
en|had been born there. Others, too--not many in number, to be sure--but representing most of
en|the soul Of the village, straggled timidly up to the group, and were informally admitted
en|to what was not conventionally a love-feast, but approached nearer to one than any formal
en|gathering could have done. Barton has never since known a monster temperance meeting; but the
en|few righteous men who dwell therein have proved to their own satisfaction, and that of
en|certain one-time wretches, that, in a successful temperance movement, the reform must begin among those
en|who never drink. End of the Project Gutenberg EBook of The Barton Experiment, by John
