en|36 ESSEX STREET, W.C. LONDON 1897 THE LADY’S WALK CHAPTER I I was on a
en|visit to some people in Scotland when the events I am about to relate took
en|place. They were not friends in the sense of long or habitual intercourse; in short,
en|I had met them only in Switzerland in the previous year; but we saw a
en|great deal of each other while we were together, and got into that easy intimacy
en|which travelling brings about more readily than anything else. We had seen each other in
en|very great _déshabillé_ both of mind and array in the chilly mornings after a night’s
en|travelling, which perhaps is the severest test that can be applied in respect to looks,
en|and amid all the annoyances of journeys short and long, with the usual episodes of
en|lost luggage, indifferent hotels, fusses of every description, which is an equally severe test for
en|the temper; and our friendship and liking (I am at liberty to suppose it was
en|mutual, or they would never have invited me to Ellermore) remained unimpaired. I have always
en|thought, and still think, that Charlotte Campbell was one of the most charming young women
en|I ever met with; and her brothers, if not so entirely delightful, were nice fellows,
en|capital to travel with, full of fun and spirit. I understood immediately from their conversation
en|that they were members of a large family. Their allusions to Tom and Jack and
en|little Harry, and to Mab and Mary, might perhaps have been tedious to a harsher
en|critic; but I like to hear of other people’s relations, having scarcely any of my
en|own. I found out by degrees that Miss Campbell had been taken abroad by her
en|brothers to recover from a long and severe task of nursing, which had exhausted her
en|strength. The little ones had all been down with scarlet fever, and she had not
en|left them night or day. “She gave up seeing the rest of us and regularly
en|shut herself in,” Charley informed me, who was the younger of the two. “She would
en|only go out for her walk when all of us were out of the way.
en|That was the worst of it,” the young fellow said, with great simplicity. That his
en|sister should give herself up to the nursing was nothing remarkable; but that she should
en|deny herself their precious company was a heroism that went to her brother’s heart. Thus
en|by the way I learned a great deal about the family. Chatty, as they called
en|her, was the sister-mother, especially of the little ones, who had been left almost in
en|her sole charge since their mother died many years before. She was not a girl,
en|strictly speaking. She was in the perfection of her womanhood and youth--about eight-and-twenty, the age
en|when something of the composure of maturity has lighted upon the sweetness of the earlier
en|years, and being so old enhances all the charm of being so young. It is
en|chiefly among young married women that one sees this gracious and beautiful type, delightful to
en|every sense and every requirement of the mind; but when it is to be met
en|with unmarried it is more celestial still. I cannot but think with reverence that this
en|delicate maternity and maidenhood--the perfect bounty of the one, the undisturbed grace of the other--has
en|been the foundation of that adoring devotion which in the old days brought so many
en|saints to the shrine of the Virgin Mother. But why I should thus enlarge upon
en|Charlotte Campbell at the beginning of this story I can scarcely tell, for she is
en|not the foremost figure in it, and I am unintentionally deceiving the reader to begin
en|with. They asked me to come and see them at Ellermore when we parted, and,
en|as I have nothing in the way of a home warmer or more genial than
en|chambers in the Temple, I accepted, as may be supposed, with enthusiasm. It was in
en|the first week of June that we parted, and I was invited for the end
en|of August. They had “plenty of grouse,” Charley said, with a liberality of expression which
en|was pleasant to hear. Charlotte added, “But you must be prepared for a homely life,
en|Mr. Temple, and a very quiet one.” I replied, of course, that if I had
en|chosen what I liked best in the world it would have been this combination, at
en|which she smiled with an amused little shake of her head. It did not seem
en|to occur to her that she herself told for much in the matter. What they
en|all insisted upon was the “plenty of grouse,” and I do not pretend to say
en|that I was indifferent to that. Colin, the eldest son, was the one with whom
en|I had been least familiar. He was what people call reserved. He did not talk
en|of everything as the others did. I did not indeed find out till much later
en|that he was constantly in London, coming and going, so that he and I might
en|have seen much of each other. Yet he liked me well enough. He joined warmly
en|in his brother’s invitation. When Charley said there was plenty of grouse, he added, with
en|the utmost friendliness, “And ye may get a blaze at a stag.” There was a
en|flavour of the North in the speech of all; not disclosed by mere words, but
en|by an occasional diversity of idiom and change of pronunciation. They were conscious of this
en|and rather proud of it than otherwise. They did not say Scotch, but Scots; and
en|their accent could not be represented by any of the travesties of the theatre, or
en|what we conventionally accept as the national utterance. When I attempted to pronounce after them,
en|my own ear informed me what a travesty it was. It was to the family
en|represented by these young people that I was going when I started on August 20,
en|a blazing summer day, with dust and heat enough to merit the name of summer
en|if anything ever did. But when I arrived at my journey’s end there was just
en|change enough to mark the line between summer and autumn: a little golden haze in
en|the air, a purple bloom of heather on the hills, a touch here and there
en|upon a stray branch, very few, yet enough to swear by. Ellermore lay in the
en|heart of a beautiful district full of mountains and lochs within the Highland line, and
en|just on the verge of some of the wildest mountain scenery in Scotland. It was
en|situated in the midst of an amphitheatre of hills, not of any very exalted height,
en|but of the most picturesque form, with peaks and couloirs like an Alpine range in
en|little, all glowing with the purple blaze of the heather, with gleams upon them that
en|looked like snow, but were in reality water-white threads of mountain torrents. In front of
en|the house was a small loch embosomed in the hills, from one end of which
en|ran a cheerful little stream, much intercepted by boulders, and much the brighter for its
en|interruptions, which meandered through the glen and fell into another loch of greater grandeur and
en|pretensions. Ellermore itself was a comparatively new house, built upon a fine slope of lawn
en|over the lake, and sheltered by fine trees--great beeches which would not have done discredit
en|to Berkshire, though that is not what we expect to see in Scotland, besides the
en|ashes and firs which we are ready to acknowledge as of northern growth. I was
en|not prepared for the luxuriance of the West Highlands--the mantling green of ferns and herbage
en|everywhere, not to say the wealth of flowers, which formed a centre of still more
en|brilliant colour and cultivation amid all the purple of the hills. Everything was soft and
en|rich and warm about the Highland mansion-house. I had expected stern scenery and a grey
en|atmosphere. I found an almost excessive luxuriance of vegetation and colour everywhere. The father of
en|my friends received me at a door which was constantly open, and where it seemed
en|to me after a while that nobody was ever refused admission. He was a tall
en|old man, dignified but homely, with white hair and moustache and the fresh colour of
en|a rural patriarch; which, however, he was not, but an energetic man of business, as
en|I afterwards found. The Campbells of Ellermore were not great chiefs in that much-extended clan,
en|though they were perfectly well-known people and had held their little estate from remote antiquity.
en|But they had not stood upon their gentility, or refused to avail themselves of the
en|opportunities that came in their way. I have observed that in the great and wealthy
en|region of which Glasgow is the capital the number of the irreconcilables who stand out
en|against trade is few. The gentry have seen all the advantages of combining commerce with
en|tradition. Had it not been for this it is likely that Ellermore would have been
en|a very different place. Now it was overflowing with all those signs of care and
en|simple luxury which make life so smooth. There was little show, but there was a
en|profusion of comfort. Everything rolled upon velvet. It was perhaps more like the house of
en|a rich merchant than of a family of long descent. Nothing could be more perfect
en|as a pleasure estate than was this little Highland property. They had “plenty of grouse,”
en|and also of trout in a succession of little lochs and mountain streams. They had
en|deer on the hills. They had their own mutton, and everything vegetable that was needed
en|for the large, profuse household, from potatoes and cabbage up to grapes and peaches. But
en|with all this primitive wealth there was not much money got out of Ellermore. The
en|“works” in Glasgow supplied that. What the works were I have never exactly found out,
en|but they afforded occupation for all the family, both father and sons; and that the
en|results were of the most pleasing description as regarded Mr. Campbell’s banker it was easy
en|to see. They were all at home with the exception of Colin, the eldest son,
en|for whose absence many apologies, some of which seemed much more elaborate than were at
en|all necessary, were made to me. I was, for my own part, quite indifferent to
en|the absence of Colin. He was not the one who had interested me most; and
en|though Charley was considerably younger than myself, I had liked him better from the first.
en|Tom and Jack were still younger. They were well occupied at “the works,” and came
en|home only from Saturday to Monday. The little trio in the nursery were delightful children.
en|To see them gathered about Charlotte was enough to melt any heart. Chatty, they called
en|her, which is not a very dignified name, but I got to think it the
en|most beautiful in the world as it sounded all over that cheerful, much-populated house. “Where
en|is Chatty?” was the first question everyone asked as he came in at the door.
en|If she was not immediately found, it went volleying through the house, all up the
en|stairs and through the passages--“Chatty! where are you?”--and was always answered from somewhere or other
en|in a full, soft voice, which was audible everywhere though it never was loud. “Here
en|am I, boys,” she would say, with a pretty inversion which pleased me. Indeed, everything
en|pleased me in Chatty--too much, more than reason. I found myself thinking what would become
en|of them all if, for example, she were to marry, and entered into a hot
en|argument with myself on one occasion by way of proving that it would be the
en|most selfish thing in the world were this family to work upon Chatty’s feelings and
en|prevent her from marrying, as most probably, I could not help feeling, they would. At
en|the same time, I perceived with a little shudder how entirely the whole thing would
en|collapse if by any chance Chatty should be decoyed away. I enjoyed my stay beyond
en|description. In the morning we were out on the hills or about the country. In
en|the evening it very often happened that we all strolled out after dinner, and that
en|I was left by Chatty’s side, “the boys” having a thousand objects of interest, while
en|Mr. Campbell usually sat in his library and read the newspapers, which arrived at that
en|time either by the coach from Oban or by the boat. In this way I
en|went over the whole “policy,” as the grounds surrounding a country house are called in
en|Scotland, with Chatty, who would not be out of reach at this hour, lest her
en|father should want her, or the children. She would bid me not to stay with
en|her, when no doubt it would be more amusing for me to go with the
en|boys; and when I assured her my pleasure was far greater as it was, she
en|gave me a gracious, frank smile, with a little shake of her head. She laughed
en|at me softly, bidding me not to be too polite or think she would mind
en|if I left her; but I think, on the whole, she liked to have me
en|with her in her evening walk. “There is one thing you have not told me
en|of,” I said, “and that you must possess. I cannot believe that your family has
en|been settled here so long without having a ghost.” She had turned round to look
en|at me, to know what it was that had been omitted in her descriptions. When
en|she heard what it was she smiled a little, but not with the pleasant mockery
en|I had expected. On the contrary, it was a sort of gentle smile of recognition
en|of something left out. “We don’t call it a ghost,” she said. “I have wondered
en|if you had never noticed. I am fond of it, for my part; but then
en|I have been used to it all my life. And here we are, then,” she
en|added, as we reached the top of a little ascent and came out upon a
en|raised avenue, which I had known by its name of the Lady’s Walk, without as
en|yet getting any explanation what that meant. It must have been, I supposed, the avenue
en|to the old house, and now encircled one portion of the grounds without any distinct
en|meaning. On the side nearest the gardens and house it was but slightly raised above
en|the shrubberies, but on the other side rose to the summit of a high bank,
en|sloping steeply to the river, which, after it escaped from the loch, made a wide
en|bend round that portion of the grounds. A row of really grand beeches rose on
en|each side of the path, and through the openings in the trees the house, the
en|bright gardens, the silvery gleam of the loch were visible. The evening sun was slanting
en|into our eyes as we walked along; a little soft yet brisk air was pattering
en|among the leaves, and here and there a yellow cluster in the middle of a
en|branch showing the first touch of a cheerful decay. “Here we are, then.” It was
en|a curious phrase; but there are some odd idioms in the Scotch--I mean Scots--form of
en|our common language, and I had become accustomed now to accept them without remark. “I
en|suppose,” I said, “there must be some back way to the village or to the
en|farmhouse under this bank, though there seems no room for a path?” “Why do you
en|ask?” she said, looking at me with a smile. “Because I always hear someone passing
en|along--I imagine down there. The steps are very distinct. Don’t you hear them now? It
en|has puzzled me a good deal, for I cannot make out where the path can
en|be.” She smiled again, with a meaning in her smile, and looked at me steadily,
en|listening, as I was. And then, after a pause, she said, “That is what you
en|are asking for. If we did not hear them it would make us unhappy. Did
en|you never hear why this was called the Lady’s Walk?” When she said these words
en|I was conscious of an odd enough change in my sensations--nay, I should say in
en|my very sense of hearing, which was the one appealed to. I had heard the
en|sound often, and, after looking back at first to see who it was and seeing
en|no one, had made up my mind that the steps were on some byway out
en|of sight and came from below. Now my hearing changed, and I could not understand
en|how I had ever thought anything else; the steps were on a level with us,
en|by our side--as if some third person were accompanying us along the avenue. I am
en|no believer in ghosts, nor the least superstitious, so far as I had ever been
en|aware (more than everybody is), but I felt myself get out of the way with
en|great celerity and a certain thrill of curious sensation. The idea of rubbing shoulders with
en|something unseen gave me a shock in spite of myself. “Ah,” said Charlotte, “it gives
en|you an--unpleasant feeling. I forgot you are not used to it like me.” “I am
en|tolerably well used to it, for I have heard it often,” I said. It was
en|cowardly to get to the other side, but I fear I did so with an
en|involuntary movement. Then I laughed, which I felt to be altogether out of place and
en|fictitious, and said, “No doubt there is some very easy explanation of it--some vibration or
en|echo. The science of acoustics clears up many mysteries.” “There is no explanation,” Chatty said
en|almost angrily. “She has walked here far longer than anyone can remember. It is an
en|ill sign for us Campbells when she goes away. She was the eldest daughter, like
en|me; and I think she has got to be our guardian angel. There is no
en|harm going to happen as long as she is here. Listen to her!” she cried,
en|standing still with her hand raised. The low sun shone full on her, catching her
en|brown hair, the lucid clearness of her brown eyes, her cheeks so clear and soft,
en|in colour a little summer-brown too. I stood and listened with a something of excited
en|feeling which I could not control. If I had followed my first impulse I am
en|not sure that I should not have bolted through the shrubbery; but of course I
en|did not. And the sound of this third person, whose steps were not to be
en|mistaken though she was unseen, made my heart beat. It was no doubt the utmost
en|folly; for there must be an explanation for it in nature: of that I could
en|not doubt for a moment. “You are startled,” she said, with a smile. “Well, I
en|should not be acting my part, should I, as I ought, if I did not
en|feel the proper thrill. It must be disrespectful to a ghost not to be afraid.”
en|“Don’t say a ghost,” said Chatty; “I think _that_ is disrespectful. It is the Lady
en|of Ellermore; everybody knows about her. And do you know,” she added, “when my mother
en|died--the greatest grief I have ever known--the steps ceased? Oh, it is true! You need
en|not look me in the face as if there was anything to laugh at. It
en|is ten years ago, and I was only a silly sort of girl, not much
en|good to anyone. They sent me out to get the air when she was lying
en|in a doze; and I came here. I was crying, as you may suppose, and
en|at first I did not pay any attention. Then it struck me all at once--the
en|Lady was away. They told me afterwards that was the worst sign. It is always
en|death that is coming when she goes away.” The pathos of this incident confused all
en|my attempts to touch it with levity, and we went on for a little without
en|speaking, during which time it is almost unnecessary to say that I was listening with
en|all my might to those strange footsteps, which finally I persuaded myself were no more
en|than echoes of our own. “It is very curious,” I said politely. “Of course you
en|were greatly agitated and too much absorbed in real grief to have any time to
en|think of any explanation--the state of the atmosphere perhaps”-- She gave me an indignant look.
en|We were nearly at the end of the walk, and at that moment I could
en|have sworn that the footsteps, which had got a little in advance, here turned and
en|met us coming back. I am aware that nothing could sound more foolish, and that
en|it could only be some vibration or atmospheric phenomenon. But yet this was how it
en|seemed: it was not an optical but an aural delusion. So long as the steps
en|were going with us it was less impossible to account for it; but when they
en|turned and audibly came back to meet us! Not all my strength of mind could
en|prevent me from springing aside to let them pass. This time they came directly between
en|us, and the agility of my start and withdrawal was naturally much more significant than
en|the faltering laugh with which I excused myself. “It is a very curious sound indeed,”
en|I said, with a tremor which slightly affected my voice. Chatty gave me a reassuring
en|smile. She did not laugh at me, which was consolatory. She stood for a moment
en|as if looking after the visionary passenger. “We are not afraid,” she said, “even the
en|youngest; we all know she is our friend.” When we had got back to the
en|side of the loch, where, I confess, I was pleased to find myself, in the
en|free open air without any perplexing shadow of trees, I felt less objection to the
en|subject. “I wish you would tell me the story; for of course there is a
en|story?” I said. “No, there is no story--at least nothing tragical or even romantic. They
en|say she was the eldest daughter. I sometimes wonder,” Chatty said, with a smile and
en|a faint increase of colour, “whether she might not be a little like me. She
en|lived here all her life, and had several generations to take care of. Oh no,
en|there was no murder or wrong about our Lady; she just loved Ellermore above everything;
en|and my idea is that she has been allowed the care of us ever since.”
en|“That is very sweet, to have the care of you,” I said, scarcely venturing to
en|put any emphasis on the pronoun; “but, after all, it must be slow work, don’t
en|you think, walking up and down there for ever? I call that a poor sort
en|of reward for a good woman. If she had been a bad one, it might
en|have answered very well for a punishment.” “Mr. Temple!” Chatty said, now reddening with indignation,
en|“do you think it is a poor thing to have the care of your own
en|people, to watch over them, whatever may happen--to be all for them and their service?
en|I don’t think so; I should like to have such a fate.” Perhaps I had
en|spoken thus on purpose to bring about the discussion. “There is such a thing as
en|being too devoted to a family. Are they ever grateful? They go away and marry
en|and leave you in the lurch.” She looked up at me with a little astonishment.
en|“The members may vary, but the family never goes away,” she said; “besides, that can
en|apply to us in our present situation only. _She_ must have seen so many come
en|and go; but that need not vex her, you know, because they go where she
en|is.” “My dear Miss Campbell, wait a bit; think a little,” I said. “Where she
en|is! That is in the Lady’s Walk, according to your story. Let us hope that
en|all your ancestors and relations are not there.” “I suppose you want to make me
en|angry,” said Chatty. “She is in heaven--have you any doubt of that?--but every day when
en|the sun is setting she comes back home.” “Oh, come!” I said, “if it is
en|only at the sunset, that is not so bad.” Miss Campbell looked at me doubtfully,
en|as if not knowing whether to be angry. “You want to make fun of it,”
en|she said, “to laugh at it; and yet,” she added, with a little spirit, “you
en|were very nervous half an hour ago.” “I acknowledge to being nervous. I am very
en|impressionable. I believe that is the word. It is a luxury like another to be
en|nervous at the fit moment. Frightened, you might say, if you prefer plain speaking. And
en|I am very glad it is at sunset, not in the dark. This completes the
en|circle of my Highland experiences,” I said; “everything now is perfect. I have shot grouse
en|on the hill and caught trout on the loch, and been soaked to the skin
en|and then dried in the wind; I wanted nothing but the family ghost. And now
en|I have seen her, or at least heard her”-- “If you are resolved to make
en|a joke of it I cannot help it,” said Chatty, “but I warn you that
en|it is not agreeable to me, Mr. Temple. Let us talk of something else. In
en|the Highlands,” she said, with dignity, “we take different views of many things.” “There are
en|some things,” I said, “of which but one view is possible--that I should have the
en|audacity and impertinence to laugh at anything for which you have a veneration! I believe
en|it is only because I was so frightened”-- She smiled again in her lovely motherly
en|way, a smile of indulgence and forgiveness and bounty. “You are too humble now,” she
en|said, “and I think I hear someone calling me. It is time to go in.”
en|And to be sure there was someone calling her; there always was, I think, at
en|all hours of the night and day. CHAPTER II To say that I got rid
en|of the recollection of the Lady of Ellermore when I went upstairs, after a cheerful
en|evening, through a long and slippery gallery to my room in the wing, would be
en|untrue. The curious experience I had just had dwelt in my mind with a touch
en|of not unpleasant perplexity. “Of course,” I said to myself, “there must be something to
en|account for those footsteps--some hidden way in which the sounds must come.” Perhaps my first
en|idea would turn out to be correct--that there was a byroad to the farm or
en|to the stables, which in some states of the atmosphere, or perhaps it might even
en|be always, echoed back the sounds of passing feet in some subterranean vibration. One has
en|heard of such things, one has heard, indeed, of every kind of natural wonder, some
en|of them no more easy to explain than the other interpretation; but so long as
en|you have science with you, whether you understand it or not, you are all right.
en|I could not help wondering, however, whether, if by chance I heard those steps in
en|the long gallery outside my door, I should refer the matter comfortably to the science
en|of acoustics. I was tormented, until I fell asleep, by a vague expectation of hearing
en|them. I could not get them out of my mind or out of my ears,
en|so distinct were they--the light step, soft but with energy in it, evidently a woman’s
en|step. I could not help recollecting, with a tingling sensation through all my veins, the
en|distinctness of the turn it gave--the coming back, the steps going in a line opposite
en|to ours. It seemed to me that from moment to moment I must hear it
en|again in the gallery, and then how could it be explained? Next day--for I slept
en|very well after I had succeeded in getting to sleep, and what I had heard
en|did not by any means haunt my dreams--next day I managed to elude all the
en|pleasant occupations of the house, and, as soon as I could get free from observation,
en|I took my way to the Lady’s Walk. I had said that I had letters
en|to write--a well-worn phrase, which of course means exactly what one pleases. I walked up
en|and down the Lady’s Walk, and could neither hear nor see anything. On this side
en|of the shrubbery there was no possibility of any concealed path; on the other side
en|the bank went sloping to the water’s edge. The avenue ran along from the corner
en|of the loch half-way round the green plateau on which the house was planted, and
en|at the upper end came out upon the elevated ground behind the house; but no
en|road crossed it, nor was there the slightest appearance of any mode by which a
en|steady sound not its own could be communicated here. I examined it all with the
en|utmost care, looking behind the bole of every tree, as if the secret might be
en|there, and my heart gave a leap when I perceived what seemed to me one
en|narrow track worn along the ground. Fancy plays us curious pranks even when she is
en|most on her guard. It was a strange idea that I, who had come here
en|with the purpose of finding a way of explaining the curious phenomena upon which so
en|long and lasting a superstition had been built, should be so quickly infected by it.
en|I saw the little track, quite narrow but very distinct, and though of course I
en|did not believe in the Lady of Ellermore, yet within myself I jumped at the
en|certainty that this was her track. It gave me a curious sensation. The certainty lay
en|underneath the scepticism, as if they were two things which had no connection with each
en|other. Had anyone seen me it must have been supposed that I was looking for
en|something among the bushes, so closely did I scrutinise every foot of the soil and
en|every tree. It exercised a fascination upon me which I could not resist. The Psychical
en|Society did not exist in those days, so far as I know, but there are
en|many minds outside that inquisitive body to whom the authentication of a ghost story, or,
en|to speak more practically, the clearing up of a superstition, is very attractive. I managed
en|to elude the family arrangements once more at the same hour at which Miss Campbell
en|and I had visited the Lady’s Walk on the previous evening. It was a lovely
en|evening, soft and warm, the western sky all ablaze with colour, the great branches of
en|the beeches thrown out in dark maturity of greenness upon the flush of orange and
en|crimson, melting into celestial rosy red as it rose higher, and flinging itself in frothy
en|heaps across the serene blue above. The same wonderful colours glowed in reflection out of
en|the loch. The air was of magical clearness, and earth and sky seemed stilled with
en|an almost awe of their own loveliness, happiness, and peace. “The holy time was quiet
en|as a nun, Breathless with adoration.” For my part, however, I noticed this only in
en|passing, being intent on other thoughts. From the loch there came a soft tumult of
en|voices. It was Saturday evening, and all the boys were at home. They were getting
en|out the boats for an evening row, and the white sail of the toy yacht
en|rose upon the gleaming water like a little white cloud among the rosy clouds of
en|that resplendent sky. I stood between two of the beeches that formed a sort of
en|arch, and looked out upon them, distracted for an instant by the pleasant distant sound
en|which came softly through the summer air. Next moment I turned sharply round with a
en|start, in spite of myself; turned quickly to see who it was coming after me.
en|There was, I need not say, not a soul within sight. The beeches fluttered softly
en|in the warm air; the long shadows of their great boles lay unbroken along the
en|path; nothing else was visible, not even a bird on a bank. I stood breathless
en|between the two trees, with my back turned to the loch, gazing at nothing, while
en|the soft footsteps came quietly on, and passed me--passed me! with a slight waft of
en|air, I thought, such as a slight, light figure might have made; but that was
en|imagination perhaps. Imagination! was it not all imagination? or what was it? No shadows or
en|darkness to conceal a form by; full light of day radiant with colour; the most
en|living, delightful air, all sweet with pleasure. I stood there speechless and without power to
en|move. _She_ went along softly, without changing the gentle regularity of her tread, to the
en|end of the walk. The steps grew fainter as they went farther and farther from
en|me. I never listened so intently in my life. I said to myself, “If they
en|go out of hearing I shall know it is merely an excited imagination.” And on
en|they went, almost out of hearing, only the faintest touch upon the ground; then there
en|was a momentary pause, and my heart stood still, but leaped again to my throat
en|and sent wild waves of throbbing to my ears next moment: they had turned and
en|were coming back. I cannot describe the extraordinary effect. If it had been dark it
en|would have been altogether different. The brightness, the life around, the absence of all that
en|one associates with the supernatural, produced a thrill of emotion to which I can give
en|no name. It was not fear; yet my heart beat as it had never done
en|in any dangerous emergency (and I have passed through some that were exciting enough) before;
en|my breath seemed to go away from me. Would she come back to where I
en|was? She did, passing me once more, with the same movement of the air (or
en|so I thought), and turned again. But by that time my pulses were all clanging
en|so in my ears that I could bear it no longer. I turned and walked
en|precipitately away, stumbling down the little slope and losing myself in the shrubberies which were
en|beneath the range of the low sun, now almost set, and felt dank and cold
en|in the contrast. It was something like plunging into a bath of cold air after
en|the warmth and glory above. It was in this way that my first experience ended.
en|Miss Campbell looked at me a little curiously with a half-smile when I joined the
en|party at the loch side. She divined where I had been, and perhaps something of
en|the strange agitation I felt, but she took no further notice; and as I was
en|in time to find a place in the boat, where she had established herself with
en|the children, I lost nothing by my meeting with the mysterious passenger in the Lady’s
en|Walk. I did not go near the place for some days afterwards, but I cannot
en|say that it was ever long out of my thoughts. I had long arguments with
en|myself on the subject, representing to myself that I had heard the sound before hearing
en|the superstition, and then had found no difficulty in believing that it was the sound
en|of some passenger on an adjacent path, perhaps invisible from the walk. I had not
en|been able to find that path, but still it might exist at some angle which,
en|according to the natural law of the transmission of sounds--Bah! what jargon this was! Had
en|I not heard _her_ turn, felt her pass me, watched her coming back? And then
en|I paused with a loud burst of laughter at myself. “Ass! you never had any
en|of these sensations before you heard the story,” I said. And that was true; but
en|I heard the steps before I heard the story; and, now I think of it,
en|was much startled by them, and set my mind to work to account for them,
en|as you know. “And what evidence have you that the first interpretation was not the
en|right one?” myself asked me with scorn; upon which question I turned my back with
en|a hopeless contempt of the pertinacity of that other person who has always so many
en|objections to make. Interpretation! could any interpretation ever do away with the effect upon my
en|actual senses of that invisible passer-by? But the most disagreeable effect was this, that I
en|could not shut out from my mind the expectation of hearing those same steps in
en|the gallery outside my door at night. It was a long gallery, running the full
en|length of the wing, highly polished and somewhat slippery, a place in which any sound
en|was important. I never went along to my room without a feeling that at any
en|moment I might hear those steps behind me, or after I had closed my door
en|might be conscious of them passing. I never did so, but neither have I ever
en|got free of the thought. A few days after, however, another incident occurred that drove
en|the Lady’s Walk and its invisible visitor out of my mind. We were all returning
en|home in the long northern twilight from a mountain expedition. How it was that I
en|was the last to return I do not exactly recollect. I think Miss Campbell had
en|forgotten to give some directions to the watchman’s wife at the lodge, which I volunteered
en|to carry for her. My nearest way back would have been through the Lady’s Walk,
en|had not some sort of doubtful feeling restrained me from taking it. Though I have
en|said and felt that the effect of these mysterious footsteps was enhanced by the full
en|daylight, still I had a sort of natural reluctance to put myself in the way
en|of encountering them when the darkness began to fall. I preferred the shrubberies, though they
en|were darker and less attractive. As I came out of their shade, however, someone met
en|me, coming apparently from that direction. I thought at first it was Charlotte, for the
en|outline was like hers. It was almost dark, and what little light there was was
en|behind her, so that I could not distinguish her features. She was tall and slight,
en|and wrapped apparently in a long cloak, a dress usual enough in those rainy regions.
en|I think, too, that her veil was over her face. The way in which she
en|approached made it apparent that she was going to speak to me, which surprised me
en|a little, though there was nothing extraordinary in it; for of course by this time
en|all the neighbourhood knew who I was and that I was a visitor at Ellermore.
en|There was a little air of timidity and hesitation about her as she came forward,
en|from which I supposed that my sudden appearance startled her a little, and yet was
en|welcome as an unexpected way of getting something done that she wanted. _Tant de choses
en|en un mot_, you will say--and yet it was quite true. She came up to
en|me quickly as soon as she had made up her mind. Her voice was very
en|soft, but very peculiar, with a sort of far-away sound, as if the veil or
en|evening air interposed a sort of visionary distance between her and me. “I cannot speak
en|to them. I must not speak to them,” she said, then paused a little and
en|seemed to look at me with eyes that shone dimly through the shadows like stars
en|in a misty sky. “Can I be of any use to you? I am living
en|here,” I said, very much surprised. “Tell them it’s Colin! Colin! in trouble and temptation.
en|Oh, and I must not speak!” “Colin!” I said, startled; then, after a moment, “Pardon
en|me, this is an uncomfortable message to entrust to a stranger. Is he ill? What
en|must I tell them?” I was still less interested than surprised. “In great trouble and
en|temptation,” she repeated, with a sort of wail. “Oh, the bonnie boy, the bonnie boy!”
en|“Stop,” I cried, “stop!” for she seemed about to pass on. “If I am to
en|say this there must be something more. Who is it that sends the message? They
en|will ask me, of course. And what is wrong?” She seemed to wring her hands
en|under her cloak, and looked at me with an attitude and gesture of supplication. “In
en|great trouble,” she said, “in great trouble! And me, I can do nothing; nor even
en|speak, nor even speak!” And, notwithstanding all that I could say, she left me so,
en|with a wave of her hand, disappearing among the dark bushes. It may be supposed
en|that this was no agreeable charge to give to a guest, one who owed nothing
en|but pleasure and kindness to the Campbells, but had no acquaintance beyond the surface with
en|their concerns. They were, it is true, very free in spirit, and seemed to have
en|as little _dessous des cartes_ in their life and affairs as could be imagined. But
en|Colin was the one who was spoken of less freely than any other in the
en|family. He had been expected several times since I came, but had never appeared. It
en|seemed that he had a way of postponing his arrival, and “of course,” it was
en|said in the family, never came when he was expected. I had wondered more than
en|once at the testy tone in which the old gentleman spoke of him sometimes, and
en|the line of covert defence always adopted by Charlotte. To be sure, he was the
en|eldest, and might naturally assume a greater independence of action than the other young men,
en|who were yet scarcely beyond the age of pupilage and in their father’s house. But
en|from this, as well as from the still more natural and apparent reason that to
en|bring them bad news of any kind was most disagreeable and inappropriate on my part,
en|the commission I had so strangely received hung very heavily upon me. I turned it
en|over in my mind as I dressed for dinner (we had been out all day,
en|and dinner was much later than usual in consequence) with great perplexity and distress. Was
en|I bound to give a message forced upon me in such a way? If the
en|lady had news of any importance to give, why did she turn away from the
en|house, where she could have communicated it at once, and confide it to a stranger?
en|On the other hand, would I be justified in keeping back anything that might be
en|of so much importance to them? It might perhaps be something for which she did
en|not wish to give her authority. Sometimes people in such circumstances will even condescend to
en|write an anonymous letter to give the warning they think necessary, without betraying to the
en|victims of misfortune that anyone whom they know is acquainted with it. Here was a
en|justification for the strange step she had taken. It might be done in the utmost
en|kindness to them, if not to me; and what if there might be some real
en|danger afloat and Colin be in peril, as she said? I thought over these things
en|anxiously before I went downstairs, but even up to the moment of entering that bright
en|and genial drawing-room, so full of animated faces and cheerful talk, I had not made
en|up my mind what I should do. When we returned to it after dinner I
en|was still uncertain. It was late, and the children were sent to bed. The boys
en|went round to the stables to see that the horses were not the worse for
en|their day’s work. Mr. Campbell retired to his library. For a little while I was
en|left alone, a thing that very rarely happened. Presently Miss Campbell came downstairs from the
en|children’s rooms, with that air about her of rest and sweetness, like a reflection of
en|the little prayers she has been hearing and the infant repose which she has left,
en|which hangs about a young mother when she has disposed her babies to sleep. Charlotte,
en|by her right of being no mother, but only a voluntary mother by deputy, had
en|a still more tender light about her in the sweetness of this duty which God
en|and her goodwill, not simple nature, had put upon her. She came softly into the
en|room with her shining countenance. “Are you alone, Mr. Temple?” she said, with a little
en|surprise. “How rude of those boys to leave you,” and came and drew her chair
en|towards the table where I was in the kindness of her heart. “I am very
en|glad they have left me if I may have a little talk with you,” I
en|said; and then, before I knew, I had told her. She was the kind of
en|woman to whom it is a relief to tell whatever may be on your heart.
en|The fact that it was her own concern seemed to move her less than the
en|care to myself. She was very much surprised and disturbed. “Colin in trouble? Oh, that
en|might very well be,” she said, then stopped herself. “You are his friend,” she said;
en|“you will not misunderstand me, Mr. Temple. He is very independent, and not so open
en|as the rest of us. That is nothing against him. We are all rather given
en|to talking; we keep nothing to ourselves--except Colin. And then he is more away than
en|the rest.” The first necessity in her mind seemed to be this, of defending the
en|absent. Then came the question, From whom could the warning be? Charley came in at
en|this moment, and she called him to her eagerly. “Here is a very strange thing
en|happened. Somebody came up to Mr. Temple in the shrubbery and told him to tell
en|us that Colin was in trouble.” “Colin!” I could see that Charley was, as Charlotte
en|had been, more distressed than surprised. “When did you hear from him last?” he said.
en|“On Monday; but the strange thing is, who could it be that sent such a
en|message? You said a lady, Mr. Temple?” “What like was she?” said Charley. Then I
en|described as well as I could. “She was tall and very slight; wrapped up in
en|a cloak, so that I could not make out much, and her veil down. And
en|it was almost dark.” “It is clear she did not want to be recognised,” Charley
en|said. “There was something peculiar about her voice, but I really cannot describe it; a
en|strange tone, unlike anything”-- “Marion Gray has a peculiar voice; she is tall and slight.
en|But what could she know about Colin?” “I will tell you who is more likely,”
en|cried Charley, “and that is Susie Cameron. Her brother is in London now; they may
en|have heard from him.” “Oh, Heaven forbid! oh, Heaven forbid! the Camerons of all people!”
en|Charlotte cried, wringing her hands. The action struck me as so like that of the
en|veiled stranger that it gave me a curious shock. I had not time to follow
en|out the vague, strange suggestion that it seemed to breathe into my mind; but the
en|sensation was as if I had suddenly, groping, come upon someone in the dark. “Whoever
en|it was,” I said, “she was not indifferent, but full of concern and interest”-- “Susie
en|would be that,” Charley said, looking significantly at his sister, who rose from her chair
en|in great distress. “I would telegraph to him at once,” she said, “but it is
en|too late to-night.” “And what good would it do to telegraph? If he is in
en|trouble it would be no help to him.” “But what can I do? what else
en|can I do?” she cried. I had plunged them into sudden misery, and could only
en|look on now as an anxious but helpless spectator, feeling at the same time as
en|if I had intruded myself upon a family affliction; for it was evident that they
en|were not at all unprepared for “trouble” to Colin. I felt my position very embarrassing,
en|and rose to go away. “I feel miserably guilty,” I said, “as if I had
en|been the bearer of bad news; but I am sure you will believe that I
en|would not for anything in the world intrude upon”-- Charlotte paused to give me a
en|pale sort of smile, and pointed to the chair I had left. “No, no,” she
en|said, “don’t go away, Mr. Temple. We do not conceal from you that we are
en|anxious--that we were anxious even before--but don’t go away. I don’t think I will tell
en|my father, Charley. It would break his rest. Let him have his night’s rest whatever
en|happens, and there is nothing to be done to-night”-- “We will see what the post
en|brings to-morrow,” Charley said. And then the consultation ended abruptly by the sudden entrance of
en|the boys, bringing a gust of fresh night air with them. The horses were not
en|a grain the worse, though they had been out all day; even old Grumbling Geordie,
en|the coachman, had not a word to say. “You may have them again to-morrow, Chatty,
en|if you like,” said Tom. She had sat down to her work, and met their
en|eyes with an unruffled countenance. “I hope I am not so unreasonable,” she said, with
en|her tranquil looks; only I could see a little tremor in her hand, as she
en|stooped over the socks she was knitting. She laid down her work after a while,
en|and went to the piano and played accompaniments, to which first Jack and then Tom
en|sang. She did it without any appearance of effort, yielding to all the wishes of
en|the youngsters, while I looked on wondering. How can women do this sort of thing?
en|It is more than one can divine. Next morning Mr. Campbell asked “by the bye,”
en|but with a pucker in his forehead, which, being now enlightened on the subject, I
en|could understand, if there was any letter from Colin. “No,” Charlotte said (who, for her
en|part, had turned over all her letters with a swift, anxious scrutiny). “But that is
en|nothing,” she said, “for we heard on Monday.” The old gentleman uttered a “Umph!” of
en|displeasure. “Tell him I think it a great want in manners that he is not
en|here to receive Mr. Temple.” “Oh, father, Mr. Temple understands,” cried Charlotte, and she turned
en|upon me those mild eyes, in which there was now a look that went to
en|my heart, an appeal at once to my sympathy and my forbearance, bidding me not
en|to ask, not to speak, yet to feel with her all the same. If she
en|could have known the rush of answering feeling with which my heart replied; but I
en|had to be careful not even to _look_ too much knowledge, too much sympathy. After
en|this two days passed without any incident. What letters were sent, or other communications, to
en|Colin I could not tell. They were great people for the telegraph, and flashed messages
en|about continually. There was a telegraph station in the little village, which had been very
en|surprising to me at first; but I no longer wondered, seeing their perpetual use of
en|it. People who have to do with business, with great “works” to manage, get into
en|the way more easily than we others. But either no answer or nothing of a
en|satisfactory character was obtained, for I was told no more. The second evening was Sunday,
en|and I was returning alone from a ramble down the glen. It was Mr. Campbell’s
en|custom to read a sermon on Sunday evenings to his household, and as I had,
en|in conformity to the custom of the family, already heard two at church, I had
en|deserted on this occasion, and chosen the freedom and quiet of a rural walk instead.
en|It was a cloudy evening, and there had been rain. The clouds hung low on
en|the hills, and half the surrounding peaks had retired altogether into the mist. I had
en|scarcely set foot within the gates when I met once more the lady whose message
en|had brought so much pain. The trees arched over the approach at this spot, and
en|even in full daylight it was in deep shade. Now in the evening dimness it
en|was dark as night. I could see little more than the slim, straight figure, the
en|sudden perception of which gave me--I could scarcely tell why--a curious thrill of something like
en|fear. She came hurriedly towards me, an outline, nothing more, until the same peculiar voice,
en|sweet but sharp, broke the silence. “Did you tell them?” she said. It cost me
en|an effort to reply calmly. My heart had begun to beat with an excitement over
en|which I had no control, like a horse that takes fright at something which its
en|rider cannot see. I said, “Yes, I told them,” straining my eyes, yet feeling as
en|if my faculties were restive like that same horse, and would not obey me, would
en|not look or examine her appearance as I desired. But indeed it would have been
en|in vain, for it was too dark to see. “But there’s nothing done, nothing done!”
en|she said. “Would I come for nothing?” And there was again that movement, the same
en|as I had seen in Charlotte, of wringing her hands. “Pardon me,” I said, “will
en|you tell me who you are? I am a stranger here; no doubt if you
en|would see Miss Campbell herself, or tell me who it is”-- I felt the words
en|somehow arrested in my throat, and she drew back from me with a sudden movement.
en|It is hard to characterise a gesture in the dark, but there seemed to be
en|a motion of impatience and despair in it. “Who would I be?” she cried, “that
en|could not speak? It’s because you’re a stranger, and wish them well. Colin, Colin! oh,
en|the bonnie boy!” “I will carry your message, but, for God’s sake, if it is
en|so important, tell me who sends it,” I said. She shook her head and went
en|rapidly past me, notwithstanding the anxious appeals that I tried to make. She seemed to
en|put out a hand to wave me back as I stood gazing after her. Just
en|then the lodge door opened. I suppose the woman within had been disturbed by the
en|sound of the voices, and a gleam of firelight burst out upon the road. Across
en|this gleam I saw the slight figure pass quickly, and then a capacious form with
en|a white apron came out and stood in the door. The sight of the coachman’s
en|wife in her large and comfortable proportions gave me a certain ease, I cannot tell
en|why. I hurried up to her. “Who was that that passed just now?” I asked.
en|“That passed just now? There was naebody passed. I thought I heard a voice, and
en|that it was maybe Geordie; but nobody has passed here that I could see.” “Nonsense!
en|you must have seen her,” I cried hastily; “she cannot be out of sight yet.
en|No doubt you would know who she was--a lady, tall and slight--in a cloak”-- “Eh,
en|sir, ye maun be joking!” cried the woman. “What lady, if it werna Miss Chatty,
en|would be walking here at this time of the night? Lady! it might maybe be
en|the schoolmaster’s daughter. She has one of those ulsters like her betters. But naebody has
en|passed here this hour back, o’ that I’m confident,” she said. “Why did you come
en|out, then, just at this moment?” I cried. The woman contemplated me in the gleam
en|from the fire from top to toe. “You’re the English gentleman that’s biding up at
en|the house,” she said. “’Deed, I just heard a step, that was nae doubt your
en|step, and I thought it might be my man; but there has naebody, far less
en|a lady, whatever she had on, passed my door coming or going. Is that you,
en|Geordie?” she cried suddenly, as a step became audible approaching the gate from the outer
en|side. “Ay, it’s just me,” responded her husband out of the gloom. “Have ye met
en|a leddy as ye came along? The gentleman here will have it that there’s been
en|a leddy passing the gate, and there’s been no leddy. I would have seen her
en|through the window even if I hadna opened the door.” “I’ve seen no leddy,” said
en|Geordie, letting himself in with considerable noise at the foot entrance, which I now remembered
en|to have closed behind me when I passed through it a few minutes before. “I’ve
en|met no person; it’s no’ an hour for leddies to be about the roads on
en|Sabbath day at e’en.” It was at this moment that a wild suggestion darted into
en|my mind. How it came I cannot tell. I was not the sort of man,
en|I said to myself, for any such folly. My imagination had been a little touched,
en|to be sure, by that curious affair of the footsteps; but this, which seemed to
en|make my heart stand still and sent a shiver through me, was very different, and
en|it was a folly not to be entertained for a moment. I stamped my foot
en|upon it instantly, crushing it on the threshold of the mind. “Apparently either you or
en|I must be mistaken,” I said, with a laugh at the high tone of Geordie,
en|who himself had evidently been employed in a jovial way--quite consistent, according to all I
en|had heard, with very fine principles in respect to the Sabbath. I had a laugh
en|over this as I went away, insisting upon the joke to myself as I hurried
en|up the avenue. It was extremely funny, I said to myself; it would be a
en|capital story among my other Scotch experiences. But somehow my laugh died away in a
en|very feeble sort of quaver. The night had grown dark even when I emerged from
en|under the trees, by reason of a great cloud, full of rain, which had rolled
en|up over the sky, quenching it out. I was very glad to see the lights
en|of the house gleaming steadily before me. The blind had not been drawn over the
en|end window of the drawing-room, and from the darkness without I looked in upon a
en|scene which was full of warmth and household calm. Though it was August there was
en|a little glimmer of fire. The reading of the sermon was over. Old Mr. Campbell
en|still sat at a little table with the book before him, but it was closed.
en|Charlotte in the foreground, with little Harry and Mary on either side of her, was
en|“hearing their paraphrase.”[A] The boys were putting a clever dog through his tricks in a
en|sort of clandestine way behind backs, at whom Charlotte would shake a finger now and
en|then with an admonitory smiling look. Charley was reading or writing at the end of
en|the room. The soft little chime of the children’s voices, the suppressed laughter and whispering
en|of the boys, the father’s leisurely remark now and then, made up a soft murmur
en|of sound which was like the very breath of quietude and peace. How did I
en|dare, their favoured guest, indebted so deeply as I was to their kindness, to go
en|in among them with that mysterious message and disturb their tranquillity once more? When I
en|went into the drawing-room, which was not till an hour later, Charlotte looked up at
en|me smiling, with some playful remark as to my flight from the evening reading. But
en|as she caught my eye her countenance changed. She put down her book, and after
en|a little consideration walked to that end window through which I had looked, and which
en|was in a deep recess, making me a little sign to follow her. “How dark
en|the night is,” she said, with a little pretence of looking out, and then in
en|a hurried under-tone, “Mr. Temple, you have heard something more?” “Not any more, but certainly
en|the same thing repeated. I have seen the lady again.” “And who is she? Tell
en|me frankly, Mr. Temple. Just the same thing--that Colin is in trouble? no details? I
en|cannot imagine who can take so much interest. But you asked her for her name?”
en|“I asked her, but she gave me no reply. She waved her hand and went
en|on. I begged her to see you, and not to give me such a commission;
en|but it was of no use. I don’t know if I ought to trouble you
en|with a vague warning that only seems intended to give pain.” “Oh yes,” she cried,
en|“oh yes, it was right to tell me. If I only knew who it was!
en|Perhaps you can describe her better, since you have seen her a second time. But
en|Colin has friends--whom we don’t know. Oh, Mr. Temple, it is making a great claim
en|upon your kindness, but could not you have followed her and found out who she
en|was?” “I might have done that,” I said. “To tell the truth, it was so
en|instantaneous and I was so startled.” She looked up at me quickly with a questioning
en|air, and grew a little pale, gazing at me; but whether she comprehended the strange
en|wild fancy which I could not even permit myself to realise I cannot tell; for
en|Charley, seeing us standing together, and being in a state of nervous anxiety, also here
en|came and joined us, and we stood talking together in an undertone till Mr. Campbell
en|called to know if anything was the matter. “You are laying your heads together like
en|a set of conspirators,” said the old gentleman, with a half-laugh. His manner to me
en|was always benign and gracious; but now that I knew something of the family troubles,
en|I could perceive a vein of suppressed irritation, a certain watchfulness, which made him alarming
en|to the other members of the household. Charlotte gave us both a warning look. “I
en|will tell him to-morrow--I will delay no longer--but not to-night,” she said. “Mr. Temple was
en|telling us about his ramble, father. He has just come in in time to avoid
en|the rain.” “Well,” said the old man, “he cannot expect to be free from rain
en|up here in the Highlands. It is wonderful the weather we have had.” And with
en|this the conversation fell into a very domestic channel. Miss Campbell this time could not
en|put away the look of excitement and agitation in her eyes. But she escaped with
en|the children to see them put to bed, and we sat and talked of politics
en|and other mundane subjects. The boys were all going to leave Ellermore next day--Tom and
en|Jack for the “works,” Charley upon some other business. Mr. Campbell made me formal apologies
en|for them. “I had hoped Colin would have been at home before now to do
en|the honours of the Highlands; but we expect him daily,” he said. He kept his
en|eye fixed upon me as if to give emphasis to his words and defy any
en|doubt that might arise in my mind. Next morning I was summoned by Charley before
en|I came downstairs to “come quickly and speak to my father.” I found him in
en|the library, which opened from the dining-room. He was walking about the room in great
en|agitation. He began to address me almost before I was in sight. “Who is this,
en|sir, that you have been having meetings with about Colin? Some gossip or other that
en|has taken ye in. I need not tell you, Mr. Temple, a lawyer and an
en|Englishman, that an anonymous statement”--For once the old gentleman had forgotten himself, his respect for
en|his guest, his fine manners. He was irritated, obstinate, wounded in pride and feeling. Charlotte
en|touched him on the arm with a murmured appeal, and turned her eyes to me
en|in anxious deprecation. But there was no thought farther from my mind than that of
en|taking offence. “I fully feel it,” I said; “nor was it my part to bring
en|any disagreeable suggestions into this house--if it had not been that my own mind was
en|so burdened with it and Miss Campbell so clear-sighted.” He cast a look at her,
en|half affectionate, half displeased, and then he said to me testily, “But who was the
en|woman? That is the question; that is what I want to know.” My eyes met
en|Charlotte’s as I looked up. She had grown very pale, and was gazing at me
en|eagerly, as if she had divined somehow the wild fancy which once more shot across
en|my mind against all reason and without any volition of mine. CHAPTER III Mr. Campbell
en|was not to be moved. He was very anxious, angry, and ill at ease; but
en|whether it was that he would not betray to me that the message, which he
en|viewed as an anonymous statement, could cause him any uneasiness, or whether it was perplexity
en|and confusion of mind, or if he really felt a confidence which neither his son
en|nor daughter shared, I cannot tell. But he refused to be influenced in any way
en|by this strange communication. It would be some intrusive woman, he said; some busybody--there were
en|many about--who, thinking she could escape being found out in that way, had thought it
en|a grand opportunity of making mischief. He made me a great many apologies for his
en|first hasty words. It was very ill-bred, he said; he was ashamed to think that
en|he had let himself be so carried away; but he would hear nothing of the
en|message itself. It appeared that Miss Campbell had both written and telegraphed to her brother.
en|To the letter there was as yet no reply; but Colin had answered the telegram
en|by a somewhat angry one, declaring that he was all right. “What more would you
en|have him to do?” Mr. Campbell said, with a sort of restrained fury. Charlotte said
en|nothing more in my presence, but I divined that she was anxiously endeavouring to induce
en|him, if not to go himself, yet to permit her to go to her brother.
en|The position was a very embarrassing one, especially when all the brothers left for their
en|business, which they did by the morning boat. It seemed out of all character that
en|a stranger should remain in the circumstances; so I contrived to have a letter by
en|the midday post summoning me back to town. They were, of course, quite well aware
en|that letters do not come from London on a Monday; but Charlotte at least made
en|no remark. Her father looked at me rather fiercely, being irritated and susceptible, and disposed
en|to take offence at anything that seemed to attach importance to this curious episode; and
en|the children made a great outcry and lamentation; but they did not make any serious
en|attempt to change my resolution. It was even agreeable to Miss Campbell I saw, and
en|this gave me a pang, anxious as I was to be agreeable to her in
en|every way. The last boat would get me to the nearest station in time for
en|the night train, or it was suggested that I might be driven there, which would
en|give me still more time. I had made all my arrangements, and had come downstairs
en|again, somewhat forlorn, to have my last talk with the woman whose sweet company during
en|these two or three weeks past had been more to me than I could say.
en|I found her with her hat on, waiting for me in the hall. “I thought
en|you would like to take one turn more,” she said, with a smile, in which
en|(I hoped) there was some sadness. There was certainly excitement in her eyes, in her
en|movements a sort of eagerness and almost impatience. We went out and walked across the
en|lawn to the side of the loch. The sun was beginning to sink; the sky
en|was all aglow, putting on by degrees the gorgeous hues of a northern sunset. She
en|said nothing till we were clear of all possibility of listeners--too far off for the
en|children to rush out upon us, as they so often did. Then she paused suddenly,
en|and looked up into my face. “Mr. Temple,” she said, “you will think me heartless,
en|letting you go without a word, though well I know the reason why. You think
en|you are a trouble to us at such a time. Oh no, you are no
en|trouble. But I am selfish; I don’t wish to detain you--I want you to do
en|something for me.” “Anything,” I cried, “anything--whatever man can.” “I knew you would say so;
en|that is why I have scarcely said I am sorry. I have not tried to
en|stop you. Mr. Temple, I am not shutting my eyes to it like my father.

en|in her eyes. And I hope I was man enough to understand without either offence

en|was a sort of forlorn hope; that I was like a rope thrown out to

en|would disappear--my use being served--and be seen no more. But this was not--oh, surely not--what
en|she meant! She was not a woman to throw anyone over who had served her.
en|We walked up and down the side of the water, which every moment grew more

en|our minds had no room for its beauty, and it only touched my eyesight in

en|how his friends and his ways were unknown to them. “But we have always hoped

en|in this conversation, for it was time that I should go in and eat my

en|Campbell said nothing to his daughter when she made up to him. He almost pushed

en|colourless. Alarm and dismay were written on every line of it. Large drops of perspiration
en|stood upon his forehead. He seemed to desire to speak, but could not; then held

en|desired the man to follow. It seemed impossible to predict what might happen. I had
en|begun to be superstitious and fanciful myself, and a dozen times over fancied that I
en|saw a woman in a cloak following our course with wistful looks, or shaking her

en|if the latter, I asked myself in her own words, What difference did it make?
en|CHAPTER IV Colin’s lodgings proved to be in the last place to which I should
en|have thought him likely to have gone--in one of the prim, respectable, old-fashioned streets about
en|Bloomsbury. Probably he felt himself more out of the way of remark there than he
en|would have been in regions more under public inspection, and where acquaintances might have found
en|him more readily. I got out quickly to hand Mr. Campbell from the cab, and
en|he held fast to my arm, apparently with a little confusion of mind. “Yes, I
en|want your arm. I am--a little shaky this morning; don’t leave me, Charley!” he said.
en|“Father, it is Mr. Temple,” said Charlotte. He looked up at me with dim eyes,
en|and a half smile. “Ay, to be sure, it is Mr. Temple. Never mind, he
en|will just come with me all the same.” He had been so determined before not
en|to acknowledge to me any anxiety about Colin, that this sudden abandonment of all reticence
en|struck me with strange surprise. I exchanged a glance with Charlotte over his shoulder. “Will
en|you come, since he says so?” she said. I could not blame her for not
en|wishing for my presence, but I felt by the weight of his hand upon my
en|arm that I was necessary, and said nothing more. There was evidently a little excitement
en|in the house at the sight of the carriage and the party arriving. The door
en|was opened by a young woman, too much dressed for a servant--the landlady’s daughter, no
en|doubt--who came out with the distinct intention of admitting nobody. Yes, Mr. Campbell lived there,
en|she acknowledged; but he was not very well--he was confined to his room. She believed
en|he was still in bed; he had left orders that he could see nobody. “He
en|will see us,” said Charlotte. “Will you let us pass at once, please, and show
en|me my brother’s room.” The young woman gave a little scream. “Oh! I can’t let
en|you go in,” she cried; “I daren’t. What would they all say to me?” “What
en|is all this?” said the old man, pushing forward; indeed, it was I whom he
en|pushed forward, like an implement to clear the way. He made his way thus up
en|the steps and in at the door, the girl retreating before him. This put me
en|forward a little in advance of him into the first room that presented itself, an
en|untidy parlour. Here he resigned my arm and sat down. “Go and tell Colin I
en|am here,” he said to his daughter. “Oh! I tell you, Mr. Campbell can’t see
en|you--he is ill in bed,” cried the girl, shutting the door upon us, and standing
en|with her back to it, evidently too frightened to know what to do. The room
en|was good-sized, though completely out of order, badly furnished and faded. It was connected by
en|folding-doors, which were closed, with another room behind. Presently one of these opened and admitted
en|another young woman, a little older than the first, and still more elaborately dressed, who
en|came into the midst of us with sudden impetuosity, but closed carefully the door behind
en|her. “I would like to know,” she said, “who it is that is making so
en|much noise, with a sick person in the house. I am Mrs. Campbell, if you
en|have anything to say to me.” She tossed her head with a determined air, confronting
en|Miss Campbell as if this was her natural antagonist. Charlotte gave a low cry. She
en|put herself in her turn before her father, as if to defend him from an

en|know who you are that push in like this to a strange house, nor that
en|person there--that is your daughter, I suppose? If you’ve got any claim upon him, I’m

en|father,” said Charlotte, with a sorrowful shake of her head. And then she said, “I
en|am very thankful. It might have been worse. If there is no more harm than
en|this, oh, father dear--many a good man has been mistaken. All may be well yet.”
en|“My God! Colin’s wife!” the old man cried, pushing me away from him and dropping
en|back into his chair. He had raised his voice, and the words seemed to ring
en|through the house. They were answered by a loud cry and groan mingled together from
en|the other side of the closed door. Then it was pulled open forcibly, and, haggard,
en|unshaven, half-dressed, Colin himself looked in. Never have I seen so tragic a figure. His
en|eyes were bloodshot and wild, his beard half grown, the darkness of his countenance and
en|straggling hair thrown up by the white shirt, crumpled and untidy, which covered his shoulders.
en|He gave one terrible glance round, taking in everything; and I have never myself doubted
en|that, not only the sudden appearance of his father and sister, and the old man’s
en|look of death (which none of us perceived at the moment), but the contrast between
en|Charlotte, standing there, and the woman, who immediately began to exclaim at his appearance, and
en|to attempt to force him back again, struck to the very heart of the half-maddened
en|man, and turned the scale at once. He gave one desperate look, pushing off with
en|fury the hand of the wife, which she had laid upon his arm, and disappeared
en|again. The next moment the sharp ring of a pistol shot, close at hand, rang
en|into us all, as if we each had received the bullet. That, I know, was
en|my own sensation. At the same moment there was a heavy fall in the room
en|beyond, and a groan--the only one and the last. It would be in vain for
en|me to attempt to describe the scene that followed. The woman who had called herself
en|Mrs. Campbell flung open the folding-doors and rushed into the room behind. He was lying
en|in a heap half under a table which had been drawn up to the side
en|of a sofa-bed. He had just risen, it was evident from the tumbled mass of
en|coverings. A cup of tea and the remains of some food were on the table,
en|placed where he could reach them from the bed. He had been at breakfast when
en|this terrible interruption came. On one side of his plate lay a quantity of letters,
en|some of which he had opened. An open case with one pistol in it was
en|on the table. The other lay, with a curl of smoke still about the mouth,
en|on the floor. I followed the woman, who flung herself down beside him on the
en|floor, and made the house resound with her shrieks. I had no special knowledge of
en|such matters, but I had a little experience, and had seen wounds and accidents. I
en|was convinced at the first glance that the doctor, whom I immediately rushed out to
en|seek, was unavailing. The shot had been mortal. But the living had to be cared
en|for, if not the dead. By good fortune I found a doctor only a few
en|doors off, who was still at home, attending to a number of poor patients who
en|crowded about his door. He came with me instantly. I told him what had happened
en|as exactly as I could while we ran from one house to the other. When
en|I took him into the scene of the tragedy, I found the table cleared away,
en|the room open, the morning air from the opened window playing upon the head, heavy
en|as marble, which Charlotte, seated on the floor, was supporting upon her lap. But no
en|one, not even the most inexperienced tyro, would have been deceived in that look. As
en|far from the warm pursuits of the cheerful day as if he had been dead
en|for a century was Colin, never to be called to account for his errors, or
en|shrink from the eye of an angry father in this world any more. His wife
en|was standing by, crying and scolding together. “Oh, what did you come here for to
en|drive him wild?” she cried. “He was getting better. And what if he had lost
en|money? there was plenty still. We’d have gone abroad, as he said. We’d have got
en|on. It’s not so very much as him and me could have wanted. What did
en|you come here for to madden him altogether? He said as I wasn’t to have
en|nothing to do with you. Oh, Campbell! Campbell! can’t you hear me crying? It’s only
en|a faint. I know it’s only a faint. If you would go away and let
en|him be quiet with me, he would come to himself.” The doctor opened the shirt,
en|on which there was scarcely any blood, though it was black with the smoke of
en|the pistol, which seemed to have been placed against it. Charlotte, motionless as the form
en|that leaned upon her, sat with her tearless eyes fixed upon him, following every movement.
en|After a brief examination, the doctor laid his hand gently on her arm. “If you
en|will let me,” he said compassionately, “we will lift him on to the bed.” Then
en|he added, still more gently, “You can do nothing more for him.” He and I
en|together, not without difficulty, did this last service. The woman behind broke out into tears
en|and cries, and mingled questions and reproaches. “It’s a faint he is in, doctor. Oh,
en|bring him round, bring him round! What is the good of being a doctor if
en|you cannot do that? It’s all their doing, coming so sudden, and he frightened to
en|face them, they’re so hard and cruel. Oh, doctor, don’t you see he’s in a
en|faint? Give him something; do something to bring him to!” “Try to be quiet,” said
en|the doctor, with some severity in his tone. He knew who she was, and thought
en|of her, as was evident, only as the landlady’s daughter. “You might take example by
en|this lady, who has far more to do with it. All the doctors in the
en|world could not bring him to, poor fellow! Compose yourself, my good girl, and take
en|the lady away.” The young woman gave a great shriek. “Who are you calling girl?”
en|she cried. “I’m his wife! his lawful wife! and he is only in a faint.
en|Oh, Campbell! Campbell! don’t you hear me? Oh, doctor, for God’s sake, bring him to!”
en|Poor creature! beyond her passion and her pride there was some real feeling too. She
en|flung herself at the foot of the bed in a passion of weeping, with loud
en|hysterical shrieks that rang through the house. This brought to her her mother and sister,
en|who, awed by Charlotte’s presence, and by the horror of the catastrophe, had been left
en|in the background, but who now rushed in, and, one at either side, began to
en|pour forth mingled wails for the dead and entreaties to the living. “Take the lady
en|away,” the doctor said, turning to me. Charlotte had not moved. She stood at the
en|end of the bed, with a face like marble, not noticing the noise and tumult
en|near her. She might have been all alone with her dead--so still was she; her
en|eyes fixed upon him, the handkerchief in her hand with which she had been bathing
en|his dead forehead. The blow seemed to have struck her to marble. Sometimes her mouth
en|quivered a little, but she did not shed a tear nor utter a word. I
en|took her hand to lead her away, and then she turned a little toward me
en|with a pitiful look. “We were too late,” she said. “Come away; there is nothing
en|you can do now. Oh, let me take you away; there are others who need
en|you!” “Nothing I can do,” she said dreamily; “nothing anyone can do. Too late! too
en|late!” But she did not move. She was in a region where sounds from without
en|did not reach her. Perhaps, for the moment, it was not even pain she felt,
en|but a wondering awe and solemnity which silenced every thought. “Charlotte,” I cried, being too
en|much moved myself to remember any kind of rule, “where is your father? You have
en|forgotten him! Where has he gone? Your father?” I was alarmed, indeed, at his absence
en|from among the group about poor Colin’s bed. “Ah!” she said, rousing herself with a
en|sigh, that seemed to come from the very bottom of her heart. “My father!” and
en|turned away slowly from the couch and him that lay upon it. The folding-doors were
en|half open, and showed at first only a portion of the chair on which Mr.
en|Campbell had placed himself, and from which it seemed he had never moved. One limp
en|arm and colourless hand hung from the arm of it. His head, sunk upon his
en|breast, was but half visible. For the moment I had no thought but that he
en|had died there, where he sat, and the sight of him added the last horror
en|to the scene. Both dead, father and son, and by one blow! Charlotte was at
en|his side in a second, while I had done no more than start in my
en|horror. She had loosened the wrappings about his throat, and changed the position of his
en|head, before I could get to her. She was all thought, all energy; she who
en|a moment before had been smitten, too, into marble! Happily, the doctor, who was still
en|there, went to him at once; and we got him laid upon the shabby sofa
en|in this room, which corresponded to the bed in the other on which his son
en|lay. It was a slight paralytic seizure, the doctor said; a fit out of which
en|he would recover probably soon. The situation altogether was so pitiful that even this stranger
en|was moved. He took me aside, and asked where they were living, and what were
en|the circumstances; and when I explained that they had arrived only this morning, offered to
en|have rooms prepared in his own house, and to take them there until Mr. Campbell
en|should have recovered. I was thankful to accept this proposal in place of anything better,
en|finding myself in the strange position of head of this sad party, and responsible for
en|everything; for Charlotte was entirely occupied with her father, and I could not bear that
en|she should be disturbed by the miserable details which had now to be thought of.
en|I had to leave her to attend to all these, but hurried back as soon
en|as I was able to share her vigil. And I have seldom known anything so
en|terrible as the long watch by that speechless old man; the creeping on of the
en|endless daylight hours, the coming of the evening. She took my presence without remark, and
en|referred to me as if I had been a brother without a word. This gave
en|me a personal pleasure, of which I was half ashamed, at so dreadful a moment;
en|but otherwise the day passed like a dream. In the evening there was a stir
en|of returning consciousness. He opened his eyes, and seemed to recognise his daughter by his
en|side, and attempted to raise his arm, which was powerless. The inability to do this
en|troubled him, and, perhaps, helped to rouse him. At first his speech was only a
en|confused babble, principally of complaint and annoyance at being kept there. He seemed to think
en|he was bound to his couch, and got very angry in his inarticulate commands to
en|her to loose him. But, by and by, his mind took a milder mood, and
en|his power of speech gradually came back. “I’m thinking, I’m thinking,” he said, “I’ve maybe
en|been--light-headed. Where is the doctor? Maxwell, where are ye?” and he tried to turn his
en|head to look round. Maxwell was the name of their doctor at home. Charlotte stood
en|almost over him, in her anxiety to prevent him from seeing anything that could bring
en|the scene of the morning to his mind; and by this time it was dark,
en|and two wretched, flickering candles made the room difficult to decipher. He remembered something, however,
en|of what had passed. A flicker of a smile passed over his face. “But, Chatty,
