en|ye’ve--ye’ve found Colin?” he stammered. “Yes, father.” “That’s well--that’s well! What’s all that noise and
en|shouting? It’ll be the lads bringing him home.” I turned away, feeling that I could
en|not bear it, almost afraid, excited and fatigued as I was, of making some hysterical
en|outburst. But she could. She stood perfectly still, with her back to the light, shutting
en|out from him as with wings all those local particulars which might have survived his
en|recollection. She told him, with a voice that never faltered, how he must submit to
en|be carried to bed. And, as a matter of fact, he submitted like a child,
en|and was soon feebly interested by his removal, and refreshed by the soft air of
en|the night that blew in his face, as we placed him in the litter to
en|carry him away. Fortunately, the way was very short, and though there was some difficulty
en|in getting through the crowd which had been gathered around the door for some hours,
en|drawn together by rumours of the tragedy within, we managed it without disturbing him much.
en|I saw him give a tremulous glance about him, and stooped down to hear what
en|he was saying, but all that I could make out was a murmur about “A
en|queer place London, a queer place.” He had, it seemed, forgotten everything except a faint,
en|confused idea of Colin, and that he was found and being brought home. He fell
en|asleep very soon after he had been settled in bed, in a comfortable room, where
en|there was already a nurse in attendance. Then Charlotte came out to me and held
en|out her hands. “You are tired to death,” she said; “you ought to rest; but
en|I must go back to _him_.” “Not to-night, not to-night. All this is enough to
en|kill you. I have seen to everything.” “That I am sure of,” she said, with
en|a faint, tender smile; “and I’ll not even say that I am thankful. But will
en|you take me back? I will ask no more.” We went _back_, as she said,
en|to the room where Colin was lying. Decency and calm had been restored to the
en|chamber of death; the sound of hysterical crying was heard occasionally from above, increasing I
en|thought (but I might be wrong) when it was known who was below. But below
en|all was silent and still; a miserable candle burning, the only watch over the dead.
en|I held up this poor light to show his face, as he lay there in
en|all the vigour of early manhood, a frame that seemed made to resist all the
en|storms of life. He lay as if he had been asleep, perfectly tranquil, as if
en|shame or sorrow had never come near him. She stood silent a long time, not
en|saying a word or shedding a tear. He had been her special companion in all
en|their earlier days--a year younger than she, no more. I comprehended that the pang of
en|this separation was not one to be evaporated in easy tears. I myself, who had
en|so little to do with him, it seemed to me that every hour in which
en|I had seen him, and every word I had ever heard him speak, came back
en|to me in the tragic silence and gloom, only broken by the faint light which
en|flickered in the air from the open window. A young man in the blossom of
en|his days, with everything before him; a rich man’s son, heir of all that money
en|could buy or household love bestow--yet all concluded like this. In squalor and wretchedness, in
en|the company of a woman not worthy to serve his sister as her maid, but
en|made into his wife--in a horror of discovery so deep, that to escape his father’s
en|eye he rushed into God’s presence with his own blood on his hands: out of
en|the calm of existence, everyday and ordinary, what a leap into the mysterious abysses of
en|life and death! When Charlotte sank down on her knees beside the bed, I could
en|endure it no longer, but, setting down the light, stole away into the adjoining room,
en|the scene of the other chapter of this tragedy, and sat down there in the
en|dark to wait for her, my head throbbing, my whole being confused and shaken. Even
en|at such a moment other thoughts will intrude. It may be imagined that I should
en|have felt, after so wonderful a drawing together of the bonds of intimacy, that there
en|were no hopes I might not entertain. But this was far, far from being the
en|case. Had I ever ventured to imagine that she could detach herself from all the
en|hands that clung to her, and come into my life and become a portion of
en|me? If so, I saw now the utter madness of the thought. I stood at
en|the window looking out upon the lamps, and the glimmer of reflection upon the pavement,
en|which was wet with repeated showers. A few people still hung about the outskirts of
en|a house in which a man had killed himself. The curiosity which waits upon death,
en|especially upon violent death, gaped at the door, as if something of that mystery would
en|be disclosed when it opened. For my part, I felt as if there was no
en|novelty in any incident, but that this, and only this, could have happened from the
en|beginning of time. When we left the house of death, Charlotte clung to me with
en|a nervous trembling which was the first sign of exhaustion she had shown. Even in
en|her, the claims of human weakness had to be acknowledged; her firm step wavered as
en|she descended the steps, and she was glad to have my arm for support. But
en|the peace of that scene after the tumult of the morning had produced its effect
en|upon her. She began to talk to me of Colin. “He was _my_ brother,” she
en|said. “Don’t you know a large family falls into pairs? Charley’s sister died too, and
en|since then he has been more with me; but it was always Colin and Chatty,
en|Chatty and Colin.” “He and you will comfort each other,” I said. “Charley is so
en|good a fellow.” “Ah!” she said, “he is good, and Colin was always a trouble--but
en|he is not Colin. Mr. Temple, if our boy had died by God’s hand and
en|not his own”--She paused a little and trembled, and her voice died away in her
en|throat. “I could almost have been glad,” she added afterwards, with a sudden energy. “He
en|and his life were never at harmony.” I felt her whole frame quiver with the
en|long sigh of a sorrow that was past tears. “Then it was not only this
en|marriage?” “Oh, can you think so little of us?” she cried. “We would have made
en|the best of it. Me, there is nothing, nothing I would not have done. Colin’s
en|wife, she would have been sacred. And so long as she loved him”--then she made
en|a pause. “You will hear afterwards,” she said; “I know our name, our honour was
en|in question. Oh, when you hear, do not judge him, Mr. Temple. If he did
en|wrong, he paid for it--always twice over, in misery and pain--and now he is in
en|God’s hand.” We went on in silence after this. We walked very slowly, for she
en|was worn out, and I should have been glad had every yard been a mile;
en|for it seemed to me that never again would Charlotte be so much mine. CHAPTER
en|V Charley, for whom I had telegraphed, came next day, very anxious and miserable, with
en|a horror of the shame and exposure which struck me in the strangest way. To
en|be sure it was not my name which was thus held up to everybody’s observation,
en|as connected with such a catastrophe; but the catastrophe itself was so pitiful, that I
en|scarcely could understand this special aspect in which he viewed it. He shrank even from
en|going about the necessary business, and drew back from everybody that might by possibility recognise
en|him. The first thing he had seen coming into London was the report in the
en|morning paper of the inquest; and the horror of this, and the certainty that it
en|would circulate everywhere, and make all possibility of concealment impossible, was almost more than he
en|could bear. It was from him that I heard the whole state of the case.
en|Colin’s expenditure had been for some years back the trouble and terror of the family,
en|and it appeared that he had plunged into speculation by way of mending this. The
en|letters that were found half read upon his table showed of themselves how the coils
en|of fate were closing around him. It was evident from Charley’s half revelations that the
en|case was clearly desperate for the offender, and not much less so for the family,
en|whose name had been made use of on all hands. This came upon the young
en|man not all in a moment, but by degrees, as Colin’s letters, and various business
en|representations from one side and another, came flowing in. On the eve of the funeral
en|he came to me with the paleness of despair in his face. “What am I
en|to do?” he cried. “My father is not able to pay any attention--they tell me
en|any new shock might kill him.” “Is it so very bad?” I said. “Bad? we’re
en|ruined; that is all,” cried Charley. He was, as I found out afterwards, a very
en|good man of business; but he had never had occasion to take the responsibility on
en|his shoulders, and now, suddenly left alone, suddenly brought face to face with unexampled calamity,
en|his self-command forsook him for the moment. Little by little he opened out to me
en|the state of affairs. The “works” were so profitable and the business so good, that
en|eventually everything might come right; but in the meantime he was paralysed, and did not
en|know what to do. Mr. Campbell was in a sort of tranquil, half-childish state, not
en|suffering much, and quite unconscious of what had happened. To consult him was impossible, and
en|Tom and Jack were but boys, who knew little as yet of the ramifications of
en|the business, or anything beyond the department of which they had charge. “Have you said
en|anything to your sister?” I asked; and then poor Charley broke down. “How can I
en|speak to Chatty?” he said; “he was always _her_ brother. I cannot bide to break
en|her heart. It is bad enough as it is--Colin gone, and all this misery--and my
en|father knowing nothing. If she finds out all he’s brought upon us, what will she
en|do?” “Do you think she does not know?” I said. “It was not for nothing
en|that your brother took such dreadful means of escape. You may be sure she suspects
en|the worst, even if she does not know.” “If I could think that!” he said.
en|It gave him a little composure. The mere idea that there was someone to whom
en|he could speak freely was a support. Even to talk it over with me was
en|something. We had been to the house of death to see that all was ready
en|for next day’s melancholy business, and the sight of Mrs. Colin done up in new
en|crape, with the white streamers of a coquettish widow’s cap setting off her commonplace comeliness,
en|had been almost more than either of us could bear. For my part, everything seemed
en|more mysterious to me in the light of this wife. Had Colin squandered the family
en|substance in luxurious chambers, at the feet of one of those beautiful harpies who are
en|never satisfied with luxury, it would have been more comprehensible. But the lodgings in Bloomsbury
en|and the landlady’s daughter seemed to throw an air of burlesque upon the tragedy. The
en|accessories ought to have been bad and vicious, not respectable and commonplace. But it seems
en|there are many ways of courting ruin; and there must have been other unknown chapters
en|in his life before he came to this. Perhaps, indeed, the hasty marriage, the retirement
en|into this shabby retreat, were of themselves efforts to get back into a better way.
en|I walked along with Charley to the house of the doctor, in which his father
en|and sister still were, meaning to leave him there; but he clutched at my arm.
en|“You’ve been through it all,” he said, in a broken voice. Charlotte came down to
en|us in the dining-room of the doctor’s house, the one corresponding to that in which
en|the first chapter of our tragedy had been enacted. She was very pale, yet greeted
en|us with a smile. Her father was always the same--quite comfortable, suspecting nothing, now and
en|then asking if Colin had gone home. “‘The best place for him, Chatty, the best
en|place for him,’ he says to me,” she said, the tears springing to her eyes,
en|“and we must let him think so, the doctor says. He says, ‘You must do
en|the London business, Charley. We must keep it up as long as we can.’” “If
en|there is any business to do--or if anybody will ever trust us more,” Charley said.
en|She had been pale enough before, but she seemed to me to grow paler, almost
en|ghastly. “Trust us!” she said, in a faint voice. “Is it so bad as that--have
en|we broken trust?” “Chatty, I don’t know how you’ll bear it. We are ruined, I
en|think,” the young man cried. She waved her hand as if this was nothing. “What
en|do you mean about trust?” she asked. “Is there anything that we cannot fulfil?” “I
en|don’t know how the ‘works’ are to go on. I don’t know how we are
en|to live. We are pledged and bound on every side, and I am not clever,
en|like my father. We will have to sacrifice everything.” Chatty drew a long breath. “Then
en|let us sacrifice everything, Charley. That is what my father would do. There need be
en|no hesitation about that; but no, not the ‘works’--we must keep the ‘works.’ Cannot you
en|think of anything that will keep them going for the children’s sake?” she cried. “And
en|then think of all the poor men thrown out of work in the middle of
en|winter!” “We must think of ourselves, Chatty,” her brother said, with a certain indignation. “I
en|do. They would recover in time. They are your life,” she said. “Save them, if
en|it is possible. Don’t give away our life into other hands.” “Then,” said Charley, drawing
en|a long breath--he propped himself up against the mantelshelf with a sort of despairing action--“then,”
en|he said, “there is but one thing else for it, Chatty. We must sell Ellermore.”
en|She stood and gazed at him for a moment with dilating eyes; then she suddenly
en|sat down on the nearest chair. She wanted support of a mechanical kind, as he
en|did. No doubt a vision of her home and all its pleasantness--the place where they
en|had all been born, the centre of their family pride and importance and all their
en|traditions--flashed across her. For a few minutes she made no reply. Then she made an
en|effort to command her voice. “Well,” she said faintly, “well, then, we must make up
en|our minds to it. We must sell Ellermore.” “Chatty,” cried the young man, with the
en|tears in his eyes, “how good it is to have you to talk to! Is
en|that what you say? Keep the ‘works’ and sell Ellermore? It will bring a fancy
en|price, you know. It’s not just like so many acres. Some Englishman”-- “Oh, Charley, don’t
en|torture me!” she cried, in a voice of anguish; then faintly, “The ‘works’ are your
en|life. And there are so many of us--still. We must think of the boys and
en|the little ones--next after our honour and my father’s name.” “That was what I thought,”
en|said Charley, “but I was afraid to say it. I thought you would cry out,
en|Ellermore! Ellermore! and let the ‘works’ take care of themselves.” She looked up at him
en|with a faint smile. “I never knew you took me for a fool before. I
en|suppose it is because I am a woman.” “Chatty, don’t say that!” cried the young
en|man loudly. Such a suggestion begins to rouse the wrath of young men. He almost
en|forgot the gravity of the position in his annoyance. She went on musingly: “Ellermore means
en|happiness, but the ‘works’ mean life. Life we must have, till--till it is taken away,”
en|she added, with a shiver; “but happiness! oh yes, it will come back. I am
en|not so young or so ignorant as not to know that it will come back,
en|and for all the young ones, soon, soon! There will be you and me will
en|think a little longer, Charley, and me longest of all, but not for ever. But
en|at present we are not happy. We will escape something--the pitying, and the sympathy, and
en|the inquiries--at least we shall escape all that. I am sure my father would think
en|so. But how we are to tell him I don’t know,” she cried again. “He
en|is quite cheerful; he is like a child; but if we take him home, and
en|it is not Ellermore, he will know.” They had both dried their tears as they
en|contemplated this difficulty, which neither knew how to deal with. I had been naturally left
en|outside of this discussion; but to hear them thus debating, consulting each other, arguing on
en|the main thing to be done, was more than I could bear. I could not
en|but remember the happy house, with all its advantages and homely wealth--the boats on the
en|loch, the grouse on the hills, the luxury and abundance. That door had never been
en|shut upon the stranger or the poor. And they were so entirely to the manner
en|born, seated in their old house among their native hills, it seemed impossible to conceive
en|of them in another place in other circumstances. This made it all the more wonderful
en|to perceive that neither of them hesitated a moment. The thought of how to tell
en|their father, how to keep him in his present state of cheerful unconsciousness, moved them
en|indeed with a pang of bewilderment; but no irresolution, no clinging to what they liked
en|best, no outcry against the cruel fate which deprived them of their home, was in
en|the thoughts of either. There may be people to whom this choice would seem want
en|of feeling. To me the quiet heroism was far more touching than any heroics. I
en|knew the wrench it would be, and I respected them all the more that neither
en|of them made anything of this, or even paused over it, as if the sacrifice
en|were too much. I went home, leaving them together, with a pang in my heart
en|of powerlessness to help them. I wonder whether the very rich are ever worked upon
en|by those burning desires to help, to step forth and act as the providence of
en|the suffering, which so often flame up in the bosoms of the comparatively poor. I
en|had enough for my own wants, and desired riches little, but when I thought of
en|stepping in to their aid, of becoming their surety and helper, my heart burned within
en|me. I thought almost hungrily of an inheritance which was coming to me, which up
en|to this moment I had been very well content to wait for. I wonder, I
en|repeat, if such longings never come to the very rich who could indulge them with
en|ease and without any personal struggle. Perhaps not; or one would hear something of it.
en|As it is, the Quixotes of private life are seldom millionaires. I could do nothing;
en|and perhaps it was for this reason that I desired with such painful yearning what
en|was thus absolutely out of my power. I have to admit, amid all these most
en|serious thoughts, a curious delusion, as I suppose I must call it, which accompanied me
en|wherever I went. It seemed to me that I constantly met the same figure which
en|had encountered me in the grounds at Ellermore and warned me of Colin’s danger. I
en|took myself to task about it in every way, trying to find out some unsteadiness
en|of nerve, some functional derangement, which could account for it. But I was quite well--my
en|mind was far too much occupied and excited to leave me any time to consider
en|the body, which went along swiftly and easily, occupied with everything rather than itself. These,
en|I think, are the conditions of perfect health; and I was as well as ever
en|I had been in my life. Yet constantly I was conscious of meeting about the
en|streets this veiled and shadowy woman. She would come towards me, so that we encountered
en|each other, face to face, or she would go softly past me, brushing me with
en|her dress, making all my pulses beat wildly. This occurred chiefly in the neighbourhood in
en|which the tragedy had happened; but there were other places far enough from that in
en|which the same strange apparition was visible. Sometimes I could perceive in her that familiar
en|custom, the wringing of the hands, which reminded me of Charlotte. Sometimes I seemed almost
en|to penetrate the obscurity of the veil, and recognise a face not unlike Charlotte’s. I
en|got used to this imagination. I persuaded myself that it was nothing but an impression
en|on my brain, which I could not get rid of, but which was altogether illusory.
en|Though why my heart should leap up in me, and all my pulses throb, because
en|of a thing which was nothing, I could not understand. The last time I thought
en|I saw her was by the grave in which, with a silent misery beyond words,
en|we laid poor Colin. Charley and I alone accompanied him to that last resting-place. Our
en|friend the doctor had managed, I cannot tell how, to keep the wife and her
en|family from attending, as they had all intended to do, in full panoply of woe.
en|He told them, I don’t know what--that it was a thing ladies of social importance
en|never did, a point upon which Mrs. Colin was very susceptible--or some other argument of
en|this description. Anyhow, he succeeded in keeping any such vulgarising element away from the simple
en|ceremonial of the funeral. We followed him alone, Charley and I. Charlotte did not dare
en|to leave her father for so long a time without explanation, and Charley shrank with
en|a painful susceptibility from the sight of everybody he knew. Without any of that mocking
en|garniture of flowers which has become a matter of fashion and vanity, without any indifferent
en|retinue, we two stood by the grave, the young brother with a heart-breaking control of
en|grief, and I with all the reverence of a pity scarcely less heart-rending. When I
en|lifted my eyes from the “deep-delved” bed of utter silence and quiet, I thought I
en|saw her standing by the edge of it, wringing her hands. The sound of a
en|stifled sob from Charley called my attention away for a moment, and when I looked
en|again she was gone. The face--the gesture was like Charlotte. It is impossible for me
en|to describe the mingled tenderness and terror with which I perceived this--as if it might
en|have been Charlotte herself in the spirit who had come forth in sheer longing to
en|her brother’s grave. “Did you see that lady?” I asked Charley, as we went home.
en|“What lady?” he said fretfully. He was irritable with grief, and misery, and shame; for
en|he had never been able to get over this accessory of the terrible family misfortune,
en|and his mind, poor fellow, was distracted with thinking what to do, and how to
en|manage the complicated business which had come into his hands. Then he begged my pardon
en|piteously. “I don’t know what I’m doing. To think yon was Colin, and that’s all
en|over with him--him that had more head than us all put together; and if he
en|had only held his hand might have put all right. I would have set my
en|strength to his,” cried the young fellow, unable to restrain himself, “shoulder to shoulder; and
en|we would have redeemed everything.” Then, after a pause, during which he turned away his
en|head to dash off the hot, quick gathering tears, “Did you say there was a
en|lady? I took no notice. It would be his wife.” I did not say anything
en|more; but I knew very well it was not Colin’s wife. Who was it? or
en|was it nothing more than a delusion, the offspring of my own overwrought and excited
en|brain? In a few days after I went to the railway with them to see
en|them go away. Mr. Campbell had never mended more than he did the first night.
en|His hand and one side were almost without power, and his mind had fallen into
en|a state which it would be cruel to call imbecility. It was more like the
en|mind of a child recovering from an illness, pleased with, and exacting constant attention. Now
en|and then he would ask questions innumerable. What had become of Colin, if he was
en|ill, if he had gone home? “The best place for him, the best place for
en|him, Chatty,” he would repeat; “and if you got him persuaded to marry, that would
en|be fine.” All this Charlotte had to bear with a placid face, with gentle agreement;
en|and now that Charley was here, I had passed altogether from his mind. When he
en|saw me he made me little apologies about not being in a state to receive
en|strangers. “You see, I am recovering from a severe illness,” he would say. “Tell Mr.
en|Temple, Chatty, how ill I have been.” He was in this condition when I took
en|leave of him in the invalid carriage they had secured for the journey. They had
en|all the habits of luxury, and never hesitated, as people accustomed to the daily sacrifices
en|of poverty would have done, at this expense. He told me that he was glad
en|to go home; that he would have left London some time before but for Chatty,
en|who “wanted to see a little of the place.” “I am going to join my
en|son Colin, who has gone home before us--isn’t that so, Chatty?” “Yes, father,” she said.
en|“Yes, yes; I have grown rather doited, and very, very silly,”[B] the old man said,
en|in a tone of extraordinary pathos. “I am sometimes not sure of what I am
en|saying; but Chatty keeps me right. Colin has gone on before; he has a grand
en|head for business; he will soon set everything right--connected,” he added, with a curious sense
en|which seemed to have outlived his other powers, that explanation of Colin’s actions was necessary--“connected
en|with my retirement. I am past business; but we’ll still hope to see you at
en|Ellermore.” At Ellermore! Charlotte raised her eyes to mine with a look of anguish, of
en|self-control, and steadfast patience, which was almost sublime. While he spoke thus her hands sought
en|and clasped each other with the same movement I had noted in another. In another--in
en|whom? CHAPTER VI Then there ensued a period of total stillness in my life. It
en|seemed to me as if all interest had gone out of it. I resumed my
en|old occupations, such as they were, and they were not very engrossing. I had enough,
en|which is perhaps of all conditions of life, if the most comfortable, the least interesting.
en|If it was a disciple of Solomon who desired that state, it must have been
en|when he was like his master, _blasé_, and had discovered that both ambition and pleasure
en|were vanity. There was little place or necessity for me in the world. I pleased
en|myself, as people say. When I was tired of my solitary chambers, I went and
en|paid visits. When I was tired of England, I went abroad. Nothing could be more
en|agreeable, or more unutterably tedious, especially to one who had even accidentally come across and
en|touched upon a real and bustling life. Needless to say that I thought of the
en|household at Ellermore almost without intermission. Charlotte wrote to me now and then, and it
en|sometimes seemed to me that I was the most callous wretch on earth, sitting there
en|watching all they were doing, tracing every step and vicissitude of their trouble in my
en|own assured well-being. It was monstrous, yet what could I do? They would not have
en|accepted the help of my small sufficiency. But if, as I have said, such impatient
en|desire to help were to come now and then to those who have the power
en|to do so, is political economy so infallible that the world would not be the
en|better for it? There was not a word of complaint in Charlotte’s letters, but they
en|made me rage over my impotence. She told me that all the arrangements were being
en|completed for the sale of Ellermore, but that her father’s condition was still such that
en|they did not know how to communicate to him the impending change. “He is still
en|ignorant of all that has passed,” Charlotte wrote, “and asks me the most heart-rending questions;
en|and I hope God will forgive me all that I am obliged to say to
en|him. We are afraid to let him see anyone lest he should discover the truth;
en|for indeed falsehood, even with a good meaning, is always its own punishment. Dr. Maxwell,
en|who does not mind what he says when he thinks it is for his patient’s
en|good, is going to make believe to send him away for change of air; and
en|this is the artifice we will have to keep up all the rest of his
en|life to account for not going back to Ellermore.” She wrote another time that there
en|was every hope of being able to dispose of it by private bargain, and that
en|in the meantime friends had been very kind, and the “works” were going on. There
en|was not a word in the letter by which it could have been divined that
en|to leave Ellermore was to the writer anything beyond a matter of necessity. She said
en|not a word about her birthplace, the home of all her associations, the spot which
en|I knew was so dear. There had been no hesitation, and there was no repining.
en|Provided only that the poor old man, the stricken father, deprived at once of his
en|home and firstborn, without knowing either, might be kept in that delusion--this was all the
en|exemption Charlotte sought. And I do not think they asked me to go to them
en|before they left the place. It was my own doing. I could not keep away
en|any longer. I said to Charlotte, and perhaps also to myself, by way of excuse,
en|that I might help to take care of Mr. Campbell during the removal. The fact
en|was that I could not stay away from her any longer. I could have risked
en|any intrusion, thrust myself in anyhow, for the mere sake of being near her and
en|helping her in the most insignificant way. From the time of their leaving London, the
en|appearance I had seen so often had disappeared. I need not say that I thought
en|upon it often enough to have raised up--had it been dependent upon my thoughts--appearances in
en|any number; but this one never came again. I tried in my own mind every
en|way to account for it. That it was a mere delusion of my excited eyes
en|and brain I could not believe, for I had been aware of no excitement or
en|reason for it when I first saw her in the shrubbery at Ellermore; and if
en|imagination was enough to produce such an image, how much more reason was there now
en|that it should have come back to me! And then I thought, which gave me
en|a certain pleasure, of a possibility which had occurred to me, that Charlotte’s anxious heart
en|and thoughts had somehow assumed a shadowy form, a sort of veil of substance, and
en|that it was she herself unawares who had haunted me. If our deepest thoughts could
en|thus take form, how often, when we ourselves were elsewhere, might a visionary shadow of
en|us be about those we love? It would be little wonder, I said to myself,
en|if Charlotte were to see _me_, under the trees or by the loch side at
en|Ellermore. Often and often, seated in my rooms, I had been there in the spirit
en|following her, remembering what she would probably be doing at that moment, flitting after her
en|from room to room. This was a solution of the mystery that was very sweet
en|to me. I said to myself, it might very well be that only to one
en|entirely in sympathy with the spirit thus gone out of itself in passionate yearning could
en|it be visible in its love-pilgrimage. Therefore I felt, with a subdued humility, that it
en|was very unlikely she would see any adumbration of my longing and lingering about her;
en|but that I should see her was very natural. And this explained so entirely why
en|I saw nothing now. It was not me she had been thinking of, but of
en|Colin, both living and dead--all the dreadful tragedy of his soon-ended story. If she had
en|ever admitted me to any such place in her thoughts, no doubt I should have
en|seen her now. I do not give this as a theory by which such apparitions
en|might be accounted for, I only state it as one of the many thinkings on
en|the subject which filled my mind, and the one which gave me most pleasure. I
en|thought that nobody save Charlotte herself--not even a visitor out of the unseen--could have so
en|made my heart beat; but it was all fanciful, founded upon nothing, a supposition among
en|so many other suppositions. It was nearly Christmas when the insistence of myself with myself
en|that I could stay away no longer came to a crisis. They were to leave
en|Ellermore in a week or two. Mr. Campbell had been persuaded that one of the
en|soft and sheltered spots where Scotch invalids are sent in Scotland would be better for
en|him. Charlotte had written to me, with a half despair, of the difficulties of their
en|removal. “My heart almost fails me,” she said; and that was a great deal for
en|her to say. After this I could hesitate no longer. She was afraid even of
en|the revival of life that might take place when her father was brought out of
en|his seclusion, of some injudicious old friend who could not be staved off, and who
en|might talk to him about Colin. “My heart almost fails me.” I went up to
en|Scotland by the mail train that night, and next day, while it was still not
en|much more than noon, found myself at Ellermore. What a change! The heather had all
en|died away from the hills; the sun-bright loch was steely blue; the white threads of
en|water down every crevice in the mountains were swollen to torrents. Here and there on
en|the higher peaks there was a sprinkling of snow. The fir trees were the only
en|substantial things in the nearer landscape. The bushes stood about all bare and feathery, with
en|every twig distinct against the blue. The sun was shining almost as brightly as in
en|summer, and scattered a shimmer of reflections everywhere over the wet grass, and across the
en|rivulets that were running in every little hollow. The house stood out among all this
en|light, amid the bare tracery of the trees, with its Scotch-French _tourelles_, and the sweep
en|of emerald lawn, more green than ever at its feet, with all the naked flower-beds;
en|the blue smoke rising peacefully into the air, the door open as always. There was
en|little stir or movement, however, in this wintry scene. The outdoor life was checked. There
en|was no son at home to leave traces of his presence. The lodge was shut
en|up and vacant. I concluded that the carriage had been given up, and all luxuries,
en|and the coachman and his family were gone. But this was all the visible difference.
en|I was received by one of the maids, with whose face I was familiar. There
en|had never been any wealth of male attendants at Ellermore, and this did not strike
en|me as unusual. She took me into the drawing-room, which was deserted, and bore a
en|more formal look than of old. “Miss Charlotte is mostly with her papa,” the woman
en|said. “He is very frail, but just wonderful contented, like a bairn. She’s always up
en|the stair with the old gentleman. It’s no’ good for her. You’ll find her white,
en|white, sir, and no’ like hersel’.” In a few minutes Charlotte came in. There was
en|a gleam of pleasure (I hoped) on her face, but she was white, white, as
en|the woman said; worn and pale. After the first greeting, which had brightened her, she
en|broke down a little, and shed a few hasty tears, for which she excused herself,
en|faltering that everything came back, but that she was glad, glad to see me! And
en|then she added quickly, that I might not be wounded, “It has come to that,
en|that I can scarcely ever leave my father; and to keep up the deception is
en|terrible.” “You must not say deception.” “Oh, it is nothing else; and that always punishes
en|itself. It is just the terror of my life that some accident will happen; that
en|he will find out everything at once.” Then she looked at me steadily, with a
en|smile that was piteous to see, “Mr. Temple, Ellermore is sold.” “Is it so--is it
en|so?” I said, with a sort of groan. I had still thought that perhaps at
en|the last moment something might occur to prevent the sacrifice. She shook her head, not
en|answering my words, but the expression of my face. “There was nothing else to be
en|desired,” she said; and, after a pause, “We are to take him to the Bridge
en|of Allan. He is almost pleased to go; he thinks of nothing further--oh, poor old
en|man, poor old man! If only I had him there safe; but I am more
en|terrified for the journey than I ever was for anything in my life.” We talked
en|of this for some time, and of all the arrangements she had made. Charley was
en|to come to assist in removing his father; but I think that my presence somehow
en|seemed to her an additional safeguard, of which she was glad. She did not stay
en|more than half an hour with me. “It will be dull, dull for you, Mr.
en|Temple,” she said, with more of the lingering cadence of her national accent than I
en|had perceived before--or perhaps it struck me more after these months of absence. “There is
en|nobody at home but the little ones, and they have grown far too wise for
en|their age, because of the many things that they know must never be told to
en|papa; but you know the place, and you will want to rest a little.” She
en|put out her hand to me again. “And I am glad, glad to see you.”
en|Nothing in my life ever made my heart swell like those simple words. That she
en|should be “glad, glad” was payment enough for anything I could do. But in the
en|meantime there was nothing that I could do. I wandered about the silent place till
en|I was tired, recalling a hundred pleasant recollections. Even to me, a stranger, who a
en|year ago had never seen Ellermore, it was hard to give it up; and as
en|for those who had been born there, and their fathers before them, it seemed too
en|much for the cruellest fate to ask. But nature was as indifferent to the passing
en|away of the human inhabitants, whose little spell of a few hundred years was as
en|nothing in her long history, as she would have been to the falling of a
en|rock on the hillside, or the wrenching up of a tree in the woods. For
en|that matter, of so small account are men, the rock and tree would both have
en|been older dwellers than the Campbells; and why for that should the sun moderate his
en|shining, or the clear skies veil themselves? Afterwards I went in and wandered about the
en|house, which was so silent. A subdued sound indeed came from the children’s rooms, and
en|when I knocked at the door I was received with a tumult of delight; but
en|next moment little Mary lifted her small finger and said, “Oh, Harry! oh, Katie! how
en|can you make a noise and disturb papa!” The old man in his chamber dominated
en|the whole house; the absolute quiet of it and desertion (when the children went out
en|for their afternoon walk) had an indescribable effect upon my mind. It was as if
en|the chamber, still and clean, all garnished and decked as for daily living, yet empty
en|of all visible life, was full of beings unseen, for whom and for whose pleasure
en|they existed. A kind of awe stole over me when I sat down in one
en|of these rooms. I felt myself out of place there--as if all the solemn visitors
en|in their old house must resent the presence of a stranger. Yes, I was a
en|stranger; even Charlotte herself had called me so, though no one had been so near
en|to her, or had so much to do with her life in the last crisis.
en|It gave me a sort of bitter pleasure to think this, even though I might
en|be disowned by those others, as having nothing to do with the house. My mind
en|was so taken up by these thoughts that it was almost inadvertence that took me,
en|in the course of my solitary rambles about, to the Lady’s Walk. I had nearly
en|got within the line of the birch trees, however, when I was brought hurriedly back
en|to the strange circumstances which had formed an accompaniment to their family history. It gave
en|me a shock and start to hear once more the footsteps of the guardian of
en|Ellermore. She had come back, then! After that first thrill of instinctive emotion this gave
en|me a singular pleasure. I stood between the trees and heard the soft step coming
en|and going with absolute satisfaction. It seemed to me that they were not altogether abandoned
en|so long as she was here. My heart rose in spite of myself. I began
en|to speculate on the possibility even yet of saving the old house. I asked myself
en|how it could be finally disposed of without Mr. Campbell’s consent and signature; and tried
en|to believe that at the last moment some way might open, some wonderful windfall come.
en|But when I turned back to the house, this fantastic confidence naturally failed me. I
en|began to contemplate the other side of the question--the new people who would come in.
en|Perhaps “some Englishman,” as Charley had said with a certain scorn; some rich man, who
en|would buy the moors and lochs at many times their actual value, and bring down,
en|perhaps, a horde of Cockney sportsmen to banish all quiet and poetry from Ellermore. I
en|thought with mingled pity and anger of what the Lady would do in such hands.
en|Would she still haunt her favourite walk when all whom she loved were gone? Would
en|she stay there in forlorn faithfulness to the soil, or would she go with her
en|banished race? Or would she depart altogether, and cut the tie that had bound her
en|to earth? I thought--for fancy once set out goes far without any conscious control from
en|the mind--that it might be possible that the intruders into the home of the Campbells
en|should be frightened by noises and apparitions, and all those vulgarer powers of the unseen
en|of which we hear sometimes. If the Lady of Ellermore would condescend to use such
en|instruments, no doubt she might find lower and less elevated spirits in the unseen to
en|whom this kind of play would be congenial. I caught myself up sharply in this
en|wandering of thought, as if I were forming ideas derogatory to a dear friend, and
en|felt myself redden with shame. She connect her lovely being with tricks of this kind!
en|I was angry with myself, as if I had allowed it to be suggested that
en|Charlotte would do so. My heart grew full as I pursued these thoughts. Was it
en|possible that some mysterious bond of a kind beyond our knowledge connected her with this
en|beloved soil? I was overawed by the thought of what she might suffer, going upon
en|her solitary watch, to see the house filled with an alien family; yet, perhaps, by
en|and by, taking them into amity, watching over them as she had done over her
en|own, in that sweetness of self-forgetfulness and tender love of humankind which is the atmosphere
en|of the blessed. All through this spiritual being was to me a beatified shadow of
en|Charlotte. I felt that this was what she might be capable of doing, if it
en|were possible that those whom she loved most were no longer dependent upon her care.
en|You will say all this was very fantastic, and I do not deny that the
en|sentence is just. Next day passed in something the same way. Charlotte was very anxious.
en|She had wished the removal to take place that afternoon, but when the moment came
en|she was afraid. She said “To-morrow,” with a shiver. “I don’t know what I am
en|afraid of,” she said, “but my heart fails me--my heart fails me.” I had to
en|telegraph to Charley that it was deferred, and another long day went by. It rained,
en|and that was an obstacle. “I cannot take him away in bad weather,” she said.
en|She came downstairs to me a dozen times a day, wringing her hands. “I have
en|no resolution,” she cried. “I cannot--I cannot make up my mind to it. I feel
en|that something dreadful is going to happen.” I could only take her trembling hand and
en|try to comfort her. “But if it must be done,” I ventured to say, “you
en|will be happier when it is over.” She gave me a wild look of panic.
en|“I don’t know what I am afraid of,” she said. “I wish it might be
en|taken out of my hands.” I understood her, and I made all the arrangements. Next
en|day, at noon, was to be the time. I ordered a carriage from the nearest
en|town, not without feeling the risk that the old man might perceive it was not
en|his own, and inquire into the meaning of it. Every step of the way was
en|beset by risks; but still, if it had to be done--“if ’twere done when ’tis
en|done, then it were well it were done quickly.” Those words had haunted me before.
en|I settled everything. I made her come out with me to get a little air
en|in the afternoon. “You are killing yourself,” I said. “It is this that makes you
en|so nervous and unlike yourself.” She consented, though it was against her will. A woman
en|who had been all her life in their service, who was to go with them,
en|whom Charlotte treated, as she said, “like one of ourselves,” had charge of Mr. Campbell
en|in the meantime. And I think Charlotte got a little pleasure from her unusual excursion.
en|She was very tremulous, as if she had almost forgotten the way to walk, and
en|leant upon my arm in a way which was very sweet to me. No word
en|of love had ever passed between us; and she did not love me, save as
en|she loved Charley and Harry, and the rest. I think I had a place among
en|them, at the end of the brothers. But yet she had an instinctive knowledge of
en|my heart. And she knew that to lean upon me, to show that she needed
en|me, was the way to please me most. We wandered about there for a time
en|in a sort of forlorn happiness; then, with mutual impulse, took our way to the
en|Lady’s Walk. We stood there together, listening to the steps. “Do you hear them?” said
en|Charlotte, her face lighting up with a smile. “Dear lady! that has always been here
en|since ever I mind.” She spoke as the children spoke in the utter abandonment of
en|her being, as if returning for refreshment to the full simplicity of accent and idiom,
en|the soft, native speech to which she was born. “Will she stay after us, do
en|ye think?” Charlotte said; and then, with a little start, clinging to my arm, “Was
en|that a sound--was that a cry?” Not a cry, but a sigh. It seemed to
en|wander over all the woods and thrill among the trees. You will say it was
en|only the wind. I cannot tell. To me it was a sigh, personal, heart-rending. And
en|you may suppose what it was to her. The tears dropped from her full eyes.
en|She said, speaking to the air, “We are parting, you and me. Oh, go you
en|back to heaven, and let us trouble you no more. Oh, go back to your
en|home, my bonnie lady, and let us trouble you no more!” “Charlotte,” I said, taking
en|her arm in mine to support her. She cast me a glance, a smile, like
en|one who could not, even in the midst of the highest thoughts, neglect or be
en|unkind, but drew her hand away and clasped it in the other. “We are of
en|one stock,” she said, the tears always falling, “and the same heart. We are too
en|anxious; but God is above us all. Go back to your pleasant place, and say
en|to my mother that I will never leave them. Go away, my bonnie lady, go
en|away! and trust us to God.” We waited, and I think she almost expected some
en|reply. But there was none. I took her arm within mine again, and led her
en|away trembling. The moment, the excitement had been too much for me also. I said,
en|“You tell her to go, that she is too anxious, that she must trust you
en|to God--and in the same breath you pledge yourself never to leave them. Do you
en|think if God does not want her He wants you to stand between Him and
en|them?” I grasped her arm so closely and held it so to my side in
en|my passion that I think I almost hurt her. She gave me a startled look,
en|and put up her hand to dry her wet eyes. “It is very different,” she
en|said; “I am living and can work for them. It has come to me all
en|in a moment to think that one is just like another after all. Perhaps to
en|die does not make a woman wise any more than life does. And it may
en|be that nobody has had the thought to tell her. She will think that she
en|can stop any harm that is coming, being here; but if it was not God’s
en|pleasure to stop it, how could she? You know she tried,” said Charlotte, looking at
en|me wistfully; “she tried--God bless her for that! Oh, you know how anxious she was;
en|but neither her nor me could do it--neither her nor me!” At this moment we
en|were interrupted by someone flying towards us from the house, calling, “Miss Charlotte, Miss Charlotte!
en|you are wanted,” in a wild and agitated tone. It was the woman who had
en|been left in charge of Mr. Campbell, and Charlotte started at the sight of her.
en|She drew her hand from my arm, and flew along the path. “Oh, Margaret, why
en|did you leave him?” she said. “It was no blame of mine,” said the woman,
en|turning, following her mistress. I hurried on too, after them, and the explanation was to
en|both of us. “He would come down to the library; nothing would stop him. I
en|tried all I could; but what could I do? And there is nothing to be
en|frighted for, Miss Charlotte. Ah! I’ve nae breath to tell it. He is just real
en|like himself.” Charlotte flew along the path like a creature flying for life. She paused
en|an instant at the door of the house to beckon me to follow her. The
en|library, the room where her father had gone, was one of those which had been
en|partially dismantled. The pictures had been taken down from the walls, a number of books,
en|which she meant to take with her, collected on the tables. Mr. Campbell had displaced
en|some of the books in order to seat himself in his favourite seat. He looked
en|at her curiously, almost with severity, as she came in anxious and breathless. He was
en|greatly changed. He had been robust and hale, like a tower, when I first entered
en|Ellermore, not yet six months since. Now he had shrunken away into half his size.
en|The coat which he had not worn for months hung loosely upon him; his white
en|hair was long, and he wore a beard, which changed his appearance greatly. All this
en|change had come since the time I parted with him in London, when he told
en|me he was going to join his son Colin; but there was another change more
en|remarkable, which I with awe, and Charlotte with terror, recognised at a glance--the prostration of
en|his mind was gone. He looked his daughter in the face with intelligent, almost sternly
en|intelligent, eyes. “Oh, father, you have wanted me!” Charlotte cried. “I went out for a
en|mouthful of air--I went out--for a few minutes”-- “Why should you not have gone out,
en|Chatty?” he said. “And why was Margaret left in charge of me? I have been
en|ill, I make no doubt; but why should I be watched and spied about my
en|own house?” She gave me a glance of dismay, and then she faltered, “Oh, not
en|that, father--not that!” “But I tell you it was that. She would have hindered my
en|coming downstairs, that woman”--he gave a little laugh, which was terrible to us in the
en|state of our feelings--“and here are you rushing in out of breath, as if there
en|was some cause of fear. Who is that behind ye? Is it one of your
en|brothers--or”-- “It is Mr. Temple, father,” she said, with a new alarm. “Mr. Temple,” he
en|said, with a shade of displeasure passing over his face. Then he recovered himself, and
en|his old-world politeness. “I am glad to see ye,” he said. “So far as I
en|can remember, the house was much disorganised when you were here before, Mr. Temple. You
en|will think we are always out of order; but I’ve been ill, and everything has
en|fallen out of gear. This is not a place,” he added, turning to Charlotte, “to
en|receive a stranger in. What is all this for?” he added, in a sharp tone,
en|waving his hands towards the books, of which some were heaped at his feet on
en|the floor. Once more she made a pause of dismay. “They are some books to
en|take with us,” she said; “you remember, father, we are going away.” “Going away!” he
en|cried irritably. “Where are my letters? Where are your brothers? What are you doing with
en|a gentleman visitor (I beg ye a thousand pardons, Mr. Temple!) and the place in
en|such a state? It is my opinion that there is something wrong. Where are my
en|letters? It is not in reason that there can be no letters. After being cast
en|aside from business for a time, to have your letters kept back from you, you
en|will allow, Mr. Temple,” he said, turning to me with an explanatory air, “is irritating.
en|It is perhaps done with a mistaken notion that I am not equal to them;
en|but if you think I will allow myself to be treated as a child”-- He
en|stammered a little now and then, in his anger, then made a great effort to
en|control himself. And then he looked up at us, once more a little severely, and
en|brought confusion to all our hopes with one simple question. “Where is Colin?” he said.
en|What could be more natural? Charlotte gave me one look, and stood, white as death,
en|motionless, her fingers twisting together. How truly she had said that falsehood was its own
en|punishment, even such falsehood as this. She had answered him with ambiguous words when he
en|was in the state of feebleness from which he had thus awoke, and he had
en|been easily satisfied and diverted from too close inquiry. But now she was confounded by
en|the sudden question. She could not confront with a subterfuge her father’s serious eyes; her
en|head drooped, her hands caught at each other with a pitiful clasp, while he sat
en|looking at her with an authoritative, but as yet unalarmed, look. All this time the
en|door had been left ajar, and Margaret stood waiting outside, listening to all that went
en|on, without any thought of wrong, too much interested and anxious to feel herself out
en|of place. But when she heard this demand the woman was struck with horror. She
en|made a step within the door. “Oh, Ellermore!” she cried. “Oh! my auld maister, dinna
en|break her heart and mine! To hear ye asking for Colin, and Colin in his
en|grave this four long months, poor lad, poor lad!” She threw her apron over her
en|head as she spoke, and burst forth into loud sobs and tears. Charlotte had put
en|out a hand to stop the revelation, but dropped it again, and stood by speechless,
en|her head bent, and wringing her hands, a silent image of grief and guilt, as
en|if it had been her from whom the blow came. The old man sat and
en|listened with a countenance growing ashy pale, and with intent eyes, that seemed to flicker
en|as if beyond his control. He tried to speak, but in the trembling of his
en|lips could articulate nothing. Then he slowly raised himself up and stood, pallid and dizzy,
en|like a man on the edge of a precipice. “My son is dead, and I
en|knew it not,” he said slowly, pausing between the words. He stood with his trembling
en|lips falling apart, his countenance all moving and twitching, transfixed, it seemed, by a sort
en|of woeful amaze, wondering at himself. Then he turned upon Charlotte, with a piteous appeal.
en|“Was I told, and have I forgotten?” he asked. The humiliation of that human overthrow
en|overpowered his re-awakened soul. She came to him quickly, and put her arm round him.
en|“Father dear, you were so ill, they would not let us tell you. Oh, I
en|have known--I have known it would be so much the worse when it came.” He
en|put her away from him, and sat down again feebly in his chair. In that
en|dreadful moment he wanted no one. The horror of the individual humiliation, the idea that
en|he could have heard and forgotten, was more terrible even than the dreadful novelty which
en|thus burst upon him. “I’m glad,” he said, “I’m glad,” babbling with his loose lips.
en|I shrank away, feeling it a profanation to be here, a spectator of the last
en|mystery of nature; but Charlotte made a faint motion that kept me from withdrawing altogether.
en|For the first time she was afraid; her heart had failed her. For some minutes
en|her father continued silent in his chair. The sunset had faded away, the misty twilight
en|was falling. Margaret, guilty and miserable, but still unable altogether to subdue her sobs, shaking
en|her white apron from her head, and looking round with a deprecating, apologetic glance, had
en|withdrawn to the other side of the room. All was silence after that broken interchange
en|of words. He lay back, clasping and unclasping his hands, his lips and features all
en|moving, whether with a wish to speak or with the mere workings of emotions unspeakable,
en|I cannot tell. When suddenly, all at once, with the voice of a strong man,
en|loud and full, he broke out into the cry which has sounded through all the
en|world--the utterance of every father’s anguish. “Oh, Absalom, my son, my son! Would God that
en|I had died for thee, my son, my son!” We both rushed towards him simultaneously.
en|He did not remark me, fortunately; but again he put Charlotte away. “What are you
en|afraid for?” he said, almost sternly; “that I will forget again? That is not possible.
en|Ye think sorrow kills; but no, it stings ye back to life. It stings ye
en|back to life,” he repeated, raising himself in his chair. Then he looked round him
en|solemnly. “Margaret, my woman, come here, and give me your hand. We’re partners in trouble,
en|you and me, and never shall we part. As long as this is my house
en|there is a place in it for you. Afterwards, when it goes to--ah! when it
en|goes to Charley,” he cried, with a sudden burst of unforeseen sobs. Charlotte looked at
en|me again. Her face was white with despair. How was this last news to be
en|broken to him? “Father,” she said, standing behind him, “you are sorely tried. Will you
en|not come back to your room and rest till to-morrow, and then you will hear
en|all? Then we will tell you--about all that has happened”-- Her voice shook like a
en|leaf in the wind, but she managed to show no other sign of her terror
en|and despair. There was a long pause after this, and we stood waiting, not knowing
en|how the moment would terminate. I believe it was the sight of me that decided
en|it after all. A quick movement of irritation passed over his face. “I think you
en|are right, Chatty,” he said; “I think you are right. I am not fit, in
en|my shattered state, and with the information I have just received, to pay the attention
en|I would like to pay”--He paused, and looked at me fixedly. “It is a great
en|trouble to me that we have never been able to show you proper attention, Mr.
en|Temple. You see, my son was detained; and now he is dead, and I’ve never
en|known it till this moment. You will excuse a reception which is not the kind
en|of reception I would like to give you.” He waved his hand. “You were my
en|Colin’s friend. You will know how to make allowances. Yes, my dear, I am best
en|in my own chamber. I will just go, with Mr. Temple’s permission--go--to my bed.” A
en|faint groan burst from him as he said these words; a kind of dreary smile
en|flickered on his lips. “To my bed,” he repeated; “that is all we can do,
en|we old folk, when we are stricken by God’s hand. Lie down, and turn our
en|faces to the wall--our faces to the wall.” He rose up, and took his daughter’s
en|arm, and made a few steps towards the door, which I was holding open for
en|him. Then he turned and looked round with the air of one who has a
en|favour to bestow. “You may come too, Margaret,” he said. “You can come and help
en|me to my bed.” This strange interruption of all plans, which it was evident filled
en|Charlotte with despair, gave me much to think of, as I stayed behind in the
en|slowly darkening room. It was evident that now nothing could be concealed from him; and
en|who was there so bold as to tell the bereaved father, in his first grief
en|for his firstborn, what horrors had accompanied Colin’s death, and what a penalty the family
en|had to pay? I cast over in my mind every expedient, but nothing seemed practicable,
en|no way of disclosing the situation without such a shock as might kill. The half-dismantled
en|room looked more and more dreary as the light faded away. The door stood open
en|as when that little procession quitted it. My senses were all on the alert. It
en|seemed to me that the premonition of some fresh calamity was in the air; and
en|when Charlotte came down about half an hour later, like a ghost through the dim-coming
en|shadows, I almost expected to hear that it had already occurred. But even in these
en|depths of distress, it was a happiness to me to feel that she came to
en|me for relief. She dropped into a chair close by the window where I was
en|standing. I could just see the soft, pale lines of her face--the look of restrained
en|anxiety in her eyes. She told me that he had gone to bed without asking
en|any further questions, and that Margaret, who had been Colin’s nurse, seemed almost more agreeable
en|to him than herself. He had turned his face to the wall, as he had
en|said, and nothing but a long-drawn, occasional sigh told that he was awake. “I think
en|he is not worse--in body,” she said. “He has borne it far better than we
en|could have thought possible. But how am I to tell him the way it happened,
en|and how am I to tell him about Ellermore?” She wept with a prostration and
en|self-abandonment which alarmed me; but she stopped my remonstrances and entreaties with a motion of
en|her hand. “Oh, let me cry! It is the only ease I have,” she said.
en|When she had gone away from me, restless, anxious, afraid to be out of hearing,
en|I went out, myself, as restless, as incapable of banishing all these anxieties from my
en|mind as she. The night was almost dark, soft and mild. It was one of
en|those nights when the moon, without being visible, softens and ameliorates the gloom, and makes
en|of night a sort of twilight. While I went pacing softly about, to occupy myself,
en|a soft, small rain began to fall; but this did not affect me in any
en|way. It was rather soothing than disagreeable. I went down to the side of the
en|loch, where the pale light on the water was touched by innumerable droppings of the
en|rain; then up again, round and round the house, not caring where I went. At
en|this hour I had always avoided the Lady’s Walk, I can scarcely tell why. To-night,
en|in my strange familiarity with everything, and carelessness of all but one subject, I suddenly
en|turned into it with a caprice I could not account for, perhaps with an understood
en|wish for company, for somebody who might understand my thoughts. The mystic footsteps gave me
en|a sort of pleasure. Whether it was habit, or some new sense of human fellowship
en|which Charlotte’s impassioned words had caused, I can scarcely tell; but the excitement with which
en|I had always hitherto regarded the mysterious watcher here was altogether gone out of my
en|mind. I felt a profound and tender pity for her rising in me instead. Was
en|it possible that a spirit could be “over-anxious,” as Charlotte said, endeavouring vainly, and yet
en|not undutifully, to take God’s supreme guardianship out of His hands? The thought was new
en|to me. To think that a good and blessed creature could so err, could mistake
en|so humanly and persevere so patiently, though never able to remedy the evils, seemed somehow
en|more possible than that a guardian from heaven could watch and watch for generations with
en|so little result. This gave me a great compassion for the lonely watcher thus rebelling
en|in a heavenly way of love against the law of nature that separated her from
en|visible life. My old idea that it might be Charlotte herself in an unconscious shadow-shape,
en|whose protecting, motherly love made these efforts unawares, glided gratefully into the feeling that it
en|was an earlier Charlotte, her very kin and prototype, who could not even now let
en|God manage her race without her aid. While I was thus thinking, I was startled
en|once more by the same sigh which I had heard with Charlotte. Yes, yes, it
en|might be the wind. I had no time to bandy explanations with myself. It was
en|a soft, long sigh, such as draws the very breath out of an over-laden bosom.
en|I turned half round, it was so near to me, and there, by my side,
en|so close that I could have touched her, stood the Lady whom I had imagined
en|so often--the same figure which I had met in the London streets and in the
en|woods of Ellermore. I suppose I stepped back, with a little thrill of the old
en|sensations, for she seemed to put out a hand in the pale gloom, and began
en|to speak softly, quickly, as if there was scarcely time enough for what she had
en|to say. “I am going away like the rest,” she said. “None of them have
en|ever bid me go before; but it was true--it was true. I have never done
en|any good--just frightened them, or pleased them. It is in better hands--it is in better
en|hands.” With this there came the familiar movement, the wringing of the hands, which was
en|like Charlotte, and she seemed to weep; but before I could say anything (and what
en|could I have said?) she said again mournfully, “I must not speak to them; but
en|you wish them well, and you may help if you will--if you will--now, again, again!”
en|“How can I help?” I cried. “Tell me, Lady, whoever you are; I will do
en|it. I will do it--but how can I do it? I have no power. Tell
en|me”-- I put out my hand to touch her dress, but it melted out of
en|my hold. “You may help if you will--if you will,” she seemed to say, with
en|a breathless faintness, as if of haste; and already her voice was farther off, breathing
en|away. “What can I do?” I cried. So much had I forgot the old terror
en|that I put myself in her path, stopping the way. “Tell me how, how! Tell
en|me, for God’s sake, and because of Charlotte.” The shadowy figure seemed to retreat before
en|me. It seemed to fade, then reappeared, then dissolved altogether into the white dimness, while
en|the voice floated away, still saying, as in a sigh, “If you will, if you
en|will.” I could hear no more. I went after this sighing voice to the end
en|of the walk. It seemed to me that I was pursuing her, determined to understand,
en|and that she softly fled; the footsteps hurrying, becoming almost inaudible as they flew before
en|me. I went on hotly, not knowing what I did, determined only to know what
en|it was; to get an explanation, by what means I did not care. Suddenly, before
en|I knew, I found my steps stumbling down the slope at the farther end, and
en|the pale water alive with all the dimplings of the rain appearing at my very
en|feet. The steps sank upon the loch side, and ceased with a thrill like the
en|acutest sound in a silence more absolute than any I have heard in nature. I
en|stood gasping, with my foot touching the edge of the water; it was all I
en|could do to arrest myself there. I hurried back to the house in a state
en|of agitation which I cannot describe. It was partly nervous dread. I do not disguise
en|this; but partly it was a bewildered anxiety and eagerness to know what it was
en|that I might be able to do. That I had the most absolute faith in
en|it I need hardly say. One does not have, or think one has, such an
en|interview as this without believing what is told in it. There was no doubt in
en|my mind, save an anxious, excited wonder--how was it, how could it be? I searched
en|all my horizon for possibilities. Before I reached the house, I had forgotten all the
en|other incidents in my eagerness about this. My pursuit of her seemed nothing but natural,
en|and the sudden silence that had seemed to tingle and thrill about me went clean
en|out of my mind. “You may help if you will! if you will!” I said
en|it over and over to myself a thousand times with a feverish hurry and eagerness.
en|Indeed, I did nothing but repeat it. I could not eat, I could not rest.
en|When Charlotte came down late to tell me her father was asleep, that the doctor
en|who had been sent for had pronounced his recovery real, I was walking up and
en|down the half-lighted drawing-room repeating these wonderful words over and over to myself. “He says
en|it is wonderful, but it may be complete recovery,” Charlotte said; “only to tell him
en|nothing we can help, to keep all the circumstances from him; especially, if it is
en|possible, about Ellermore. But how is it possible? how can I do it? ‘Help if
en|you will?’ Mr. Temple, what are you saying?” “It is nothing,” I said; “some old
en|rhyme that has got possession of me.” She looked very anxiously into my face. “Something
en|else has happened? You have seen or heard”--Her mind was so alive to every tone
en|and glance that it was scarcely possible to conceal a thought from her. “I have
en|been in the Walk,” I said, “and, being excited and restless, it was more than
en|my nerves could bear.” She looked at me again wistfully. “You would not deceive me,
en|Mr. Temple,” she said; then returned to her original subject. The doctor was anxious, above
en|all things, that Mr. Campbell should leave Ellermore to-morrow; that he should go early, and,
en|above all, that he should not suspect the reason why. She had the same dread
en|of the removal as ever, but there was no alternative; and not even a day’s
en|delay was to be thought of, for every day, every hour, made the chances of
en|discovery more. “But you cannot keep up the delusion for ever,” I said, “and what
en|when it is found out?” Again she wrung her hands. “It is against my judgment;
en|but what can I do?” She paused a moment, and then said, with a melancholy
en|dignity, “It can but kill him, soon or syne. I would not myself have my
en|life saved by a lie; but I am weak where my father is concerned, and
en|God understands all. Oh, I am beginning to feel that so, Mr. Temple. We search
en|and search, and think what is best, and we make a hundred mistakes; but God
en|sees the why and the wherefore. Whoever misunderstands, He never misunderstands.” She went away from
en|me in the calm of this thought--the secret of all calm. It seemed to me
en|that I, in my blind anxiety, guessing at the enigma that had been given to
en|me, and my poor Lady vagrant from the skies, still trying to be the providence
en|of this house, were left alike behind. I could not rest all night. It was
en|all I could do to remain in my room after the diminished household had gone
en|to rest. It was a weird night, lighted up by that mystic light of the
en|waning moon, in which there seems to be always something that is baleful and prophesies
en|evil. One can fancy evil creatures about, ill dews falling. I sat at my window,
en|as often as I could persuade myself to keep still at all, with the damp
en|air saturating me, and the cold light enveloping everything below in a whiteness and blackness
en|of exaggerated contrast. I can say nothing less than that I expected to see the
en|anxious, wistful figure which I had seen so often, looking up at me, appealing to
en|me again. But nothing broke the blank of the white light; nothing but the shadow
en|of the bare trees, outlined in every twig above the darkness of the shrubberies below,
en|interrupted the shining of the moon. When I threw myself upon my bed, it was
en|only to think more acutely, more restlessly than before. What was it that I could
en|do? How could I help them? What power of mine could save? The dull morning
en|was a relief to me which I cannot describe, and to hear the first stirrings
en|of the household. Now at least there would be something which I could do--something not
en|lying vague and shadowy among the possibilities, but certain and feasible, which is of all
en|things the greatest relief to anxious thought. Charlotte came down to breakfast with me, which
en|she had not done before. She told me that her father had passed a good
en|night, that he had shed tears on awaking, and begun to talk tenderly and calmly
en|of Colin; and that everything seemed to promise that the softening and mournful pre-occupation of
en|grief, distracting his mind from other matters, would be an advantage to him. It was
en|pleasant to be left with Margaret, who had adored her nursling, and who had been
en|fully warned of the necessity of keeping silence as to the circumstances of his death.
en|The post-bag came in while we were talking. It lay on the table for a
en|few minutes untouched, for neither of us were anxious for our correspondence. We were alone
en|at table, and Charlotte had rested, though I had not, and was almost cheerful now
en|that the moment had arrived for the final severance. The necessity of doing inspirited her,
en|as it did me. And perhaps, though I scarcely dared to think so, this tranquil
en|table at which we sat alone, which might have been our table, in our home,
en|in a new life full of peace and sober happiness, soothed her. The suggestion it
en|conveyed made the blood dance in my veins. For the moment, it seemed as if
en|the hope I dared not even entertain, for one calm hour of blessedness and repose,
en|had come true. At last she gave me the key, and asked me to open
en|the bag. “I have been loth to disturb this peaceful moment,” she said, with a
en|smile which was full of sweetness and confidence, “and nothing outside seems of much consequence
en|just now; but the boys may have something to tell, and there will be your
en|letters. Will you open it, Mr. Temple?” I, too, was loth--more loth than she--to disturb
en|the calm; and the outside world was nothing to me, while I sat here with
en|her, and could fancy her my own. But I did what she told me. Letters
en|are like fate; they must be encountered, with all that is good and evil in
en|them. I gave her hers, and laid out some, probably as important to them, though
en|they seemed to me so trifling and unnecessary, that were for the maids. Then I
en|turned to my own share. I had two letters, one with a broad black border,
en|which had been forwarded from one place to another in search of me, and was
en|nearly ten days old--for, like most people, I examined the outside first; the other a
en|large, substantial blue letter, which meant business. I can remember now the indifference with which
en|I opened them, the mourning envelope first. There were so many postmarks on it, that
en|that of its origin, which would have enlightened me at once, never struck me at
en|all. Heaven above! what was this that met my eyes? An announcement, full of periphrasis,
en|of formal regrets, of the death of my old Cousin Jocelyn ten days before. I
en|gave a sort of fierce cry--I can hear it now--and tore open the second, the
en|official letter. Of course I knew what it was, of course I was aware that
en|nothing could interfere, and yet the opportuneness of the announcement was such that human nature,
en|accustomed to be balked, would not allow me to believe in the possibility. Then I
en|sprang from my seat. “I must go,” I cried; “there is not a moment to
en|lose. Stop all proceedings--do nothing about the going, for God’s sake, till I come back.”
en|“Mr. Temple, what has happened? Charley”--cried Charlotte, blanched with terror. She thought some other catastrophe
en|had occurred, some still more fatal news, that I could not tell her. But I
en|was too much absorbed in my own excitement to think of this. “Do nothing,” I
en|said; “I will meet Charley on the way, and tell him. All will be right,
en|all will be right; only wait till I come back.” I rushed to the door
en|in my haste, then came back again, not knowing what I did, and had caught
en|her in my arms before I knew--not in my arms, but with my hands on
en|her shoulders, holding her for one mad moment. I could hardly see her for the
en|water in my eyes. “Wait,” I said, “wait till I come back! Now I can
en|do what she said! Now my time is come; do nothing till I come back.”
en|I let my hands drop down to hers, and caught them and kissed them in
en|a wild tremor, beyond explanation. Then I rushed away. I have a recollection of meeting
en|the children and pushing aside their little outstretched hands and morning salutations. It was a
en|mile or more to the little quay where the morning boat carried communications back to
en|the world. I seemed to be there as on wings, and scarcely came to myself
en|till I descended into the noise, the haze, the roar of the damp streets, the
en|crowds and traffic of Glasgow. Next moment (for time flew, and I with it, so
en|that I took no note of its progress) I was in the clamour of the
en|“works,” making my way through the grime and mud of a great courtyard, with machinery
en|lying round me on every side, amid the big skeleton houses with their open windows,
en|into the office, where Charley, in close converse with a stranger, jumped up with terror
en|at the sight of me. “What has happened?” he cried, “my father?” I had scarcely
en|breath enough to say what I had to say. “Your father,” I cried, “has come
en|to himself. You can make no sale without him--every arrangement must be stopped at once.”
en|All that I was capable of knowing was, with a certainty, beyond all proof, that
en|the man with whom Charley was talking, a sportsman in every line of his countenance
en|and clothes, was the intending purchaser of Ellermore. I remember little of the conversation that
en|followed. It was stormy and excited; for neither would Charley be convinced, nor would the
en|other consent to be off his bargain. But I made my point clear. Mr. Campbell
en|having recovered his faculties, it was clear that no treaty could be concluded without his
en|consent. (It would not have been legal in any case, but I suppose they had
en|in some way got over this.) I remember Charley turning upon me with a passionate
en|remonstrance, when, almost by violence and pertinacity, I had driven his Cockney sportsman away. “I
en|cannot conceive what is your object, Temple,” he said. “Are you mad? My father must
en|give his consent; there is no possibility of a question about it. Ellermore must be
en|sold--and as well to him as to another,” he said, with a sigh. I took
en|out my blue letter, which I had huddled into my pocket, and laid it before
en|him. “It is to me that Ellermore must be sold,” I said. My inheritance had
en|come. There was nothing wonderful about it--it was my right; but never did inheritance come
en|at a more suitable moment. Charley went back with me that afternoon, after a hurried
en|conference with his young brothers, who came round me, shaking my arms nearly off, and
en|calling to each other in their soft young basses, like rolls of mild thunder, that,
en|whatever happened, I was a good fellow, a true friend. If they had not been
en|so bashful they would have embraced me, less, I verily believe, from the sense of
en|escape from a great misery which they had scarcely realised, than from generous pleasure in
en|what they thought a sort of noble generosity. That was their view of it. Charley
en|perhaps was more enlightened. He was very silent during the journey, but at one point
en|of it burst out suddenly upon me. “You are doing this for Chatty, Temple. If
en|you take her away, it will be as bad as losing Ellermore.” I shook my
en|head. Then, if never before, I felt the hopelessness of the position. “There is but
en|one thing you can do for me: say not a word of that to her,”
en|I said. And I believe he kept counsel. It was of her own accord that
en|Charlotte came up to me after the hurried interview in which Charley laid my proposal
en|before her. She was very grave, though the sweetness of her look drew the heart
en|out of my breast. She held out her hands to me, but her eyes took
en|all warm significance out of this gesture. “Mr. Temple,” she said, “you may think me
en|bold to say it, but we are friends that can say anything to one another.
en|If in your great generosity there may yet be a thought--a thought that a woman
en|might recompense what was done for her and hers”--Her beautiful countenance, beautiful in its love
en|and tenderness and noble dignity, but so pale, was suddenly suffused with colour. She took
en|her hands out of mine, and folded them together--“That is out of my power--that is
en|out of my power!” she said. “I like it better so,” I cried. God help
en|me! it was a lie, and so she knew. “I want no recompense. I will
en|be recompensed enough to know you are here.” And so it has remained ever since,
en|and may, perhaps, for ever--I cannot tell. We are dear friends. When anything happens in
en|the family I am sent for, and all is told to me. And so do
en|I with her. We know all each other’s secrets--those secrets which are not of fortune
en|or incident, but of the soul. Is there anything better in marriage than this? And
