en|yet there is a longing which is human for something more. That evening I went
en|back to the Lady’s Walk, with a sort of painful desire to tell her, the
en|other, that I had done her bidding, that she had been a true guardian of
en|her race to the last. I paced up and down through the dim hour when
en|the sun ought to have been setting, and later, long into the twilight. The rain
en|fell softly, pattering upon the dark glistening leaves of the evergreens, falling straight through the
en|bare branches. But no soft step of a living soul was on the well-worn track.
en|I called to her, but there was no answer, not even the answer of a
en|sigh. Had she gone back heart-sick to her home in heaven, acknowledging at last that
en|it was not hers to guard her race? It makes my heart ache for her
en|to think so, but yet it must have been a sweet grief and easily healed
en|in those blessed regions, to know that those she loved were most safe in God’s
en|only care when hers failed--as everything else must fail. THE SHIP’S DOCTOR The Gushat-house stood,
en|as its name denotes, at the angle where two roads met. These were pleasant country
en|roads both--one, shadowed by trees, here and there, threading through rich and broad fields, led
en|up into the wealthy inland country, the rich heart of Fife; the other, with scattered
en|cottages instead of the trees, growing after a while closer and closer together, was the
en|straight road to the “town,” and was open to the sea view and the sea
en|breezes. The town was the little town of Anstruther on the Fife coast; the sea
en|was the Firth of Forth, half ocean, half river; the time was fifty years ago.
en|In this locality, and at that distant period, happened the very brief and simple story
en|I have now to tell. In the Gushat-house lived Mrs. Sinclair and Nora, her daughter.
en|The house was, in its humble way, a kind of jointure-house, though it belonged to
en|no potent family or county magnate. It had been for generations--since it was built, indeed--the
en|refuge of one widow or other, who had sufficient interest in the place to remain
en|near it, or some connection with the soil. The present occupant had been the wife
en|of the minister, and was the daughter of one of the smaller proprietors in the
en|neighbourhood. She was a woman whom the county did not disdain to visit and honour;
en|but yet she was not rich nor a great lady in her own person. In
en|those days life was simpler, more aristocratic perhaps, but less luxurious, and far more homely.
en|Nowadays the coast towns in Fife are unendurable. In summer they are nothing but great
en|receptacles of herrings, not in their silvery state as they come in in glistening shoals
en|in the boats from sea, but in the hideous course of economical preservation and traffic.
en|Salt and smells, and busy women armed with knives, operating upon the once harmless “drave,”
en|line all the stony little streets, and send up to heaven an unsavoury testimony. You
en|breathe herrings, if you are so unwary as to trust yourself in the season on
en|that too prolific coast. But it was not so fifty years ago. Then the herrings
en|came in to be eaten, not to be salted down in barrels, and they had
en|not got the upper hand of everything. There was no lucrative trade going on, no
en|salt and pungent harvest-time of the sea; but the homely wynds were passable, even in
en|summer, though cleanliness was far from perfect. In place of the herrings there was the
en|whale fishery, which sent out its ships periodically, and brought back with corresponding regularity the
en|sailor fishermen to their families when the expedition of the year was over. It was
en|a trade more picturesque, more dangerous, and less disagreeable, at least to the bystander. Nobody
en|could refuse to be interested in the solemn ships going forth to their struggle with
en|the ice, and the storms, and the monsters of the sea; nor in their exciting
en|return, when the well-known rig would heave slowly in sight on the broad Firth, under
en|eager telescopes, which reported the signs she carried, the jubilant garland on the mast, sign
en|of a successful fishing, or the melancholy flag half-mast high, which thrilled the whole town
en|with alarm, no one knowing whose son or husband, or what family’s father it might
en|be. An interest almost more exciting, and certainly more frequent, would thrill through the little
en|salt-water place when a gale came on suddenly at some time when “our boats” were
en|at sea. So that the “town” was not without its points of human interest, before
en|the herring barrels, and hideous trade consequent thereupon, had appeared in the stony little streets.
en|And to Nora Sinclair it was a very interesting place. She was fond of the
en|fisher-folk, whom she had known all her life, and who, for their part, were fond
en|of her. She and her mother were local princesses, as it were, in the parish;
en|for the reigning minister was unmarried and unsympathetic. In those days, before the advent of
en|King Herring, even the position of the minister was different. There was no dissent in
en|the place, except the little Episcopal church, “English chapel,” as it was called, to which
en|some of the adjacent gentry came, and which everybody regarded with half indulgent, half contemptuous
en|tolerance. It was tacitly admitted as a kind of necessity that the fine people should
en|frequent this little conventicle. The common people granted them the indulgence with a half smile
en|at their weakness of caste and training, but occupied the parish church themselves in close
en|masses, filling the pews with characteristic rugged faces, and the air with a faint breath
en|of fish and tar and salt water--the inalienable odour of a seafaring population. Nora Sinclair
en|was in most things a young woman of refined tastes; but she had never had
en|her eyes or her senses opened to these little imperfections. She took all the interest
en|of a daughter of the place in its vicissitudes, and knew the boats and their
en|crews, and was as anxious when it blew a gale as if she herself had
en|known what it was to venture her heart on the dangerous chances of the sea.
en|Her mother and she lived a not uncheerful life in the Gushat-house, metaphorically placed, as
en|it was, with one eye on the country and one on the sea. The “families”
en|about were many of them “connections” of Mrs. Sinclair, who was, as has been said,
en|of a very good stock--old Auchintorlie’s daughter; and those who were not connections were old
en|friends. The mother and daughter were not left alone when they had to change to
en|the wistful widow’s refuge from the manse. Kind friends and cheerful company surrounded them. In
en|the depth of winter, when the Firth was often black with storms, and the weather
en|too gloomy for enjoyment, the two ladies would go “across” in the ferry-boat from Kinghorn
en|to Edinburgh, not without some trembling for the dangers of the passage, and settle themselves
en|there for a few months, during which time Nora would have her gaieties, and be
en|taken to a few balls, and take her share in the pleasures of her youth.
en|Altogether it was a very endurable life. It was in Edinburgh she first met with
en|Willy Erskine, though he was a neighbour at home. He was one of the Erskines
en|of Drumthwacket, of as good a family as any in Fife. One of Mrs. Sinclair’s
en|perplexities was to make out in what way the Erskines and the Auchintorlie family were
en|connected, but she never succeeded in clearing it up. That there was some connection she
en|was sure, and Willy was very welcome when he paid those frequent visits in Heriot
en|Row, where they were living, and sat so long that Nora grew tired of him,
en|though he was a handsome young fellow. “Poor callant! so far away from home, what
en|would he do but come and see me, that am his mother’s near connection?” Mrs.
en|Sinclair would say. And if she could have been angry with her Nora, it would
en|have been for this cause. “Not so very near, mamma,” Nora would answer. “And if
en|all our connections were to come as often”-- “They all show a very proper feeling,
en|my dear,” was her mother’s reply; and nothing could be more true. Cousins to the
en|fifth degree always turned up to take care of Nora at her balls--to dance with
en|her when there--to cheer her mother’s solitude when she was gone, according to their several
en|ages and sexes. The Sinclairs were a very “well-connected” family, and it was a circumstance
en|which added much to the comfort of their life. As for Willy Erskine, he was
en|a very nice young fellow, everybody allowed. He was not rich, to be sure. The
en|Drumthwacket household was known not to be a rich one, and he was the third
en|son. But he was doing what it was the proper thing for a third son
en|to do. It had not been his vocation to go to India, like his second
en|and fourth brothers, though, no doubt, that would have been the best way; and New
en|Zealand and Australia had not been discovered, so to speak, in those days. His eldest
en|brother was at the Bar, and Johnny, the fifth, was to be the clergyman of
en|the family; so that Willy’s lot was clear before him, even had he not been
en|impelled towards it by a naturally scientific turn of mind. He was pursuing his medical
en|studies at Edinburgh University during those years when Nora and her mother came in the
en|winter to Heriot Row. In summer it was quite a practicable thing to walk from
en|Drumthwacket, which was only sixteen miles off, down to Anstruther on one pretence or other--an
en|expedition which made it quite natural as well as necessary to “look in” at the
en|Gushat-house, somewhere near the time of the early dinner. The fare on Mrs. Erskine’s table
en|was homely, but it never occurred to her to grumble at the frequent visitor, or
en|put on company punctilios, or even a fresh tablecloth, for Willy. The latter was a
en|point upon which the population of the Gushat-house were always very easy in their minds;
en|for no lady in Fife had a better stock of “napery,” and none were more
en|delicately, femininely alive to the beauties of clean linen. Besides which, everybody in those days
en|washed at home, and clean tablecloths cost nothing--a matter of primitive luxury unknown in our
en|days. Young Erskine would look in, and nobody was otherwise than pleased to see him;
en|other people, too, “looked in” on other days. Sometimes there would be two or three
en|strangers, equally unexpected and welcome at the widow’s table. There was glorious fish, fresh from
en|the sea--cod, with great milk-white flakes, and the delicious haddocks of the Firth, which cost
en|next to nothing, to take the edge off the wholesome appetites of these young people;
en|and savoury old Scotch dishes, such as exist no more--Scotch collops, brown and fragrant; chickens,
en|which were not called chickens, but “hens”; dainty curries, in which the homely, rural gentry,
en|with sons and brothers by the score in India, were as great critics as the
en|old Indians themselves. To the board thus spread the country neighbours were always kindly welcome;
en|and Mrs. Sinclair took no special notice of the frequency with which young Erskine made
en|his appearance. If Nora was more observant, she was also more tolerant than she had
en|been in Edinburgh. She did not even seem to dislike it much when chance brought
en|her in contact with the young student among the rocks, as sometimes happened. Though that
en|age was not so advanced as our own, it was still possible, even at so
en|rudimentary an epoch, to make good use of the sea-coast, and the marine creatures which
en|the young man was studying, to further such encounters. He called them by their Latin
en|names when he walked with Nora up to the Gushat-house, and Mrs. Sinclair respected his
en|habits of research. “It’s little good he’ll get out of the tangle on the rocks,”
en|she would say, “but it shows a diligent mind.” At which praise Willy would blush
en|and Nora smile. But there was no haste, no rush upon the inevitable, no rash
en|effort to put it to the touch, to win or lose it all. He would
en|have lost his love altogether had he been precipitate. Nora was the only child of
en|her mother, who was a widow. She had tender love to guard her, and full
en|freedom to do as she pleased. She was the favourite of all the fisher-folk, the
en|beauty of the town, admired, imitated, caressed, and followed wherever she went. The Gushat-house was
en|the cheeriest little house in all the countryside, and Mrs. Sinclair was the most indulgent
en|mother: naturally, therefore, Nora had no wish, not the most distant inclination, to sacrifice all
en|this to become any man’s wife. Love lays hold upon some people with a violent
en|hand, but with others has to go softly, and eschew all turbulence. Nora began to
en|like young Erskine’s society. She began to feel a certain lightness diffuse itself over her
en|heart when she saw him coming down the long country road, crossing the shadow of
en|the trees. When winter came, and these same trees were bare, and the journey to
en|Heriot Row drew near, it was a pleasure to her to remember that Erskine was
en|already there. Not that she went so far as to form a good resolution to
en|be kinder to him, to permit his attendance more willingly. She was only pleased to
en|think that he would be at hand to be snubbed or encouraged as the humour
en|might seize her--a very improper spirit, as the youthful reader will perceive. But Nora was
en|far from being a perfect young woman. Thus things went on in a leisurely way.
en|There was no hurry; even Willy himself, though he was deeply in earnest, was aware
en|that there was no hurry. If any competitor should appear, ready to carry her off
en|suddenly, then Willy Erskine would wake up too, and fly, violent and desperate, to the
en|assault. But no such catastrophe was threatening. Nora, everybody said, was “fancy free.” Even her
en|saucy sallies, her little caprices, proved this. Her lovers were her friends, in a quaint,
en|rural sort of way. She did not wish to cast any of them from the
en|latter eminence by regarding them in the former capacity. She might go on wandering through
en|the metaphorical forest for years, some people said, and take the crooked stick at the
en|end. Whether he was the crooked stick or not, Willy Erskine, like a wise general,
en|kept a wary eye on her tactics, and held himself ready to take advantage of
en|any weakening in her defences. It had begun years ago, when they were boy and
en|girl; it might last till they were middle-aged, for anything that could be said to
en|the contrary. He was always at Nora’s disposal, to do anything she chose to ask
en|him; and she was always friendly to Willy, ready to stand up for him when
en|he was absent, and to give him the most solemn good advice when he permitted
en|her the opportunity. Nora might have been his grand-mother, to judge by the prudent counsel
en|she gave him, and would try his devotion the next moment by laying upon him
en|the most frivolous and troublesome commissions. Thus the time went on imperceptibly, marking its progress
en|on these two at least by no remarkable events. Nora was bridesmaid so often to
en|her youthful friends that she began to declare loudly that she had forestalled her own
en|luck, and would never be a bride--but without any sort of faith in her own
en|prediction. Yet, though this state of things was a very pleasant one, it was a
en|necessity that, one time or other, it should come to an end. The end was
en|brought about, as it happened, by another event of great importance to young Erskine, and
en|in which Nora and her mother, as in duty bound, took a lively interest. Willy’s
en|professional studies came to a conclusion, and the ladies went, well pleased, to witness the
en|curious ceremonial at which he was “capped,” as it is called--the outward sign and token
en|of his having attained the dignity of M.D. He had passed his examinations with credit,
en|and his friends were proud. At night there was a little party of Fife folk
en|at Heriot Row. The good people went to tea and supper, and made one substantial
en|but light, and one still more substantial and very heavy, meal. Then the health of
en|the young doctor was drunk with kindly enthusiasm. “Willy, take you my advice and get
en|a wife next,” said one of the genial guests, and the suggestion was received with
en|general applause. “A doctor without a wife is like rigging without a ship,” said another
en|adviser. “There’s two professions that must aye have the ballast of a petticoat. As for
en|a soldier, like your brother Sandy, he’s better without one, if he could be brought
en|to think it; and John will be the laird, and he can take his time.
en|But a minister and a doctor have no choice. You’ll ask us to your wedding
en|next, if you’ll be guided by me.” “What Captain Maitland says is very true,” said
en|Mrs. Sinclair; “a doctor’s never well received in families till he’s a married man. You’re
en|but young, and there’s no hurry, except for that. When I was a young woman
en|myself, and needing doctors, not even a family connection would have led me to call
en|in a man that was without a wife.” “Here’s a man that has no mind
en|to be without a wife,” cried Willy. Perhaps he was a little excited with drinking
en|his own health, or someone else’s. “I wish it only depended on me”-- “You can
en|but try,” said one, patting him on the shoulder. “Faint heart never won fair lady,”
en|said another. “I would not wonder if it was all settled a year ago!” said
en|a third; and various looks, some veiled, some openly significant, were turned upon the corner
en|where, amid a little knot of girls, Nora sat apart. It was no revelation to
en|Nora; but the thought of being thus openly indicated set her pride up in arms.
en|She to marry Willy Erskine for any reason whatsoever except her sovereign grace and pleasure!
en|She to take him because he was a doctor and wanted a wife! She had
en|to dance the first reel with him, when the room was cleared after supper, and
en|Mrs. Sinclair went to the piano--partly because he was the hero of the occasion and
en|she the daughter of the house, partly because they were such old friends; but she
en|would scarcely grant the young fellow a look even when her hand was in his
en|in the pretty, animated dance. And Willy, in his excitement, held that soft hand longer
en|and clasped it closer than was at all needful. Nora’s girlish temper blazed up; but
en|he could not see it, the foolish boy. His own heat and ardour, long suppressed,
en|the pleasant intoxication of all those friendly plaudits and flattering good wishes, the seduction of
en|the moment, when all were gone but himself, and the careful mistress of the house
en|had begun to put away the remnants of the feast and lock up her “garde-vin,”
en|were too much for him. Willy was so far left to himself as to arrest
en|Nora in the hall when she had said good-night to the last guest. He was
en|by way of leaving himself, when he stopped her and took her hand. “Say a
en|kind word to me, Nora,” he cried, drawing her into the dimly-lighted little room behind,
en|which was called the library. Mrs. Sinclair was in the dining-room close by, with her
en|confidential handmaiden, putting away the things. They could hear her voice where they stood, and
en|there was no harm in this little chance interview. “Say a kind word to me,
en|Nora,” he pleaded; “you know how fond I am of you. I’ve never thought of
en|another since I was a boy at school. I’ve looked forward to this for years
en|and years.” “What have you looked forward to, Mr. Erskine?” said Nora, with the insolence
en|of power. “Nora--Nora, don’t speak like that!” cried the young man. “I’m not worth it,
en|but you must take me--you know you must take me; you’re all the world to
en|me. What do I care for my degree or anything else but for you? Say
en|you’ll take a poor fellow, Nora! You know you are all the world to me.”
en|“Indeed, I know nothing of the kind,” said Nora. “I am very sleepy, and I
en|don’t care much about your degree. Must take you, indeed! I never do anything that
en|I must do. What with their toasts, and their talk, and their nonsense, they’ve turned
en|your head. Good-night.” And she went away from him, while he stood and looked after
en|her stupefied. “Nora!” he said, in a voice of such pain that Mrs. Sinclair heard,
en|and left the “things” on the table. She came in while Nora stood still, haughty
en|and offended, at the door. The mother saw at once what was the matter. She
en|thought it was a lover’s quarrel, and she saw there had been enough of it
en|for the night. “I thought you had gone with the Lindsays, Willy,” she said, looking
en|at him in her motherly way; “and you must be wearied and fit for your
en|bed. What’s Nora making her little _moue_ at now? But never mind her, my man;
en|to-morrow’s a new day.” “Yes, to-morrow’s a new day,” cried Willy. “I’ll take no thought
en|of what I’ve heard to-night. To-morrow I’m coming back.” And with that he rushed away.
en|As for Nora, she flew upstairs, and went to bed, that she might not come
en|in for that little sermon which was on her mother’s lips. When she had shut
en|herself into her own room she had a good cry. She could not have told
en|anyone the reason of her perversity. She was angry with herself and Willy, and the
en|guests who had put such nonsense in his head, and all the world. _Must_ take
en|him! very likely! If she, Nora Sinclair, ever had anything to say to a man
en|who came to her with such a plea--She paused, on the verge of a petulant
en|vow. Perhaps, on the whole, it would be as well not to make any oaths
en|on the subject. And, luckily, at that moment she fell asleep, which was the easiest
en|way out of the dilemma. To-morrow would be, as Mrs. Sinclair said, a new day.
en|But, unfortunately, to-morrow is not always a new day. When Nora got up in the
en|chilly spring morning, she was, on the whole, rather more irritated and petulant than she
en|had been the evening before. As for Mrs. Sinclair, it was her fixed opinion that
en|the young folk should be left to themselves to make up their little matters. “They
en|know each other’s ways best,” she said; “older folk do more harm than good when
en|they interfere.” So when Willy came in, pale and breathless, the kind woman withdrew herself
en|that the two might get it over undisturbed. It was not a new day for
en|young Erskine any more than it was for Nora. It was a feverish supplement to
en|last night. He had not perhaps gone to bed calmly after all his excitement, as
en|a girl has to do. There was a rere-supper somewhere, to which his friends had
en|dragged him, and where, probably, Willy’s brain had been heated by strong drinks. The morning
en|found him parched with mental impatience and suspense, as well as with a certain degree
en|of bodily feverishness and misery. It seemed to his heated eyes as if Nora meant
en|to jilt him after all his devotion. He swore a big oath to himself as
en|he rushed along to Heriot Row. “If she’ll not take me now, after all,” said
en|Willy, “by----! I’ll go off to sea, and I’ll never be heard of more.” In
en|this mutual mood the two met. It was not an amiable interview on either side.
en|The young lover took up precisely the line of argument which was most prejudicial to
en|him. He pleaded his faithful services--his devotion which had lasted for years. He established a
en|claim upon Nora, which she was not the girl to put up with. And she,
en|on her side, scornfully denied any claim he had upon her. “If that is what
en|you call love,” said the indignant maiden, “to follow a girl about, whether she likes
en|or not, and then to tell her she _must_ take you, to pay you for
en|it!” This, alas, was not the way of settling their affairs! “Nora,” cried the young
en|man, desperate, “this is the moment that’s to settle my life. It’s little matter for
en|you, but for me it’s life or death. I’m not asking you to take me
en|now--say a year, say even two years, I’ll be content; but I have to know.
en|Nora, bide a moment. If you turn me away without any hope, by----! there’s the
en|_Pretty Peggy_ sails from Anster on Saturday; I’ll go to Greenland in her, and never
en|see you more.” “And why should I want to see you more?” said Nora. “What
en|do I care for your _Pretty Peggy_? It will do you a great deal of
en|good, Mr. Erskine. It will teach you that you can’t have everything your own way.”
en|“Is this your last word, Nora?” cried the poor fellow, with glistening eyes. If she
en|had looked him in the face, Nora’s heart would have given way. But she felt
en|her weakness, and would not look him in the face. She stood by the table,
en|turning over and over in her hand an Indian toy of carved ivory, with her
en|eyes fixed upon it, as if it was the intricacies of the pattern that involved
en|life and death; and then she said slowly, while the blood seemed to ebb away
en|from her heart, “I have nothing more to say.” In another moment the door shut
en|violently, and Willy Erskine was gone. The sound went through the house like a thunderclap,
en|and threw down, with its violent concussion, the castle of cards in which Nora had
en|been entrenching herself. She sank down upon a chair, stupefied, and listened to the step
en|that went echoing along the street. Was he gone? Was he really gone, and for
en|ever? Gone to Greenland in the _Pretty Peggy_, into the ice where men and ships
en|perished, into the whaling boats where they sank and were lost for ever? Should she
en|never see him more? “You’ve made the bed, and you must lie on it,” said
en|Mrs. Sinclair, when she heard all, with an indignation that was soon lost in sympathy.
en|But Nora would not give way either to the sympathy or the indignation. She declared
en|steadily that she would do the same over again if it was in her power.
en|“What right had he to come making claims, and speaking of his rights to me?”
en|she said. “If a lad follows a girl, does that give him a right to
en|her--whether or no?” This was said with burning eyes, into which tears refused to come.
en|But yet Nora shed tears enough over it. She took immense pains privately to find
en|out when the _Pretty Peggy_ sailed, and to know if she had shipped a doctor
en|before she left Anster pier. Not for her life would she have asked the doctor’s
en|name, but she satisfied herself so far. And when the fact could no longer be
en|doubted, her heart grew so sick that she could not go home. The Sinclairs had
en|friends “in England”--a vague sort of expression used by the untravelled Scotch then, as untravelled
en|islanders nowadays talk of “the Continent.” Nora persuaded her mother that it would be pleasant
en|to “go south,” and pay the long-promised visit. She was glad to go away, glad
en|to be anywhere out of the range of those people and places with which Willy
en|Erskine’s name was so closely connected. But the other day it seemed he had been
en|so jubilant, so full of good prospects and high hopes. Now he was out upon
en|the Northern seas, surgeon in a whaling ship, like any poor student or broken man.
en|And he Drumthwacket’s son! and whose fault was it all? Nora was ashamed to confront
en|even the familiar rocks that knew him so well--that knew how she had met him
en|(by accident), and strayed with him along the sea-verge, with the salt spray now and
en|then dashed into their fresh faces, and the surge rising to their feet. She dragged
en|her home-loving mother about from one “connection” to another all the summer through, enjoying the
en|visits but little, poor child. As for Mrs. Sinclair, a British matron of the present
en|day would not be more disconsolate, nor feel herself more alien in the heart of
en|French society, than was this Scottish gentlewoman among her southern connections. Their ways, their accent,
en|their mode of living, were all discordant to her. “If I were to live all
en|my life among those English,” she said, “I think I would rather die.” Her soul
en|longed for the tents of Jacob and the dwellings of Jerusalem. “But if I were
en|not to humour my own bairn,” added Mrs. Sinclair, with pathos, “who should humour her?”
en|Nora was her only child; somehow or other she had make a mistake in her
en|young life. Clouds had come up over the sun at the moment when that sun
en|should have been brightest. Her mother could have given her the best of good advice,
en|but she chose to give her something better instead--she “humoured” Nora. She was her tender
en|partisan, right or wrong. She took up her cause and supported her silently against her
en|own reproaches and all the world. And that is the best way of healing the
en|wounded, if their friends but knew. It was the end of summer before they returned
en|to the Gushat-house. And then, whether it was that they were unexpected, or whether from
en|her misdeeds towards Willy Erskine, as Nora thought, few people came to see them at
en|first, and nobody so much as mentioned the Drumthwacket family. The name of Erskine was
en|never, as Nora thought, named before her; and she felt herself more guilty still as
en|she seemed thus to read her own condemnation in the eyes of others. But now
en|the turn of the season had arrived; when she cast wistful looks from the corner
en|of the garden up the long country road, going “north,” as those geographical, seafaring populations
en|described it, a leaf would now and then flicker down through the sunny air, a
en|sign that autumn had come. A few weeks more, and the _Pretty Peggy_ might flutter
en|up the Firth with all her sails set, like a fine lady coming into a
en|ballroom, as the sailors delighted to say; and if Nora, penitent, with softness in her
en|eyes, were by, could anyone doubt that the eager face of the ship’s doctor would
en|expand too, and that the evil days would come to an end? No one could
en|have doubted it but Nora. It was as certain that it would all be made
en|up as that the _Pretty Peggy_ would come safe out of the icy seas. To
en|be sure, ships were lost there sometimes, sometimes detained among the ice. But look what
en|a season it has been! Even the men’s wives were easy in their minds, and
en|sung by their wheels, or mended the nets at their cottage doors, and looked over
en|the smooth Firth with contented hearts. A week or two more, and the seamen, with
en|their wages, and their curiosities, and their rejoicing, would have come home. There was not
en|a man’s wife in the _Pretty Peggy_ who was so anxious as Nora. But then
en|it was _her_ fault. It was she who had sent him to sea--he who was
en|no seaman, he whom a wealthier lot awaited. And perhaps he would look bitterly upon
en|the woman whose caprice had wrought him so much harm. This was the thought that
en|made her heart ache, and made the days so long to her. She used to
en|walk out to the pier to watch the sunset reflections, and listen in silence to
en|the prognostications of the fishers and seamen about. When they prophesied a gale, Nora’s heart
en|would beat wild with alarm; when they gave their word the storm was past, a
en|hush as of a consoled child would come over her. At last there came a
en|speck on the horizon, upon which all those ancient mariners fixed their telescopes. They exchanged
en|opinions about her rig, and her hull, and her manner of sailing, till Nora, standing
en|by, was half crazed with suspense. At last the news flew through the town, waking
en|up all the wynds and cottages. It was the _Pretty Peggy_ at last. It would
en|be vain to describe the excitement into which Nora, like many another woman, rose at
en|the news. The other women were the sailors’ wives, who had a right to be
en|moved. She had no such right. She had never spoken even to her mother of
en|the _Pretty Peggy_. She had been too proud at first to betray the smallest interest
en|in the movements of her lost love, and she did not even know whether Mrs.
en|Sinclair was aware that Willy was coming with the returning seamen out of the icy
en|seas. She had to invent a reason for her anxiety as the ship drew near
en|the port. “Willy Morrison is in her, mamma,” said Nora. “I’d like to go down
en|and see them come in. His mother will be so happy.” Willy Morrison’s mother had
en|been Nora’s nurse, and that was her excuse. “Well, well,” said Mrs. Sinclair, with an
en|impatience unusual to her. “I wanted you at home this afternoon; but Nancy will be
en|proud to see you have a warm heart to your foster-brother. Be home as soon
en|as you can. I would not be surprised if some friend was to look in
en|to tea.” Nora gave her mother a startled look, of which Mrs. Sinclair took no
en|notice. She looked as if she had her secret too; and most probably she knew
en|as well as her daughter did who was coming up the tranquil Firth in the
en|returning ship. Did her mother expect him too? Could it be possible, after all the
en|tragic hours that were past, that things should fall so calmly into the old routine,
en|and Willy Erskine, after his voyage, look in to tea? She did not know if
en|she walked on air or solid ground when she made her way down again to
en|the pier. If that were to be the end of it, of what use had
en|been all the agonies of those silent months? Life seemed to swim before her like
en|a dream and confused phantasmagoria, as she thought, but yet a subtle sense of happiness
en|was gathering at her heart. He was coming so soon; he was so near; and
en|all those ghosts would roll up their gloomy wings and disappear out of sight, when
en|Willy Erskine once more looked in at the Gushat-house. She went quickly down along the
en|half-deserted road to the pier where the women were all crowding. The _Pretty Peggy_ could
en|not reach the harbour yet for more than an hour; but still, to be so
en|much nearer her, to be ready to meet the men and hear that all was
en|well, five minutes earlier, was compensation enough for the wives. They made pleasant little speeches
en|to Nora as she came down among them. “Ah, Miss Nora, the day will come
en|when you’ll be lookin’ out for a man o’ your ain,” said one. “And I
en|hope with a’my heart it’ll be a good man and a pleasant day,” added another.
en|“But Miss Nora’s man will never be a seafarin’ man like ours, to make her
en|heart sair,” said a third. “Unless it was a grand captain of a frigate in
en|a’ his gold lace,” was the ambitious aspiration of Nancy Morrison. “Sure I am, I
en|didna bring up a winsome young lady for less than that.” She was a favourite,
en|and this was the pleasant chatter that passed, as she went among them, from lip
en|to lip. “I want to see Willy come in from his first voyage, nurse,” said
en|Nora. What a lying, wicked little speech it was!--and what a true one!--but before Nancy
en|had time to answer, one of the men on the outlook threw down his telescope
en|with a groan--rather the glass slid out of his hands. “Get out o’ my way,
en|women, wi’ your cacklin’,” he said, as he stumbled down. “Oh, Lord, and their mother
en|that canna stir a foot from her bed!” With this the old sailor turned his
en|back on the advancing ship, and sat down on the edge of the pier, and
en|hid his face in his hands. This action alarmed the entire community, for Peter Rodger
en|was well known to have two sons in the _Pretty Peggy_. Two or three of
en|the women crowded round him to ask what he meant, when another of the men
en|gave a sudden cry. “My God, the flag’s at the half-mast!” he exclaimed. A sudden
en|horror fell upon the group. It fell upon the town instinctively, in the twinkling of
en|an eye; the news flew by that strange electricity which is quicker than the telegraph.
en|It was a sunny afternoon, the Firth was like glass, the sky was blue--nothing but
en|the white clouds above and the soft, gliding sails below disturbed the glistening surface of
en|the sea. The ship, with its white sails, came softly on before a slight but
en|favourable breeze; but the faces of the little crowd grew pale in the sunshine, and
en|a shudder ran through them. There was a pause, and every heart stood still. “She’s
en|got the garland on the topmast; she’s made a good voyage,” said a younger sailor,
en|under his breath. “Oh, lad, how dare ye speak,” cried one of the women, “when
en|she’s bringing death maybe to your mother or to me?” The strain of the suspense
en|was terrible, as they stood and watched. Some of the poor wives fell on their
en|knees and prayed aloud--as if that would bring to life the dead man, probably long
en|ago committed to the safe-keeping of the sea; some sat down and began to rock
en|themselves, crying silently, as if their individual fate had been sealed. As for Nancy Morrison,
en|she stood rigid, with a face as pale as stone, and with big, dilated eyes
en|watched the ship that was bringing her life or death. Nora was shocked and disturbed,
en|as was natural. Her heart went forth in a certain passionate pity for the one,
en|whoever it was, upon whom the blow was about to fall; but she did not
en|feel the same overpowering anxiety as that which moved the others. She went softly to
en|her old nurse, and put her arm round the poor woman. “Oh, Nancy, take courage,”
en|she cried; “don’t think it’s him!” “Let me be! oh, let me be!” cried Nancy.
en|There was no one there in a condition to take comfort or give attention to
en|anything but one. And the ship came so slowly, as it seemed to everybody now.
en|The Firth lit up with all the glorious reflections of the sunset; the May rose
en|dark upon the blazing water, with the iron skeleton that held at night its fire
en|signal; the Bass lay like an uncouth shell against the dim outline of land on
en|the other side, and the long sun-rays slanted and fell tenderly across the water. Then
en|the horrible excitement of the watchers was roused into a sharper crisis still. A boat
en|darted forth from the shore with six stout oarsmen, to the slowly gliding ship. Could
en|it be a ship of death, like that one that the Ancient Mariner saw against
en|the sun? Could there have been pestilence on board? It came on gliding, as the
en|other vessel must have done when “the men all light, the seraph men,” brought her
en|near the port. These wild thoughts passed through Nora’s mind alone. There came into it
en|a curious vague wonder whether it might have been Providence, and not she, that sent
en|Willy Erskine into such a ship. She seemed to see him on the deck with
en|all, or almost all, the authority in his hands--the saviour of most of the disabled
en|crew; healer, ruler, hero. Such was the strange vision that glided before her eyes as
en|she too eagerly watched the boat. The thought of his supposed devotion made Nora unselfish
en|too. She ceased to tremble about their personal meeting. She kept eye and hand firm,
en|to be ready to give help and succour to her who might be smitten, whoever
en|she might be. When the boat came back, and got within hailing distance, the excitement
en|grew terrible. Some of the poor wives threw themselves among the rocks to get the
en|news a moment earlier. Peter Rodger stood on the highest ledge, with his broad hand
en|curved like a trumpet round his eager ear. Nora placed herself behind her nurse instinctively,
en|for she loved the woman. But the awful strain of all their ears and senses
en|made the first cry unintelligible to them. Twice the vague shout came over the waters
en|before it could be comprehended. Then it was caught up and echoed by a hundred
en|voices--“Only the doctor!” That was what they said. Only the doctor! There was a shout,
en|and then a cry, sharp with joy, from all those women. Joy! though it was
en|still death that was coming. They clasped each other’s hands; they wept aloud; they cried
en|out, in the relief of their deliverance. The whole community, every living creature about, began
en|to breathe, and babble, and sob forth thanksgiving. One figure alone fell forward against the
en|wall on which Nancy Morrison had been leaning. Nora was stupefied. It was like a
en|great rock falling suddenly down upon her out of the peaceful sky. She shrank, and
en|gave one wail and shudder, and then it came, crushing the heart and flesh. The
en|doctor! He had said true--she was never to see him more. “Miss Nora, cheer up,”
en|said Nancy, crying, and laughing, and shivering with joy. “Dinna take it so sair to
en|heart. It’s her nerves, my bonnie woman. But they’re a’ safe, noo, baith lads and
en|men. It’s but the doctor--do ye no’ hear what they say?” Then Nora rose up
en|desperate, and turned her stony face upon them. “Do you think there’s none to break
en|their hearts for him?” she cried, with a wild indignation. “Do you think there’s no
en|mother, no woman watching? Be silent, ye cruel women! How dare you tell me it’s
en|only _him_?” Then they all looked at her with pathetic faces, gathering round her where
en|she stood--she who did not know what she was saying. Impatiently she turned from their
en|looks. What could sympathy or anything do for her? What did it matter? “Let me
en|be!” she cried, as Nancy had cried. Let her alone! that was all she could
en|say. “Eh, Miss Nora, if we had kent the doctor was onything to you!” cried
en|one of the pitiful women. Nora turned round with a certain wild fierceness almost before
en|the words were said. “And who said he was anything to me?” she asked, with
en|a strange scorn of herself and them. He was nothing to her; she could not
en|even wear black for him, or let anybody know she mourned. She shook herself clear
en|of the pitying people, she could not tell how. Like a blind creature, seeing nothing,
en|with an instinct only to get home anyhow, she went straight forward, not knowing where
en|she placed her foot; and thus walked sightless, open-eyed, and miserable--into Willy Erskine’s arms. The
en|cry she uttered rang in the ears of all the watching population for years after.
en|They forgot the ship and the men who were so near at hand to gather
en|round this curious group. Nora fell forward into her lover’s arms like an inanimate thing.
en|One shock she had borne, and it had taken all her strength--the other she could
en|not bear. For the first time in her life she lost consciousness. The light had
en|gone out of her eyes before--now the very breath died on her lips. Mrs. Sinclair,
en|who had come down to the pier with him to find her child, could never
en|be sufficiently thankful that Willy was a doctor and knew precisely what to do. He
en|carried his love all the way along the pier, hampered by eager offers of help,
en|and still more anxious comments of sympathy, to Nancy Morrison’s cottage on the shore, his
en|heart full of remorse and exultation. Though he had long ago forgotten his threat about
en|the _Pretty Peggy_, still it was quite true that he had come, like a conspirator,
en|to surprise from Nora’s honest eyes, from her candid face, some revelation of her true
en|feelings. She had so revealed them now as that they never could be denied again;
en|and though it was not Willy’s fault, he was remorseful in his tenderness. He had
en|never set foot on the _Pretty Peggy_. He had forgotten so entirely even the use
en|he had made of her name, that he believed, like Mrs. Sinclair, that it was
en|kindness to her foster-brother which had taken Nora to the pier. Instead of an unprofitable
en|visit to the Greenland seas, he had been settling himself very advantageously in an inland
en|town, where his “connections” in the county were sure to be of use to him;
en|and after this interval, with the mother’s concurrence, had come, with sober determination not to
en|be discouraged, to know what Nora meant, and what his fate was to be. All
en|this Nora learnt afterwards by degrees, with wrath and happiness. The doctor who had died
en|was a dissipated old man, of a class too common in the Greenland ships. “I
en|kent weel that doited body could never be onything to Miss Nora,” cried Nancy Morrison,
en|drying her eyes. The mystery was cleared up in a fashion to all the admiring
en|and sympathetic population round when Willy Erskine appeared on the scene; and yet nobody knew
en|what it meant except Nora and he. She was very angry and she was very
en|happy, as we have said. But she had taken all power of resistance, had she
en|wished to resist, out of her own hands. And the story came to the usual
en|end of such stories, and there is nothing more to say. PRINTED BY MORRISON AND
